THE RED PAGE
Cleopatra
Her beauty might outface the jealous hours,
ame * ;0 * ove ar *d pain to a tender sleep,
And the strong nerve of hate to sloth and tears :
Make spring rebellious in the sides of frost,
JLnrust out lank winter with hot August growths.
Compel sweet blood into the husks of death,
And from strange beasts enforce harsh courtesy.”
T. H atm an, Fall of Antony, 1655.
Her mouth is fragrant as a vine,
A vine with birds in all its boughs;
Her pent and scarab for a sign
Between the beauty of her brows
And the amorous deep lids divine.
Her great curled hair makes luminous
Her cheeks, her lifted throat and chin.
Shall she not have the hearts of us
To shatter, and the loves therein
To shed between her fingers thus?
Small ruined broken strays of light,
Pearl after pearl she shreds them through
Her long sweet sleepy fingers, white
As any pearl’s heart veined with blue,
And soft as dew on a soft night.
A 3 if the very eyes of love
Shone through her shutting lids, and stole
The slow looks of a snake or dove ;
As if her lips absorbed the whole
Of love, her soul the soul thereof.
Lost, all the lordly pearls that were
Wrung from the sea’s heart, from the green
Coasts of the Indian gulf-river;
Lost, all the loves of the world—so keen
Towards this queen for love of her.
You see against her throat the small
Sharp glittering shadows of them shake;
And through her hair the imperial
Curled likeness of the river snake,
Whose bite shall make an end of all.
Through the scales sheathing him like wings,
Through hieroglyphs of gold and gem,
The strong sense of her beauty stings,
Like a keen pulse of love in them,
A running flame through all hia rings.
Under those low large lids of hers
She hath the histories of all time;
The fruit of foliage-stricken years;
The old seasons with their heavy chime
That leaves its rhyme in the world’s ears.
She sees the heart of death made bare,
The ravelled riddle of the skies,
The faces faded that were.fair,
The mouths made speechless that were wise,
The hollow eyes and dustjry hair;
The shape and shadow of mystic things,
Things that fate fashions or forbids;
The staff of time-forgotten kings
Whose name falls off the Pyramids,
Their coffin-lids and grave-clothings ;
Dank dregs, the scum of pool or clod,
God-spawn of lizard-footed clans,
And those dog-headed hulks that trod
Swart necks of the old Egyptians,
Raw draughts of man’s beginning God ;
The poised hawk, quivering ere he smote,
With plume-like gems on breast and back ;
The asps and water-worms afloat
Between the rush-flowera moist and slack ;
The cat’s warm black bright rising throat.
The purple days of drouth expand
Like a scroll opened out again ;
The molten heaven drier than sand,
The hot red heaven without rain,
Sheds iron pain on the empty land.
All Egypb aches in the sun’s sight;
The lips of men are harsh for drouth,
The fierce air leaves their cheeks burnt white,
Charred by the bitter blowing south,
Whose dusty mouth is sharp to bite.
All this she dreams of, and her eyes
Are wrought after the sense hereof;
There is no heart in her for sighs;
The face of her is more than love—
A name above the Ptolemies.
Her great grave beauty covers her
As that sleek spoil beneath her feet
Clothed once the anointed soothsayer;
The hallowing is gone forth from it
Now, made unmeet for priests to wear.
She treads on gods and god-like things,
On fate and fear and life and death,
On hate that cleaves and love that clings,
All that is brought forth of man’s breath
And perisheth with what it brings.
She holds her future close, her lips
Hold fast the face of things to be;
Actium, and sound of war that dips
Down the blown valleys of the sea,
Far sails that flee, and storms of ships j
The laughing red sweet mouth of wine
At ending of life’s festival;
That spice of cerecloths, and the fine
White bitter dust funereal
Sprinkled on all things for a sign ;
His face, who was and was not he,
In whom, alive, her life abode;
The end, when she gained heart to see
Those ways of death wherein she trod,
Goddess by god, with Antony.
Algernon Swinburne
[From the Comhill Magazine, 1866—written to illus-
trate a drawing by F. Sandys in which Cleopatra is
represented as treading on a consecrated vestment.
Swinburne wrote of it: “My impression is that the best
thing about the poem is the motto—from an imaginary
‘Fall of Antony.’ This was really a chipping from the
first (undergraduate) sketch of ‘ Chastelard
Prof. Hugo Miinsterberg’s Psychology
and Grime (per G. Robertson and
Co.), makes a strong claim for the
Psychology
and Crime
introduction of experimental
psychology into the Law
Courts. In a popular style,
free from technicalities, he explains that,
assuming honest intention, the value of
evidence is relative to the capacity of the
witness to observe correctly, and he gives
the results of numerous experiments which
proved that very few of the persons tested
under the most favorable circumstances,
were able to give a correct account of
what they saw. These experiments, ex-
tending over a fairly long period of time,
bring forcibly before the reader the
illusions to which the senses of the nor
mal individual are subject, and the defects
of the ordinary memory, and give weight
to the plea that just as a railway man-
ager subjects applicants for employment
to tests for color-blindness, so should the
law examine the mental color-blindness
of a witness’s memory. It may be noted
that experiments with school children
seem to show that girls have a better
memory than boys as far as omissions
are concerned—they forget less; but
they have a worse memory than boys as
far as correctness is concerned—they un-
intentionally falsify more.
The subtle influence of suggestion in
the production of crime and in the dis-
tortion of evidence is admirably treated,
but the argument for the adoption of
psychological methods in the detection of
crime is not convincing. Every skilled
cross-examiner resorts to expedients based
on empirical psychology, and every judge
has his own way of testing the credibility
of witnesses. The late J udge Windeyer,
for instance, advocated the admission of
some of the evidences referred to in the
chapter on “Traces of the Emotions.”
The Professor concedes that Applied
Psychology has not yet sufficient data
upon which to establish safe and univer-
sally applicable tests for the examination
of accused persons. He shows, however,
the class of cases in which the psycho-
logist can render valuable assistance to
the present machinery of the law ; and,
more important still, he points to the
means which society should adopt to
assist in the prevention of crime.
Excluding the pathological cases—the
insane, hypnotised and other kinds of
diseased minds, he believes that no man
is born a criminal, and Lombroso only
strengthened the charge against society
that it has not done its duty—that it
really manufactures the hopeless criminal.
Crime is not a disease, but the result of
an impulse which is normally checked;
and it is the business of society to
strengthen all the influences which auto-
matically check the chance or persistent
impulses of variable human nature to
bump against the law. This little book
of Prof. Miihsterberg’s, which is the fore-
runner of a larger technical treatise,
makes interesting reading, and illustrates
the great strides already taken by modern
psychology, based on. physiology and
employing inductive methods, since its
humble beginning with Prof. Wundt a
little more than twenty years ago.
Some
Australians
The N.S.W. Bookstall Co. deserves praise
for giving Australian story-writers a chance
to gain an audience amongst
their own people. Three
more volumes are now added
to its list of popular fiction, “written, illus-
trated and printed in Australia.” Of these
The Barb of an Arrow , by Roy Bridges, is a
well-told story of convict days in Tasmania.
The author has digested his facts well, and
does not over-color his picture of the System
and its results. The story is not in the
same class as Marcus Clarke’s ; but then it
is not so harrowing, and the adventures are
mostly in the open air.
A. E. Jobson provides a readable imitation
of Sherlock Holmes and Martin Hewitt in
The Adventures of Bussell Howard. This
gentleman, who acts as “adjuster of other
people’s affairs,” is concerned with princes
and peers in Europe, and does not set foot
in Australia.
The New Ghum is a reprint of stories
which have appeared in various periodicals,
with a couple of new ones, by Arthur H.
Adams, the former editor of this page. His
successor, having examined it carefully,
hereby certifies that the work has been well
and truly done, and the public may safely
invest its bob.
The Souse of Broken Dreams , by Kathleen
Watson (per G. Robertson and Co.), is a
diary addressed to a son, recording the
unusual life of an Australian family in a
vein of sentiment which may appeal to
mothers. The villain of the piece is a
schoolmaster, with a curious form of lunacy
—a most unreal creation.
Ex-
Australians
Australia does not easily forget any of her
children who have left to try their luck with
the fickle Jade elsewhere, even though they
may never come back to her.
Some have done her honor,
and she is proud of their sue-
cess; some have tried but failed, and for
these she'has sympathy ; while others have
sold their birthright for a mess of pottage.
Carlton Dawe, a South Australian by
birth, began with Australian stories — Mount
Desolation, The Emu’s Head, and The Pil-
grims—crude but honest. For some time
past, however, he has catered for the British
public with romance and novel in more
familiar settings. The New Andromeda
(Bell), his latest effort, begins incredibly
with an English Perseus in a motor-car find-
ing his Andromeda tied naked to a tree.
He rescues her, of course, and eventually
meets the Monster who, unlike his prototype,
triumphs in the end. For those who care
for piffle it will do very well.
Mary Gaunt (Mrs. Lindsay Miller), a Vic-
torian, made her mark with short stories,
collected in The Moving Finger, and wrote two
or three pretty good novels before collabo-
rating with J. Bidgwell Essex in the pro-
duction of Fools Bush In and The Silent
Ones. The latter (from T. Werner Laurie)
presents an interesting study of an out-of-
the-way character—an Ashanti who had been
educated in Europe, won academic honors
and scientific distinction, but lost his own
people and gained no other. The Ethiopian
could not shed his skin ; though treated with
courtesy, he felt that he was never regarded
as an equal, and therefore he was isolated
and unhappy. But he met one German
scientist who drew no color line whatever,
and in return for his affection, Dr. Craven,
the Ashanti, set off for Africa to obtain a
sacred book of the Soudan, which would be of
immense value to the German professor. At
the outset of his adventure he rescues a girl
about to be sacrificed by fanatics of the tribe
to which he belonged, and the vengeance of
these Silent Ones adds a Rider Haggard
element to the story,
H. B. Marriott Watson, of Maoriland,
has never returned to that country in his
novels since he wrote The Web of the Spider.
Now, it seems, he has turned his back on the
Galloping Dicks and the Skirts of Happy
Chance with which he made a reputation for
“go” and humorous frivolity, and, under
the influence of H. G. Wells, has plunged
into the serious study of contemporary life.
The Flower of the Heart (per Sydney Book
Club) justifies the departure. It holds the
reader by its careful character drawing and
well-managed plot. The vicissitudes of the
adventurer in fipance, Eversley, the social
campaign of his wife, and her unhappy love
for a cad of the upper class make an in-
teresting story. Marriott Watson’s future
work in this line ought to be good.
My Service Days, by Major-General Sir
Norman Stewart (Bell), is a not very well
written life of a soldier who has seen service
in India, Afghanistan, Soudan, and China.
The record relating to China, being the
latest, is the fullest, occupying more than
half the book. It might have been cut
down with advantage, as many of the entries
are not more startling than these :
7th —Very bright weather. Therm. 5° at 8 a.m.
Am not feeling very fit.
26th —Nothing to note.
The author gives the N.S.W. and Victorian
contingents in China a very good character.
“Handymen, full of resource,” “splendid
specimens of manhood,” etc. He has seen
specimens of' the soldiers of various nations,
and concludes with this reflection :
The infantry of all armies seem to be sadly
deficient in good shooting “ when under fire,”
and it makes one wonder “if all musketry train-
ing in peace time is not so much money and time
thrown away.” The closer the quarters the worse
the shooting. Until our boys are taken in hand
during their school days I don’t believe we will
have a really good “shooting” army.
Those who care for reading about hunting
will find Stalks Abroad, by H. F. Wallace
(Longmasn, Green and Co.), a well-illustrated
account of the killing > of a great variety of
animals, including the deer of M.L., which
is now “one of the finest deer-stalking
countries of the world.” Wallace’s two
years’ hunting trip began at the Yellow-
stone Park, U.S. A., where he met the mighty
hunter, Buffalo Jones. The Last of the
Plainsmen, by Zane Grey (Hodder and
Stoughton), is a very lively and interesting
account of an expedition with Jones, in
which he displayed his quality as a stalker
and gave much valuable information about
animals and their ways. Jones has devoted
most of his life to the capture and taming of
wild animals, but never killed if he could
help it. Seeing that the buffalo, which once
roamed the plains in millions, was on the
verge of extinction, he smashed his rifle and
devoted himself to their preservation. To
his care is due the presence of the herds now
in Yellowstone Park. Anyone who as a
boy read with delight of the stirring adven-
tures of Daniel Boone, Kit Carson, Custer and
Buffalo Bill, will be glad to revive old
memories with this book.
A voluminous account of Hungary of To-
day (Bell) should be useful to anyone seeking
information about the country, its people and
institutions, but does not afford much for
this page. The country of Liszt and
Joacliim is prolific of musicians and singers,
and Hungarian folk songs are amongst the
finest in the world. The chapter on the
intellectual life of Hungary is full of names,
all, we think, save that of Maurice Jokai,
unknown to most Australian readers. Jokai’s
works are said to be a rich and faithful pic-
ture gallery of Hungarian life. He produced
over 250 volumes, so that Magyar must be
an easy language to write novels in, though
it does not look like it.
4
Re Oppenheim’s “ The Long Arm,-” Red Page
(l/4/’O9), Bernard Capes, if I remember rightly,
used the same title for a long article in Chambers’s
Journal last year. Any number of authors can
turn out a possibly readable novel, but when it
comes to naming their brain-child they are barren
of ideas, and oftentimes use or alter a title that
has done-duty before. A few examples that have
come under my notice : Amelia Barr wrote a
book which she called “ The Feet of Clay.” Edgar
Turner evolved “ The Girl with the Feet of Clay,”
H. A. Vachell named one of hi's “ The Face of
Clay,’’ H. C. Bailey turned out “The God of
Clay,” and James Blyth used “The Same
Oljay.” Again, Dumas wrote a “Pauline”;
W. E. Morris’s last is called “Pauline.”
Helen Mathers wrote a “Land o’ the Leal,”
David Lyall also wrote a “Land o’ the Leal.”
Rex Beach penned “ The Spoilers,” E. Pugh also
brought out “The Spoilers.” Morley Roberts
told about “The Way of a Man,” Emerson
Hough’s latest is also “The Way of a Man.”
Sydney McCall wrote about “The Breath of the
Gods,” Dmitri Merejkowski “The Death of the
Gods. ” A. E. W. Mason made “ The Broken
Road,”Armie Swan “The Broad Road,” John
Oxenham “The Long Road,” E. Godfrey “The
Winding Road,” and Major A. Griffiths “The
Wrong Road.” Rolf Boldrewood wrote “In Bad
Company,” Guy Boothby “In Strange Com-
pany.” G. B. Burgin told of “A Woman’s
Way,” L. T. Meade “The Way of a Woman.”
Silas Hocking created “The Silent Man,” and,
wonder of wonders, somebody else found “ The
Silent Woman.” But, for general utility, the
title beginning “The Mian Who—” takes the
pastry. Here are a few : “The Man Who Was
Dead,” by A. W. Mvrchmont; “The Man Who
Was Thursday,” by G. K. Chesterton ; “ The
Man Who Rose Again,” by Joseph Hi eking;
“The Man Who Won,” by Mrs. Baillie Reynolds ;
“The Man Who Knew Better,” by Tom Gallon ;
“The Man Who Forgot,” by J. Mackie. And
there are othersS
WANG.
WELSH LULLABY.
(After the Welsh of Robert Bryan.)
Showing the Cymric “Inlaid Rhyme.”
A S a blossom, sweet and rosy,
Folds its petals for the night,
In my bosom curling cosy,
Hush you, hush you, baby bright!
While I’m by thee, nothing cruel—
Not one harmful sound or sight—
Shall come nigh thee, O my jewel!—
O my armful of delight!
Little flowerets in the meadows,
Little nestlings in the trees
Now are sleeping in the shadows
To the cradling of the breeze ;
But the blossom of my bosom,
But the birdie on my knees,
While I lock him there and rock him,
Has a warmer nest than these.
Start not ! ’tis the ivy only
Tapping, tapping o’er and o’er.
Start not ! ’tis the billow lonely
Lapping, lapping on the shore.'
Through your dreaming you are beaming,
O so purely now, my store,
You must see your angel, surely,
Smiling through Heaven’s open door.
A. P. Graves (in The Bookman).

Vertical Spindle Moulding and Shaping Machine.
ħ
This Machine is for moulding or shaping straight, circular,
or irregular outlines, and is specially suitable for using cutters
in grooved holders, on square blocks, or with cutters through
the spindle.
The frame is a cored casting in one piece. The table is 32in. square,
and has planed grooves across it so that the fences, etc., can be fixed square
without loss of time in “setting” and in any position. The slide carrying
the spindle has planed seats the full length, and is adjusted vertically by
screw worked through bevel wheels and conveniently placed hand-wheel.
The bearings are phosphor bronze, extra long and heavy, and are turned on
the outside and fitted into bored seatings. J
The spindle and long pulley are made from one solid steel forging'
in order to get a true running balance. A loose Head, lin. diameter, is
fitted to the spindle, which will carry a square cutter-block, grooved collars,
or solid reversible cutters. The countershaft supplied with the machine is
arranged to work the spindle right or left, and convenient reversing gear is
provided.

An Overtaxed System
Extreme Nervousness Completely Cured.
An epoch-making achievement is here dealt
with by Mr. Michael Somers, of 47 Camden
Street, Newtown, who speaks of final success
being inevitable in similar cases if like measures
are adopted. His narrative takes the following
form: —
/“I was sitting, down writing a letter one day
in Brisbane, feeling as well as a man could wish to
be, when I suddenly was seized with a dreadful
sensation, and collapsed. Previous to this I had
been leading a very strenuous life, working at top
pressure, and, presumably, I had overdone it.
The strongest systems will break down when
taxed too much, as I have learned to my cost.”
“ What happened,” asked our representative,
“ when you were thus attacked? ”
“ The case looked to be such a serious one that
1 was taken to a hospital in an ambulance, and
there I received the very best attention they
could devote to me. Medicine was ordered, and
everything done they could think of. In all my
travels, and I have journeyed about a good deal,
I have never met a similar case to my own. It
certainly puzzled the doctors at the institution,,
for they could not make head nor tail of the cause
of my affliction.”
“Can you describe to me the feelings you had,
Mr. Somers ? ”
“Constantly recurring feelings of faintness were
as troublesome as anything—such horrible sensa-
tions as would make a man think he was going off
at any moment, and, besides, I was afflicted with
the most extreme nervousness it had ever been
my misfortune to come into contact with. When
everybody else was in bed, comfortably asleep,
was the time that my nerve troubles assumed the
greatest dimensions, and I would have given all I
possessed in the world to have been able to doze
off and get rid, for a while, of the harassing
thoughts that disturbed my mind. I could think
of nothing pleasant; every idea that came was
created in an atmosphere of adversity. I felt that
I was hemmed in by hardships and trials of all
kinds, and I think I was, too. Perhaps I was on
the point of falling asleep when, with-a little
convulsive jump, I d start up again. It was a
feeling as if my heart had momentarily stopped,
and there I’d be dreading the same thing
happening again, for fear of what might follow.
That’s the wretched way most nights went by, so
imagine my despondent, worn-out condition
during the day. Suffocating feelings badly affected
my cliest, and with difficulty I succeeded in
getting my breath. Meanwhile, the sudds'
jumping sensations in the heart’s region con-
tinued, accompanied with a terrible faintness, which
made me often think that my time had come, and
that I surely was going to die. Sometimes a
feeling came.as if I were actually dead in parts—
a horrible kind of numbness, it was—in the
stomach, chin, and back of the neck, and agiqn
and again I was seized with the terrible conviction
that I was going to drop dead. I was really
afraid to fall asleep in case one of these seizures
would come on, and I would never wake up again.
Oh! how awful it was. The extreme weakness
and constant twitching of my nerves could not be
checked by anything the doctors prescribed tor
me, and I had long since despaired of ever being
about in the world again, when I started, through
reading a testimonial in the. paper, taking the
medicine called Clements Tonic.”
And can you conscientiously state that
Clements Tonic made you as sound as you are
now ? ” queried the writer, looking at Mr. bomers
in amazement. . ...
“ I can, indeed ; though it tnay seem incredible ;
but my wife and son here can vouch for what i
say being the gospel truth. The symptoms I have
described did not leave me for a long, long time,
and then only through the wonderful agency or
Clements Tonic. That is the truth, as God is up
judge. It seems like a dream to look back upon
—the hospital, the doctors, the numerous medicines
that did not apply to me at all beneficially, the
fainting bouts, the feelings of paralysis in parts ot
my body, the unhappy frame of mind I was m,
the sleepless nights, the twitching of my ever}
nerve, the awful heart affections, and the intense
feebleness withal—yes, it seems like a hideous
dream that has passed and gone, for wduch 1 have
nothing in the world to thank but Clements
Tonic; so please publish the facts as you like.
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, MICHAEL SOMERS, of 47 Camden Street, Newtown,
in Che State of New South Wales, Common wealth ot
Australia, do solemnly and sincerely declare that 1
carefully read the annexed document, and that it eo
and is a true and faithful account of my illness ana
by Clements Tonic, and also contains my full perm -
to publish in any way my statements— which ig
voluntarily, without receiving- any payment; ana ‘
this solemn declaration conscientiously believing ti
to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of the
Act, 1900.-
Subscribed and declared at Newtown this
seventh day of February, one thousand nine hundrea a

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Boarder.
T\7" ANTED a young Lady Boarder in private family.
'' Apply, E., this office. —Queensland paper.
Wifey’s old, perhaps a scold.
Her face mayhap is wrinkling O !
Her tongue is tart, and hubby’s heart
With passion warm is crinkling O !
Praise the fair, do not despair,
All things come in order O !
A Lady young, with dulcet tongue,
Is wanted as a Boarder O !
Of beauty rare, with golden hair
(Peroxide bloom won’t wash and wear),
A Lady young and plump and fair,
Is wanted as a Boarder O !
Calmly wait, she’ll bolt the bait;
Hubby’s heart is brimming O !
Boarder talks and Boarder walks !
To the seaside they’ll go swimming O !
Bathing mixed, the programme's fixed—
Clothed from neck to knee, you know —
Wife at home, while in the foam
Hubby and the mermaid O !
Cupid’s dart has pierced his heart,
A captivating little “tart,”
Hubby, sure, will play his part,
If once he gets his Boarder O !
She must be fair, beyond compare,
And pink and plump, and passive O !
No prude who’s prim, nor maiden slim,
Nor lassie big and massive O !
A dainty girl will fill the part,
Providing she is willing O !
And lends her ear when no one’s near
To cooing and to billing O !
A dainty girl, with teeth of pearl,
And golden ringlets all a-curl—
Oh ! hubby’s brain goes whirly-whirl
Just thinking of the Boarder O !
Oh, Wives beware, for Boarders fair
Have households wrecked full many O !
They come and go, pass to and fro,
Of peace they leave not any O !
Rows and strife full oft are rife
As time runs on its courses 0 1
And boarder Jills, like boarder Bills,
End up in divorces O !
Frocks and flounces, frills and furs,
Flowers and flimsies; wife demurs,
Hubby woe and debt incurs
To please his dainty Boarder 0 !
QUEENSLAND.
AUSTIN DODD.
Celestial Avenue.
I wandered from the highways
One eve at set of sun,
Down dirty reeking byways
Where putrid gutters run.
1 strolled with footsteps lazy,
And thoughts deep-dro'wned in murk,
Through byways grim and hazy
That lead from Little Bourke,
And, as I tramped on aimless,
Strange things I seemed to see,
For there were dark lanes shameless
Sunk deep in mystery.
But one there was more solemn
Than any that I knew,
I read graved on a column —
“ Celestial Avenue.”
Weird figures silent, stolid,
In silence came and went;
Some of them young and solid,
And others old and bent.
And there were thin, pale faces
That peered from out the gloom,
Features that showed no traces
Of Springtide’s treasured bloom.
I gazed upon them spellbound,
Pale creatures of the night.
Shunned, banned, and ever Hell-bound,
Drooped phantoms of affright!
Out from the teeming silence,
The silence dark and dree,
A figure softly glided
And whispered, “Follow me.”
I followed, and she led me
Where vulgar stenches‘grew—
A building at the head of
Celestial Avenue;
Up creaking stairs, foul-smelling,
And in a half-lit room
(This gives no joy in telling),
I learned Australia’s doom.
There, as my eyes grew stronger,
I saw a sight most weird
(For I was blind no longer —
Oh God ! my heart was seared !)
Three men o’er maps were bending,
Maps of mine country, too ;
Of marvels there’s no ending —
One of the three 1 knew !
For one of them was Yellow,
And one of them was Brown ;
The Other—may Hell blast those
Who drag their country down !
Was White : it shames me ever
To,tell these things to you,
For in our fair Australia
Are traitors not a few !
Swiftly, before I knew it,
The vision changed again,
I saw grim times of trouble
And sacrifice and pain.
Over my humbled country
The Asian ensign flew —
Our destinies were governed from
Celestial Avenue !
VICTORIA.
Burnett Gray.

THURSDAY, MAY 6, 1909.
MORE FEDERAL CAPITAL SNAGS.
The lamentable fact developed last week that
N.S.W. State Premier Wade had found still
another difficulty in the road of his own favorite
Federal Capital site at Yassberra. It was an
obstruction which anybody save Wade might
have foreseen, but Wade apparently didn’t fore-
see it. He has only discovered it now. The
Federal territory as at present mapped out will
lie right across the Goulburn-Cooma railway, and
about 20 miles of the said railway will be in
Federal territory. That means, of course, that
the Commonwealth will acquire 20 miles of the
line and work it as a Federal undertaking, for it
can’t allow of a divided authority on its own
premises. One end of the Goulburn-Cooma rail-
way will belong to N.S.W. The other end will
belong to N.S.W. The middle will belong
to Australia generally. And as the Australian
Government will own a port at Jervis Bay,
and have a railway from its Yassberra city
to the bay, it will naturally seek to annex the
trade which comes up the Cooma line, at the
spot where it enters Federal territory, and divert
it to its own port instead of letting it go to Sydney
as heretofore. It will do this on plain, simple,
natural principles of competition. It must needs
try to make its port pay, and if, through the
machinations of the Sydney press and the Sydney
Foreign-trade politicians, it is driven to have its
Federal port only 87 miles from Sydney, it must
needs try to make it pay at Sydney’s expense,
that being the only opportunity it will have. All
the members of the present Federal Government
were willing to plant the Federal port nearly 300
miles from Sydney, where it couldn’t compete
with that city, but, being overruled, they have
decided to make the best of Jervis Bay—
and, as it is only possible to make the best
of it at Sydney’s expense, then. Sydney must,
take the consequences of its own folly.
There isn’t much trade as yet on the Goulburn-
Cooma rail way, but unless the State Government
deliberately adopts the policy of keeping the great
Monaro and Eastern Riverina country—about the
most fertile portion of N.S.W.—a wilderness in
order to injure the Federal authority and the
Federal port, the trade must increase; and the in-
creased trade must be intercepted by the Federal
outlet at Jervis Bay. And if the State Government
deliberately lays itself out to restrict the progress
and development of all the State between the
Federal port and the Victorian border, because its
trade will go to the Federal port at Jervis Bay, it
will be laying waste about a third of its own terri-
tory, and that sacrifice would be too tremendous
to be seriously imagined.
* * * *
Of course, the Goulburn-Cooma line is not the
only one involved. A Federal capital in the
neighborhood of Yass, with a port at Jervis Bay
and a railroad between the city and the seashore,
would be in a position to intercept and annex the
trade of the main line from Adelaide and Mel-
bourne, of the Riverina lines from Hay, Finlay, and
Corowa, the branches from Temora and Gundagai,
some of the South Coast trade, and a share of the
commerce which comes along the cross country
line between Bathurst and Murrumburrah. And
out of the necessity to make the Federal port a
profitable investment, the Australian Govern-
ment must needs reach out for all this
trade. It must aim, in fact, at making
Jervis Bay the commercial rival of Sy'dney—if
possible its successful rival. These facts, appar-
ently, didn’t dawn on the obtuse intelligence of
Gregory Wade until he noticed that, through the
unfortunate success of his own policy, the Federal
capital territory is mapped out right across the
Goulburn-Cooma line, so as to cut off the
Monaro trade from Sydney. And, in his anguish,
he wants the Federal territory shifted. This
makes the second shift he has demanded inside two
weeks. In the first place, he found that, because
of his frantic demands that the Federal territory
should be removed from an empty to a thickly-
peopled part of N.S.W., it had been so laid outas
to include the town of Queanbeyan, and he felt,
in his inmost legal soul, that he couldn’t part
with Queanbeyan. He wanted the capital close
up to Sydney and in a thickly-peopled part of
N.S.W., but at the same time lie wanted it in an
uninhabited section of the thickly-inhabited
country; and when he found that his own
arrangement was likely to take from N.S.W.
quite a large number of its inhabitants, and
deprive it of quite a large amount of its im-
portance, he was anxious that the capital site
should be dug up and put in a barrow, and
removed to new quarters. Next, he has dis-
covered that, by reason of his own folly
in demanding that the Federal city and
territory should be taken out of a region
where there are no N.S.W. railways to be
interfered with, and placed in a district where
N.S.W. railways are thick and numerous, the
Federal property will be rig’llt astride an im-
portant line, and will cut off its trade with Sydney;
and the harassed Greg. Wade wants the Federal
territory dug up and carted away again. Appar-
ently, unless the Federal capital is chased right
into the hills, Greg. Wade will never be fully
satisfied with his own foolish work.
In his craving to have the Federal territorj' in a
densely-peopled region where it won’t take up
any of N.S.W.’s population, and in a thickly-rail-
wayed locality where it won’t intercept the
trade of any of N.S.W.’s railways;, and
to have the Federal port right up against
Sydney, and yet where it won’t compete with
Sydney, Wade is a harassed bungler these
days. Ail sorts of devices are being pro-
posed in order to make the Federal territory a
serpentine bit of land which will crawl round and
round amid a maze of valuable State railways,
towns, reservoirs, etc., and yet not touch any of
them. But Time and Circumstance are against
Gregory Wade. No matter what arrangements
he may contrive to make now, future alterations
and future amendments of the Constitution are
certain to upset them and him. The Common-
wealth may begin with a cramped little territory,
situated inside the four corners of nowhere in par-
ticular, but future demands for a better water
supply, for more water power, and for more
breathing room will compel its expansion. If the
Federal city is planted at Yassberra, close by the
back door of Sydney, it must reach out sooner or
later to take from Sydney the trade of all N.S.W.’s
southern and south-western railways, and its port
at Jervis Bay must one day set up as Sydney’s
business rival. The capital of a whole continent
can’t be permanently sat upon and kept down by
a petty clique of State politicians, and no paper
or parchment agreement that can be devised now
in order to prevent its expansion will hold good
for any length of time. The other States are all
partners in the capital, and it is absurd to sup-
pose that they will be content for all time with a
mean, unprogressive, uncommercial metropolis,
simply because Sydney doesn’t want a great rival
city and port at its door—this after insisting that
the Federal city and port must beat itsdoor. There-
fore Gregory Wade’s lamentation about the pro-
posed annexation of his town of Queanbeyan and
his Goulburn-Cooma railway are belated emotions.
The Canberra capital, if it ever comes to pass,
will almost certainly annex a lot more of N.S.W.’s
property than these before all things are done.
* * * *
It must be remembered that the members both
of the present Australian Government and of the
Government which preceded it didn’t want to
annex, steal, appropriate, or in any way in-
terfere with Gregory Wade’s town of Quean-
beyan. They didn’t want to take a large
piece out of his Goulburn-Cooma railway.
They didn’t want to steal from Sydney
the trade that is now borne towards Port Jackson
by seven or eight of the State’s southern and
south-western railways. They had no desire to
plant a rival city at Sydney’s door, or to construct
a rival port only 87 miles south of Sydney, and
beguile the mail steamers and the other carriers
of sea commerce thereto. All these things were
forced on them by a foolish, parochial-minded
party of which the prize bungler, Gregory
Wade, is the figurehead and representative.
The original idea was to place the metropolis of
Australia where it couldn’t interfere with any
N.S.W. railways because there were none there-
abouts. The proposed capital at Dalgety jay in a
region of such abundant water that there was no
chance, as there is at Canberra, that the Common-
wealth would have shortly to demand another 1000
or 2000 square miles of N.S.W. territory for catch-
ment purposes. It didn’t involve any great annexa-
tion of N.S.W.’s population, nor any great conse-
quent diminution of N.S.W.’s political import-
ance, for it was situated where there were no
towns large enough to be worth mentioning. A
city on Monaro would be so far away from
Sydney that it couldn’t by any possibility be a
serious rival, any more than Brisbane is, or Ade-
laide. A Federal port on Twofold Bay would be
so remote from Sydney that it would have to
create a trade of its own around the Monaro and
East Gippsland country, and in this respect it is
quite unlike Jervis Bay, which must needs inter-
cept Sydney’s trade in order to get any trade at
all. The men who made the first selection of a
capital site were good Australians, desirous to do
the best that was possible for the country at large,
while inflicting no injury on any State interest.
But they were overruled by a crowd of disordered
parochialists who were too insensate in their
greed and too hurried in their grab to realise that
they might possibly be reaching out for a great
disaster instead of a great benefit. Wherever the
Federal capital may be it will be the ambition
of all good Australians to make it a great,
rich, prosperous city, and to make its port a
commercial and naval centre worthy of the country
to which it belongs. If the capital is so situated
that this can be done without entering into rivalry
with any State centre, every good Australian will
be more than satisfied. If, through the folly of
clumsy meddlers like WAde, it is placed where it
and its port can only become great by eating up
a State rival —well. Australia can find enough
money to provide all the necessary means for eat-
ing up any rival. It will be a curious situation if
it is the votes of Victorian and South Australian
members which carry the proposals by which
Australia will be taxed (N.S.W. paying two-fifths
of the taxes) to equip the Federal port of Jervis
Bay in a way that will qualify it to lie Port Jack-
son’s competitor. Yet while such persons as
Wade are allowed to control public affairs more
remarkable things than that are possible.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Letter re Monaro.
Mr. J. H. Carruthers, ex-Premier of N.S.W.,
writes :
Your frequent references to recent remarks made by
me in praise of the district of Monaro and its climate are
based on erroneous conclusions, which you have, in your
own way, arrived at. You state that I have changed my
opinion of the Monaro district, and you incorrectly
attribute to me remarks, in times gone by, most dis-
paraging to the district.
Now it is a fact, which the records of Parliament will
prove—that I moved, as Premier, a motion to offer Dal-
gety to the Federal Government as one of the sites for
the Federal capital, and I voted for the inclusion of this
site when a large majority negatived the proposal. What-
ever personal views I may have held then or now, I, at
any rate, was one of the few who voted for the Dalgety
site to be offered by this State to the Federal Govern-
ment. After that motion was rejected by the Parliament
of this State, I simply did my duty to express the wish of
the people’s representatives—as the mouthpiece of
Parliament.
My opinion of Monaro, too, is well shown by the fact
that whilst Premier I caused the tourist district of
Kosciusko to be opened up by a first-class road, accom-
modation houses to be built, and, generally, provision to
be made for the travelling and touring public to more
easily reach the Snowy River district. For over seven or
eight years I have spent the greater part of my brief
holidays on Monaro amongst good friends there, and I
hope to be spared to have many a happy day there in
years to come.
The objections to Dalgety as a Federal capital, so far
as the N.S.W. Parliament is concerned, were not to
Monaro as a whole, but to Dalgety as an isolated part of
the same. The N.S.W. Railway Commissioners, rightly
or wrongly, reported that to make the necessary railway-
connections for express traffic would cost nearly two
millions sterling, with very little prospect of a paying
return. This included not only the line to Dalgety, but
an extension to the Victorian border to meet a proposed
connection there with the railways of the sister State.
Canberra —the proposed or rather the selected Federal
territory-—is only about 60 or 60 miles from Cooma, and is
practically on the Monaro tableland. If Canberra is such
a wretched place as The. Bulletin describes it to be, then
for the life of me I cannot see where all the difference
comes in as between Dalgety and Canberra. They are
both on the Monaro or southern tableland, their elevation
is about the same, viz., 2300 ft., their climate is absolutely
Similar, even to rainfall, and those who know the
localities will honestly admit that, whilst the Snowy-
River may- offer a better water supply, yet the Canberra
site possesses in the rivers adjacent a supply of almost
equal value.
This point, however, stands in favor of Canberra, viz.,
that the railway is already- constructed, and has been
made fit for express traffic right up to Queanbeyan.
As a matter of fact, I made the offer to Mr. John
Forrest whilst he was Acting-Premier of the Common-
wealth that, if the Federal Government would remove the
strong ground of objection in regard to expenditure on
the railway by making the cost of the railway extension
and improvement a matter of Federal concern, I would re-
submit the matter to the N. S. Wales Parliament and
support strongly the grant of the Dalgety site. The re-
signation of Mr. John Forrest shortly after caused the
matter to remain unconsidered.
I desire, however, to say this in conclusionythat in my-
own case, not once but repeatedly, the bracing, rarefied
air of the Kosciusko district, charged as it is with oxygen,
pure as it comes from the snow-fields of the mountains,
has done wonders for my health. Unfortunately the wind
knows how to blow in the same regions, and one has to
put up with some discomfort in the change of seasons,
in spring and autumn, from the tempestuous gales that
occasionally arise. Taken as a whole, the Monaro district
or the southern tableland has a climate both at Canberra
and on the Snowy River which will build up a strong and
hardy breed of men and women.
It is on the whole a cheering letter, though it
suggests a need for enlightenment on some points.
It seems, for instance, hardly worth worrying
about the weather of Dalgety, seeing that the
climate at Canberra is alleged to be just the same
sort of weather. Then the statement that the
rainfall is the same at both places has no bearing
on the case. The great fact is that Dalgety’s
Snowy River comes down from Kiandra, where
there is an average annual rainfall of 64in. to feed
it; while the Cotter, which is to be the great feature
of Canberra, is only 25 miles long and toddles
down from a region with a miserable 24in. rain-
fall. A city’s water supply doesn’t depend on the
rain that falls on the city itself, but on the rain
that -falls on the catchment area of its
river. As Mr. Carruthers cannot for the life of
him see where the difference between Dalgety and
Canberra comes in, this paper has great pleasure in
thus explaining. As for the statement that there is
very little prospect of making a railway to
Dalgety pay, well, if a railway won’t pay
when it connects with the only city in the
world which is the capital of a whole con-
tinent, and when that city is also fitted,
by reason of its abundant water power, to
be the greatest and cheapest manufacturing centre
in that continent, then a line to Sydney isn’t
likely to pay, and a railway connection with
Berlin or Birmingham is calculated to be a dead
loss. If the N.S.W. Railway Commissioners
really made that insane remark, then they have
an inability to look into the future which quite
unfits them for their positions. As regards the
fact that a railway would have to be con-
structed to Dalgety, that would be a draw-
back if the making of railways were a
misfortune and the existence of them a
thing to be deplored. Seeing, however, that they
are considered rather desirable than otherwise,
one might regard the selection of a capital site in
a region where its existence would lead to,
and justify, the building of one more line
as rather desirable than otherwise. Of course,
as Mr. Carruthers says, the Goulburn-
Coomk line would have to be improved, in
order that the railway to Dalgety would
be fit for express traffic, but it seems that
that necessity has arisen without any question of a
capital on the Snowy River; at least Mr. Car-
ruthers says that the work has been started, and
is already nearly half-finished. All the same, as
already remarked, it is a rather cheering letter.
The Great Dreadnought Bluff.
A dodge or lie or device or bluff or public job
which is more characteristic, perhaps, of the Aus-
tralian daily press than of any other among the
English-speaking peoples, is the dodge or lie or
bluff which it performs in the manufacture of
bogus public opinion. Bogus public opinion is a
remarkable thing. It arises through the seed of
impudence and falsehood being planted in the soil
of credulity, and the surface being manured with
lies and irrigated with bosh, there is sometimes a
fairly good harvest. The Yassberra capital ques-
tion was a case in point. Nobody save the Sydney
papers will gain a copper out of having the capital
at Yassberra with a port and naval base at Jervis
Bay. Sydney generally will lose through the com-
petition of a Federal rival port at its very door
and the diversion of the southern trade thereto.
The N.S.W. railway department will lose through
that same diversion of trade. It will be necessary in
the end to cede to the Commonwealth far more
territory than would have been necessary had the
capital been located at Dalgety, for in a badly
watered neighborhood like Yassberra 1000 square
miles will ultimately prove quite insufficient for
water catchment purposes, so again N.S.W. will
lose. Australia will lose by . haying an inferior
capital and an inferior naval port, when it might
have had a first-rate capital and port. But Yass-
berra is so near Sydney that the Sydney papers
will get there before the Melbourne ones, and it
is so near to Sydney that the Sydney papers will
be able to compete strongly for the local circula-
tion and advertising business even against a new
daily on the spot. So the Sydney papers de-
clared persistently that there was an immense
popular demand for Yassberra, till at last they
persuaded a herd or drove of time-serving
politicians that their only chance of re-election
lay in voting for the wretched spot. That
was a fine sample of the manufacture of
bogus public opinion, and a similar method is now
being adopted to boom the Dreadnought foolish-
ness. The S. M. Herald’s London correspondent
has declared, with brutal frankness that the
marine scare was merely Balfour’s hoax, got up
to push Asquith out of office. But all the same
the Dreadnought ghost is too valuable a bogey to
be dropped. It promises (1) to get titles for Some
people who want titles badly ; (2) to advertise
some people who want advertisement badly ; (3)
to shove the Fisher Government out of office ; (4)
to divert attention from the need for Australian
defence ; (5) to tie the finances in a knot which
will cause the postponement of the Northern Ter-
ritory transfer, and the building of the two trans-
continental railways, and a number of other
matters which should plainly be postponed till
Cook is in office, so that Cook may get the glory
thereof. So we find the papers spotted over with
the evidences of bogus or manufactured public
opinion like the following :
They say they want an appeal to the country if they
should he found in a minority over this question when
the House meets. Do these gentlemen not realise that
an appeal of the most effective kind is now being made
on this identical matter? In every part of Australia an
emphatic affirmation of the Dreadnought policy is being
publicly and privately made. Almost everyone outside
the mere officialdom of the Labor-Socialists is in favor.
And we should also remember that an app al like this is
of greater force by reason of its being kept free of other
confusing issues. . . . The idea originated with the
people ;it is being propagated by the people. In other
words, the very essence of theprincipal of popular appeal
is being applied to the movement. Is this why the Labor-
Socialists are so bitterly opposed to it?—Joseph Cook.
I regret that the Fisher Ministry should have taken
such a narrow view of the Dreadnought question! The
offer of Australia is not only wise and generous, but
politic.—Alfred Deakin.
Mr. Fisher has declared that he will not give a Dread-
nought. Then the sooner he is put out of office the
better. If Mr. Fisher still declines to carry out the pro-
posal after Parliament meets, it will he the duty of Mr.
Deakin and Mr. Joseph Cook to-consider what step is
desirable to give effect to the will and mandate of the
country.—John Quick.
All these are mere echoes of the daily paper
statements that the people of Australia are
craving—bellowing indeed —to give a Dread-
nought to the Old Ma Country, and that any
politician who opposes the popular will must go
down wallop at next election with the ruins of
public sentiment falling on top of him like the
wreckage of Messina. Yet, are the people
craving, howling, insisting on paying £2,000,000
(say 9s. 4d. per head) for ativ presentation Dread-
nought? If they are, the simple way to prove the
fact is for the people to pay. It is as easy for
them to send in the money to the Dreadnought
fund as to send it in later to a Federal taxation
department. The blessed fund has been open
long enough to allow of subscriptions coming in
from furthest-out Australia. And yet the result
is below £70,000. Leaving ou t five sensational sub-
scriptions or promises, it is below £25,000. The
Commonwealth’s population is set down at
4,299,188 people, so five persons have given or
promised £45,000 and 4,299,183 have given less
than £25,000. If an average is struck it comes to
this —that about 50,000 persons have declared
their desire to give a Dreadnought by the simple
process of paying their share, and 4,249,183 have
so far declined to have anything to do with the
matter. Where is the overwhelming public
opinion, and the alleged mandate and the alleged
‘•offer of Australia ” we hear so much about in the
daily papers and in the mouths of daily paper-
bossed politicians? The Fisher Government
should meet Parliament with a Referendum Bill,
and announce that the first use to which it will be
put will be to ascertain if there is really any
Dreadnought furore at all, or if the whole thing
is a monstrous hoax. The Referendum is a great
slayer of bogus public opinion.
4
On the Discrepancy between Wholesale and
Retail Prices.
Quite a number of persons have arisen lately in
Melbourne to remark on the incompetence of the
parties-who manage the city’s food supplies-4par-
ticularly those of fish, fruit, and vegetables. First,
the Political Labor Conference hurriedly circum-
navigated the subject, and opined that a Common-
wealth Commission should inquire into it. Then
the press remarked on the circumstance that cer-
tain Gippsland orchardists were feeding apples to
their pigs, because it didn’t pay to send the fruit
to market. Then Graham, Minister of Agricul-
ture, after telling how he had seen apples for
which he got lv>d. per lb. retailed at 6d. per lb.,
whereupon he grubbed out his 20 acres of
orchard, said that “something must be done.”
Just so. Likewise ditto. But what that some-
thing is nobody seems to have any idea.
It is indisputable that in regard to at least these
items of produce—fish, fruit, vegetables—the
producer gets half what he should, and the con-
sumer pays twice as much as he need do if the
system of distribution that Private Enterprise has
inaugurated were in a less crude and ramshackle
state, and if the private landlord didn’t take so
much of the profit. What is wanted is a large
wholesale market managed entirely by the City
Council, or preferably by a Greater Melbourne
Council, as agent, with smaller markets or dis-
tributing stores in each suburb where theconsunu r
could be attended to. Of course, all the consumers


wouldn’t and couldn’t attend these stores, but the
fact that a considerable number did so would
keep down prices in the shops to a reasonable
level. But Minister Graham’s idea of “doing
something ’’-apparently means the passing of an
Act or two with a view to assisting the export
trade to the advantage of both local and exporting
middleman, and to the disadvantage of the local
consumer. The Commonwealth Parliament has
in front of it more work than it can do in 20 years
—work that urgently needs doing—and, anyhow,
the organisation and management of such food
supplies as those mentioned is the proper busi-
ness of the municipal councils. Wherefore the
sooner Labor captures a majority of the metro-
politan councils—it has collared one already, and
has a good footing in several others—the sooner
will there be a chance to deal with this question.

Wanted—Federal Control of Industrial
Legislation.
A match-maker writes :
About the beginning- of this year a couple of big British
match-making firms (Bell & 00. and Bryant & May Ltd.)
notified the Victorian Government that, in consequence
of the Australian tariff, they proposed to manufacture
matches in Victoria for the future. They asked for one
boon before erecting their factories and starting to
employ Australian workers in them at Australian wage-
rates. And it wasn’t one that was calculated to cost the
State anything either. They merely asked that the
State should prohibit the use of white phosphorus in the
making of matches within its boundaries, on the same
principle that the Commonwealth Government has pro-
hibited, after June next, the importation of matches con-
taining this awful substance.
Match-making with white phosphorus is one of the
deadliest of the dangerous trades of Europe. Since
when Lorinder, of Vienna, first drew the attention
of the medical profession to phosphorus necrosis in
lucifer-match makers, the use of phosphorus has, as Dr.
Oliver remarks, “ been followed by such an amount of
ill-health and suffering on the part of match-makers that
almost all the preventive measures which industrial
science and legislation could suggest, short of total pro-
hibition, have had a trial wit hout rendering the industry
completely safe.” As a result of a number of cases o'f
phossy jaw in London match factories some years ago,
a revision of the “Special Rules” imposed upon
manufacturers was undertaken by the Home Office.
Measures were fashioned to insure "adequate ventilation
of factories, and to provide for frequent dental examina-
tion and treatment of employees, and for the compulsory
use by them of mouth washes. Also mechanical con-
trivances for drawing the fumes of the phosphorus away
from the faces of the workers were installed broadcast.
But, in spite of these precautions and theconstant vigilance
of factory inspectors, “phossy jaw" has gone on doing
its deadly work in the United Kingdom. And now the
big match-manufacturing firms have come to the con-
nothing but the absolute stoppage of the use
of phosphorus in theirindustry will be of any avail But
as is natural, they don’t mean to start in Victoria or else-
where pledged against using phosphorus, only to find that
rival firms have no such scruples, and are employing the
cheap and deadly substance.
To their suggestion that Victoria shall bar the mur-
derous phosphorus in its factories, as is done in Den-
mark, which took that wise and humane step in the
interests of its workers many years ago, neither Bent nor
Murray has apparently any answer. A State Premier
hardly ever has in such circumstances. It affords yet
another example of the need of all industrial legislation
being controlled by the Commonwealth. Here are firms
of repute willing to give employment to manv hundreds
of Australian workers. And all the firms in question ask
is that the State shall take steps to ensure that that
employment will be carried on underthe healthiest possi-
ble conditions. The State has had the right path
indicated beforehand by the Federal legislation on
this question. Yet it remains quite cold and
disinterested in the matter. It is doubtful, anyhow
if a favorable answer from Victoria to the prospective
match manufacturers would, after all, produce satis-
factory results. There would still remain other States—
Tasmania, for example—whose chronic Toryism would
cause them to be the hunting ground of enterprises that
had been barred as against the public weal in other parts
of the Commonwealth. To Tasmania would proceed
phosphorus in hand, the manufacturer of matches who
was prevented from carrying on his awful concern in Vic-
toria or elsewhere. The only answer to the problem is
that the Australian Government should have full control
of industrial enterprises. It would be best in the long
run for both the enterprises and the people employed in
them. J
The Pro-Nigger Parson Again.
One of the knottiest problems which this publica-
tion has ever struck concerns the reason of the
frequent preacher’s craving to have foreign colored
people in Australia. It seems an incurable
craving, and is quite unaccountable except per-
haps on the ground of mental deficiency, or hypo-
crisy, or utter wickedness. There is, sad to say,
a Chinese society of some sort in Melbourne, and
at one of its functions, the other day, the usual
reverend individual spoke
He was sometimes impatient with the policy of a White
Australia. . . . Our White Australia policy could only be
defended as a temporary expedient. It could not last,
and he did not think the people wanted it to last. He
was looking forward to the time when our people would
be able to indulge what he might term the natural
British hospitality. (Applause.)
The remark about “ British hospitality ” is beauti-
fully parsonical. If hospitality consists in going
to live, uninvited, in the other fellow’s house, and
taking possession of the house, then the Briton is.
hospitable enough. He rules over about one-third
of the colored races of the world. He does that
simply because he conquered them and practised
the hospitality that consists in going to reside on the
other fellow’s premises. He ikeeps possession by
the simple process of declaring the colored man
unfit to enjoy the British citizen’s alleged right of
self-government, and by ranking him, in a political
sense, with the cow and the dog. He got Aus-
tralia in the same way as he got his other nigger
possessions; but since the original English settler-
died out and the Australian-born citizen took his.
place, this country has at least tried to do some-
part of its duty to the colored brother. He can.
have a vote and a share in the government of the
country the same as the white citizen, if he chooses,
to claim these privileges ; unfortunately his mental
status is so low that he doesn’t care to claim them.
The Australian-born is at least guiltless of that
form of British hospitality which consists in actu-
ally seizing your neighbor’s house and treating
your neighbor as an inferior animal because he was
the original owner of the house. But the average-
parson is at hearta hater of freedom and democratic
government, and a lover of tyranny and despotism ;
therefore he lies magnificently about these things,
and his perversions are marvellous. As for Aus-
tralia’s desire to avoid any admixture with colored
races from outside—well, Australia simply accepts
the warnings of history. It recognises that the
most backward nations of Europe are those with
the Asiatic or African taint—Russia, Turkey,
Greece and Southern Austria, with their Asiatic-
admixtures ; Southern Italy, Spain and Portugal,
with their African disease. Portugal was once
the most civilised kingdom of Europe, but some
crazy rulers took to importing African cheap
labor, and carried on the process till, in Lisbon
Jtself, negroes were quite as common as Europeans.
The black and white races intermarried, and now
Portugal is the most ignorant, backward, corrupt
<md hopeless country in Europe— a, country "where
even roads have almost vanished, and which is-
merely Haiti over again. The Australian has con-
sidered Russia, Turkey, Greece, Spain, Portugal,
Haiti, San Domingo, the Eurasian person of India
the people and ways of Egypt, Tripoli, Morocco!
Tunis, Guatemala, Honduras, San Salvador, Costa
Rica, Cuba, Columbia, Ecuador, Venezuela, Peru,
Brazil, and a whole lot of other partly-white and
partly-colored States. And for intelligence, pro-
gress, cleanliness, order, decency, and for every-
thing which makes man a desirable citizen these
places compare very badly with such countries as
Scandinavia, Denmark, Germany, Britain, France,
Holland, Belgium, etc., which have kept their
white blood clean. Therefore, Australia has re-
solved to keep itself among the white nations, and
nobody really objects save a few dreadful parsons
like the one quoted above. Whether a certain kind
of parson wants to start this country on the road
which Haiti and Portugal have followed because
that kind of parson is the most unutterably
wicked person in the community, or because he
is the worst mental degenerate in the com-
munity, the Australian democrat doesn’t exactly
know, nor does he greatly care. But when
he is asked why he doesn’t go to church the
answer is easy. A larger proportion of parson
than of any other olass in the community has
devoted itself heart and soul to the work of drag-
ging Australia down to the level of Haiti or Cuba
by mixing the white race with the black. Parsons
have toiled in a strangely numerous way for the
promotion of national degeneracy, and even those
of their brethren who have devoted themselves to
the national cause haven’t repudiated the horrible
kind of parson with the scorn and loathing that
they should have shown if they desired to keep
themselves clean before the world. So the aver-
age good democrat doesn’t go to church. To him
the nigger labor parson, who toils to bring Aus-
tralia down to the status of Honduras or Tripoli,
who borrows the sacred name of Got) for his evil
work, and who falsifies history in his craving to
be always pro-nigger and anti-Australian, is too
low to be worth contempt and too cheap to even
call for active loathing. The only thing is to keep
away from him and his desecrated fane, and if
possible to avoid burial in the same paddock with
him, lest the General Resurrection of the dead
should find that good democrat in bad company.
The Only Way.
Equine ” writes: —
General Hoad’s shocked query last week as to what
steps Australia was taking to provide itself with the
military horses it will assuredly need within the next few
years has caused the Anti-Sosh press to ponder some-
what. The Sydney Telegraph admitted (21/4/’O9) the
appalling' dearth in N.S.W. of animals suitable for re-
mount purposes, and then essayed to explain the phe-
nomenon. It said:—
“ With the cutting up of the big estate, a process which
has gone on especially in districts suitable for this form
■of culture, has come the small man, who needs a quicker
turnover for his labor than the horse affords. The uncer-
tainty which exists as to standards is another discourag-
ing circumstance. Different remount officers have
•different ideas, so that the bewildered farmer is driven to
leave the horse alone, and give his attention to products
for which he has a wider market scope."
And then it went on to propound the remedy :
“ But, no doubt, all these would prove transient difficul-
ties in the face of a determined effort to make Australia
the great source of supply of the Empire’s needs in horse-
flesh. We have the climate and the soil. The rest is a
question of price and stability of market.”
It is a characteristically inaccurate and vague and un-
satisfactory pronouncement. Even assuming that any
number worth mentioning of big horse-producing estates
had been cut up—which is, unfortunately, not the ease
—that would not account for the state of things which
the Telegraph deplores. As a fact, the same land-
owners who bred general utility horses a decade ago are
breeding them now. There are enough brumbies and to
spare running loose on the broad acres of Outback. The
trouble is that the animals are a different class from their
ancestors of 10 years back. The old-time Australian steed
had a character to lose, so to speak. It stood high in the
•estimation of horsey men the world over. The
modern equine bears the name at home and abroad
of being a degenerate. The foreign buyer has started
to sniff loudly at it. So has the * local military
man. And with good cause. Assuredly the average up
•country Australian horse of to-day is the poorest,
weediest, straightest-shouldered, leggiest burlesque
going. The explanation is well known to every
man interested in the horse-breeding business through-
out the Commonwealth. The serviceable steed is
not here, for the most part, because it or its
potential sire or dam have been shipped away to Japan
or to Java or to South Africa, or to India. For years
past, whenever and wherever a decent weight-carrier has
projected its ears over the landscape, it has been coralled
•and sold overseasj And so the fit have been weeded out
and the unfit remain.
It should be too plain for serious argument that unless
•this insane business is forcibly stopped, and that at once,
Australia’s already moribund horse-supply will fall utterly
•dead. Loud, general demands that “ determined efforts’”
•shall be made to make Australia “the great source of supply
of the Empire’s needs in horse flesh ” cut no ice. There
•are but two directions in which “determined efforts”
can be of avail, viz., (1) the imposition of the export
duty above mentioned, and (2) the establishment of State
horse-farms; and both of them have been roundly
damned by the Telegraph and other sheets of its political
way of thinking—the first as Protection, the second as
Socialism. The Labor Government’s scheme of a Com-
monwealth horse-breeding establishment received a warm
pat On the back from the allegedly Anti-Sosh Evening
News on the afternoon of the day the Telegraph made
its plaint. Said the News :
“ In any case, as regards this question of supply for the
Commonwealth military forces, the breeding should be
carried out systematically, and not by individuals, each
with his own notion of what constitutes a horse suitable
for the service. And for this reason, among others, it is
probable that a State enterprise of the sort suggested by
the Minister would prove the most successful scheme of
any. The business is strictly a Federal one ; one, too,
that cannot be begun too soon. It takes five years to
make a horse fitted for military work—saddle or draught.
But once the breeding is in full swing, the supply would
be constant. Also, if necessary, the boarding-out plan
might be introduced in order to provide for a reserve
always at band. To relj 7 anj' longer on private sources
for the horsing of men and material is evidently out of
the question. Indeed, it is difficult to see what other
conclusion the Board just appointed to report on the sub-
ject can arrive at, other than to support the plan of a
State horse farm. Properly established and conducted,
it should, in due course, not only be able to fulfil all
military needs, but, in addition, form a source of con-
siderable profit, and thus be self-supporting, if not
actually revenue-producing.”
But a Commonwealth horse-breeding establishment,
invaluable as it is going to be in its way, can’t cope with
the whole of Australia’s horse trouble. The root of it
lies in the widespread habit that has grown on country
horse-breeders of regarding every likely-looking hack or
hunter up to twelve sLone or over as a candidate for “ the
East.” Nothing but a smashing impost on every horse
sent abroad will cure that. When that bit of legislation
is put on the statute books, and a little more attention
paid by the States to the class of entire that roams the
country, the Australian horse should begin to be
in point of quality as well as numerousness the
same animal it was in the years that have
flown. Apropos , it was State interference that
made the English horse. In Henry VIII.’s time the
English Government took violent measures by slaying all
stallions that were below a certain standard of excellence,
and passing large and furious laws to prevent the export
and encourage the import of good breeding stock. So
the English steed, after ages of degeneracy, was lifted
almost in a day from being a stunted and grotesque weed
to the status of the finest equine in Europe. It wasn’t a
“determined ” and promiscuous “ effort ” of the kind the
Sydney Telegraph demands which did it, but State
Interference of the most reckless character.
In re Anti-Socialism, and the Fraudulence
Thereof.
Sydney Telegraph published this item recently :
In the course of a brief report to a meeting of the
Turori Shire Council concerning the late Shires’ Con-
ference in Sydney, Cr. H. C. Suttor, president, said the
chief tendency throughout the proceedings was to milk
the Government cow. He thought, however, that if they
tried to milk her too much she would kick, and instead
of giving them more grants, would compel them to put
on more taxation.
The Turon Shire’s president is one of Bathurst’s
big landowners and an earnest Anti-Socialist.
The above extract from his report is noteworthy
as epitomising the attitude of the typical Anti-
Socialist (so-called) towards the thing that his
kind asserts must cause homes, religions and soon
to burst asunder and fly in pieces. It amounts to
a cynical admission that, in hard practice, there
is nothing whatever in the creed of Anti-Socialism ;
and that, in fact, the strivings after G. Reid’s
Tiger on the part of any self-respecting Anti-
Sosh Shire Council should only halt at the
stage where it is morally certain that the
monster will turn round and, figuratively, rend
those who are trying to borrow money from it.
Anti-Socialist SuTTOßdoesn’t pretend to reprobate
the milking of the State cow on ethical grounds.
A.nd indeed if he did he would be laughed at by
his fellow Anti-Socialists for his pains. The only
point he makes in his plea for moderation in this
regard is that the animal has about reached the
kicking stage. This attitude is not peculiar to
the sort of Anti-Socialist who belongs to shire
councils. It is to be found on all sides. Bathurst,
for example, boasts one of the biggest Agricul-
tural, Horticultural and Pastoral Shows in N.S.W.
Ninety per cent, of the show committee-men
would describe themselves on any given Federal
or State polling-day as “ Anti-Socialist to the
backbone,” and would vote accordingly. Yet
there is no day—polling-day not excepted—on
which they aren’t cheerfully prepared to cadge all
the State assistance that is going for the institution
which so effectively booms their town. And if there
were talk of the £ for £ subsidy which is now
given to the Show by the State, being withdrawn
in order that an opportunity might be accorded it
of flourishing in an atmosphere of pure Bruce
Smith individualism, there would arise a howl of
Anti-Socialist agony and protest of such volume
as to be clearly audible in Bourke. The Bathurst
School of Arts gets generous help from the
Government. About the only ground for serious
complaint that the sturdy Anti-Socialists who
control the Bathurst School of Arts have against
Wade is that Wade doesn’t supply their institu-
tion with more Sosh than he does. And so with
the Hospital. There is never a word of protest
from the allegedly Anti-Socialist directors of
it against the morality-sapping system by
which the Hospital is permeated from roof
to cellar with the Sosh menace. On the
contrary. The only fear those in authority
have is lest any fragment of Sosh should be
withdrawn from them; and their main hope
lies in the direction of more of the so-called
calamity coming their way. From its municipal
gasworks to its gaol, and from its gardens, with
their free band recitals, to its State-owned rail-
way station, Bathurst is riddled through and
through with State Socialism. Most of the
better-paid officials connected with these enter-
prises would take it as an insult if anyone called
them other than Anti-Socialists. Withal there is
not one that does not long to see all the Sosh
that is going hurled at the particular concern
with which he is connected. It would be interest-
ing to hear the views of a Freetrader on a Pro-
tectionist who advocated a wholesale lowering of
duties; also it would be diverting to read the Tele-
graph or other Anti-Sosh sheet the morning after it
had discovered a Laborite who clamored for the
denationalising of the tramway or railway services
of the State. Yet prominent Anti-Socialists are
doing as contradictory things as these all over the
country. Can it be a matter of surprise that the
constituencies refused at last Federal elections to
regard the Anti-Sosh question seriousjy, and that
they give clear indications of not meaning to
regard it seriously at the coming struggle ? One
of the first rules of the electioneering game is
that, when a man sets up a fiscal or economical
doctrine for polling-day assailment, and denounces
it on and in the immediate neighborhood of that
day as a menace and a curse and a tiger, and so
forth, that he shall at least affect between whiles
to believe that it is the calamity he says it is, and
treat it accordingly. Most of N.S.W.’s bogus
Anti-Socialists don tdo that. Having feverishly
chased the thing that they claim to believe means
damnation during a period of two years nine
months and some odd weeks, they seem to con-
sider they have done their duty by their principles
if they rail at it for the matter of a fortnight or so.
The morning papers provide the public with a lot
of valuable ignorance at times. For instance, the
Sydney Telegraph got off a very old lie in its
leading article of April 28—a lie that was supposed
to be dead when the myth of Mrs. Tozer’s maid
perished :
The Federal Government, bars out anyone coming under
a contract which guarantees him employment.
Now in 1907 (the last year for which returns were
published) 972 immigrants came into Australia to
do manual labor under contract, and not one con-
tract laborer was refused admission. As for actors,
preachers, musicians, and every kind of contract
worker who isn’t strictly a manual laborer, they
come in unquestioned, and nobody even keeps a
record of them. The Telegraph knows these
things, but the habit which the importers’ organ
has of wholesale and retail lying, to the detriment
of its country, is apparently past cure. There is
in Australia still a remnant of that Tory Free-
trade importing gang which used to hold that any
lie, slander or misrepresentation was justifiable so
long as it was a lie or slander directed against
Australia. The theory of the infamous mob of
wealthy hooligans which created the old Stinking
Fish'party was that mud covereth a multitude of
sins so long as it was mud thrown at the fair fame
of their own country. And apparently Stinking
Fish will never feel the want of an outlet for its
muddy feelings while the Telegraph remains on
deck.

An angry man from Melbourne :
Vic. railway boss Tait has got another sizeable trouble
on bis hands. This concerns the Brighton electric tram-
way, which is at present a good counterfeit of the road to
ruin, or at least a road in ruins. This line was built and
run for a while by electrical expert Bradford, who was
subsequently pushed out of it. The Department said he
didn’t understand his business, and put its own expert
in charge. An inquiry exonerated him in so far as the
inquirers could lay their hands on the evidence that was
very shy of coming, and now Bradford is claiming a large
sum from the Government as compensation and so forth.
Apparently Tait and his expert are trying to show a
more economical management of the concern than Brad-
ford by starving the line. Wherefore the Brighton
council lately called the Canadian’s attention to the fact
that the sleepers were almost bare of asphalt—the line
runs along the beach road—and that the upstanding rails
would be effective as a paddock fence, but were
not an ornament to a carriage-way. Tait’s first
answer was to inform the mayor that he “wouldn’t
have every Tom, Dick, and 'Harry interfering in
his business,” but the council pointed out that, as
guarantors with St. Kilda of the interest on the cost of
the line, and as trustees of the road over which Tait ran
his trams, the matter was distinctly its business. Tait’s
expert thereupon interviewed Brighton’s mayor and
spoke him softly, saying that the Commissioners were
having trouble with Bradford aforesaid, and wished to
avoid comparisons that would be made if they incurred
further immediate expenditure on maintaining the per-
manent way. Also the expert soothingly assured
the mayor that Tait wished the interview to
be considered private, but the mayor sarcasti-
cally replied that, being a public man and not
any Tom, Dick or Harry, he wasn’t taking any
privacy ; and he didn’t. The episode suggests that Tait’s
policy seems to have been to cause a bungle and muddle
of the only electric line he has to manage, and then, when
he found comparisons were likely to be made of his
management and Bradford’s, to attempt to save his face
by underhand methods. Taking it all round, it looks as
if the Murray Government might do worse than com-
mission electrician Bradford to transform the St. Kilda
and Port Melbourne lines into electrical systems. If he
made a success of them he could then be told to bog in
some more.

Though they are superfluous enough, Heaven
knows, Federal Ministers keep on pointing out
additional reasons for the speedy impost of a dis-
ruptive land tax. Lately, Defence Minister
Pearce told the Horshamites, of Victoria, that
while in 1906 Victoria had 12,930,250 acres held in
blocks of over 1000 acres each, by 1908 the evil of
great estates had increased to such an extent that
the properties of over 1000 acres each aggregated
13,057,911 acres. This happened during the very
years when Bent was supposed to be buying up
large estates and settling people on the land. The
same day Attorney-General Hughes casually in-
formed the citizens of Benalla that, though in the
Cold Country the wealthy classes paid £60,000,000
of taxation to the general public’s £68,000,000, in
Australia Fat paid only £3,000,000 out of a total
£14,000,000. Which, seeing that the defence of a
country is needed quite as much for Fat’s pro-
perty as his person, and seeing that the poor man
has little, if any, property to defend, are addi-
tional reasons why Fat should be taxed for defence
purposes. If he kicks, he can be told to soothe
himself with the reflection that we are but
following England’s example.

According to the information of German experts, the

be able to man if it gets the article.
