In one State at any rate—Westralia—the Com-
monwealth and local electoral offices are working
in harmony, and whacking costs. And there are
various good points about Westralian electoral
management, too. The office happened to get
hold of a man with up-to-date ideas, or a man
with up-to-date ideas happened, to get hold of the -
office ; and among other things he introduced the
card system. Everybody who becomes an elector
signs two cards. One is kept in the district, the
other goes to the head-office. Electors are often
on the track in Westralia, and the man who is in
Yilgarn to-day is probably in York next week.
When he gets to York the chances are that
he will apply to go on the roll there, and won’t
mention that he was a Yilgarn elector hitherto ;
in fact, if he hasn’t actually voted there he may
disremember all about it. So he signs two more
cards, and when one goes down to the head office
it bumps against the same Yilgarn ticket.
Then the Yilgarn office is informed, and the name
is removed from the roll there. Thus the rolls
are to a great extent automatically revised, and
The Worms of Empire,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.
The worms that eat an empire down
Are busy eating now,
And soon they’ll claim as lawful prey
The broad Imperial brow ;
The tresses once with vict’ry crowned
Shall flout the breeze no more,
And all the powers of cold decay
Feast on her dying core.
The worms that eat an empire down—
High Hand and Callous Greed —
They’ve gnawed the nation’s heart away,
And spoilt a gallant breed ;
The manly stock is now a tale
Of ages past and gone,
And only shadowy legends still
And shadowy men live on !
The worms that eat an empire down—
To-day, as oft of yore.
They feed on many a morsel rare,
On many a league of shore ;
They feed them fat on gallant hearts
That vainly beat and bled—
The Veldt, the Hills of Hindostan—
How well they know the dead !
The worms that eat an empire down
They bite our breast to-day,
And somp they spring from this our soil
And some from foreign clay ;
But, be they bred of alien earth
Or sprung from native lust,
Right surely shall they raze and bring
Our glories in the dust.
The worms that eat an empire down,
We feel their presence here;
And though we curse their toil afar
We heed it not a-near;
And so the tresses vict’ry-crowned
Shall flout the breeze no more,
And all the powers of cold deeay
Feast on our dying core !
■QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.

The wisdom which sparkles so brightly in
Lloyd George’s equitable taxation proposals in-
dicates the illuminating inspiration of D.C.L.
Whisky. Continued judicious resort to the
scholarly brand, and tariff reform must follow
these humane revenue-raising methods. Pro-
gressive legislation follows the amber light of
D.C.L. like a phosphorescent gleam in the sea of
political wisdom.*

The Cabbage Garden Woman Goes into
Politics.
Melbourne, especially suburban Melbourne,
hardly knew itself on Saturday last, after 1 frm.,
when the Saturday half-holiday came into force*
The shops had been open till 10 the previous
evening, and apparently everyone had accepted
the new order of things, so in the closing hours o
the week Smith-street, Collingwood, and Chapel-
street, P’ran—once the happy hunting-ground of
the woman with the pramful of mixed baby and
vegetables, or of the family out to buy Jimmy a
new suit, or Jennie a pair of shoes—were as
deserted as the paths of righteousness. Only here
and there a belated Tory declaimed against this
“ gross interference with the liberty of the sub-
ject,” or a procrastinating housewife hammered
on locked doors, in the vain hope that they
would open of themselves. Melbourne had,
without a murmur, accepted an order of things
that 10 years ago would have caused a Rich Land-
lord to jump on his belltopper in the middle of
Bourke-street. But female suburbia was a very
angry aggregate nevertheless, and for good
reason. The Woman’s Suffrage Bill was sent to
England for King Ned’s signature months ago,
and Premier and Chief Sec. Murray knew as well
as the next man that it would be signed all right,
but he made no preparations for enrolling the
women. Consequently, when the Bill’s accept-
ance was notified a month back, the women voters
were told to go and take out rights—after the
stupid, cumbersome State plan-i-before May 1 if
they wished to be able to vote. Most of them did
wish. Now on the last week of April there is
always a rush of dilatory male vote-seekers, and
when, on top of this little crowd, came almost the
whole adult female population of Melbourne—the
country woman hasn’t wakened up yet—the hap-
less electoral registrars nearly lost their wits in a
vain attempt to cope with the demand. Day
after day on that last week crowds of weary and
angry women stood, 500 strong at times, on
verandahs and footpaths outside the offices,
often in the rain, waiting with amazing
patience to get the “right” to vote. It
was a pity the Rich Landlords hadn’t been
at hand to get an answer to their declarations
that women didn’t want the vote. Of
course the supplies of forms speedily ran out.
The printing office got to work with a rush, but
even when the books were available, the hapless
registrars were told to come into the city and
get them—the Department was so busy it
couldn’t spare a messenger to take them to a
parcel van. Finally, on Friday, Premier Murray
perceived that-something ought to be done, so the
town halls were converted into registry offices,
and every available officer was run out to help the
registrars. Even then there was a block, and
mayors and councillors swore themselves in as
assistant registrars and took a hand. And still
they couldn’t cope with the rush ! So
Murray decided to extend the time for
another month, trusting to Parliament to
validate his illegal action. Doubtless this was
the best thing he could do under the
circumstances, but it was partly through this very
caper of doing illegal acts and trusting to Parlia-
ment to validate them that Bent came to grief.
Anyhow, though Parliament may validate this
one act, it should emphatically tell Murray that
(1) he ought to have made preparation before-
hand ; (2) that with the example of Tasmania
before him he should at once take steps to use the
Federal rolls ; (3) that the longer he delays doing
so the more money he is throwing away; and
Victoria has no cash to spare at present (except
for Dreadnoughts). Finally, it might inform him
that the Tories who said women did not want
votes are asses.
The failure of the N.3.W. Wade Government to
securely “jug” Tom Mann for five years was a
national misfortune in more ways than one. In
the first place Mann is a blatant banger of chains
and exhorter to “arise,” who does the Labor
party in Australia incalculable harm ; for although
he is not of it, and is opposed to all its methods,
the party has to carry the burden of his frothy,
loud-mouthed foolishness. He is the sort of im-
possible mountebank whose only hope of doing
any good is to martyred by imprisonment or exe-
cution ; and apparently he is not anxious to do
good that way. If he had been convicted, his
enforced absence from mischief-making would have
done the Labor party a great benefit, and by the
time he came out he might have been forgotten. On
the other hand, while he was in gaol he might have
done the Wade gang considerable harm, whereas
by getting off he merely made it look foolish. His
arrest was a high-handed piece of tyranny, and
his imprisonment would have been a moral burden
that would have broken the back of even Wade’s
camel-like crowd. Therefore Mann might have
had two advantages in gaol; he might have
extinguished Wade, and he might have been
extinguished himself. It is hard to say which
extinguishment would have been better for the
country. Mann is opposed to legal arbitration,
to parliamentary reform, to everything but the
strike as a means of industrial redress.
And the strike is a two-edged weapon,
which, while it may destroy the tyrant,
usually kills the individual who wields it.
All the strikes in the world won’t make the toiler
anything else but a toiler, though they may,
under almost impossible circumstances, make
him a more affluent toiler. Always he will be a
wage slave; whereas by legislation he may be
elevated to be a partner and a profit-sharer. By
bungling his defence Mann had one glorious
opportunity to do some real good ; but he care-
fully refrained from bungling his defence, and
hired all sorts of excellent counsel to prevent him
taking advantage of his one beautiful opportunity.
“ 0.K.” writes
Rev. It. Gwynne—brother of the London Standard
editor —lias come out to Australia with a scheme
for irrigating this largely-unpopulated continent with
a small stream of English emigrants. His idea is
to ship 15-year-old boys—orphans or otherwise—
who shall be trained for a year “on the land” in Eng-
land before being sent out under apprenticeship (fur
three years) to farmers in the Commonwealth. Further-
more, Rev. Gwynne talks of pushing the British Govern-
ment to advance a sum of £50,000 a year to work the
scheme, with a view, presumably, to getting the money
refunded through some process of Government control on
this side. He promises that the boys, selected at 14
years of age for exportation twelve months later, will
he “healthy and sturdy”—not gutter-bred weaklings
with a moral and physical inaptitude for agricultural
work. So far, so good.
The fact that the Rev. R. Gwynne is an ardent Im-
perialist as well as a humanitarian, and that he naturally
dreams of supplying Australia with a fresh infusion of
Imperial sentiment by planting English-born lads upon
the land, need not alarm the anti-Dreadnought majority
of Australian nationalists. A boy of 14 who, in booking
himself for emigration to the Antipodes, mentally begins
to identify himself with the land he is bound for, is
already in a fair way to absorb its sentiment. When he
gets there, and breathes its atmosphere, his mind must
inevitably form itself on Australian lines. He brings no
adult prejudices, no deep-rooted regrets, no bitter
memories, no insufferable insular airs of superiority. The
imported British man may settle down into a philo-
sophical Australian and a sympathetic parent of native-
born children; but, as a rule, he is apt to be a very
biassed person—one way or other —all his life. If he
suffered a bad time in his birthland, and has done well for
himself in the new country, he libels the old country in
speaking always of the seamy side of its life, whereas, if
his experiences have been the reverse of these, he girds
at Australia with the vehemence of a remittance man,
who is the most obnoxious type of “Imperialist” this
country is afflicted with. The one lacks a leaven of
rational Imperial sentiment, and has no proper regard for
the privileges of his wide-spread native language. The
other is anti-Australian, and therefore an anti-imperialist,
notwithstanding his wearisome adoration of the Mother
Country and the dear old Flag.
If this country must get emigrants, it can’t do better
than get them from Britain. And it should encourage
every opportunity to get them young wherever they come
from. The lads that Mr. Gwynne talks of shipping to
Australia would grow with its growth, and having been
bent in their twig period of development towards an
Australian career, they wouldn’t be bent on returning to
England in the future, like the matured Englishman who
leaves old associations behind him. In short, the Rev.
Gwynne, in his harmless, ardent Imperialism, proposes to
dot the land with potential landowners who would have
no impelling force of curiosity as regards a “ trip ’Ome,”
and no desire to become absentees. Meanwhile he will
be glad to hear from “ farmers and employers of labor ”
with reference to his project, and his postal address is the
Melbourne Club.
♦ -
Melbourne's new central station won’t be fin-
ished for months, yet already the public begins to
suffer from that lack of foresight that is the curse
of all things created. The original idea was to
have one central station, abolishing Princes
Bridge terminus, and converting Spencer-street
into a suburban stopping place. There seems to
be a curious idea in Australia that all the traffic of
a big city should be worked from one station,
hence Sydney’s Redfern monstrosity; so Mel-
bourne, having accidentally avoided that evil, was
anxious to undo its sensible act, and get into the
same state of congestion as its neighbor. (Inci-
dentally, when Glasgow was just a shade larger
than Sydney or Melbourne it worked its railway
business from five terminii, and thus gave its trains
a certain amount of elbow room.) Very soon after
coming to this resolve Melbourne realised that it
was something extra of an ass. First of all,
it found that Spencer-street couldn’t be
spared, and that idea dropped with a thud. Then
Princes Bridge was found to be absolutely neces-
sary for the Heidelberg-Preston-Collingwood and
the Gippsland and Great Southern traffic. Already
it is overcrowded with this ever-increasing busi-
ness, while the Central—late Flinders-street—is
during the busy hours, a struggling, seething mass
of swearing humanity. Also the ramps and sub-
ways are too small, and they all empty into
Flinders-street, where the lorries, drays, parcel-
vans, trams, etc., already make foot-traffic a fleeting
spasm and a wild hysteria. Heaven only knows
how matters can be remedied. The big station
can be extended by building it on piles over the
Yarra, or it can be lengthened under Swanston-
street and “ the Bridge ” made part and parcel of
the one station. Also new subways can be put
right under Flinders-street so as to bring the
importer out at his office door in the Lane. But
that is an awkward solution at the best. The fact
is that the whole Congested Population system is
coming, home to roost. In the first place N.S.W.
and Victoria have a volume of trade that is
quite out of proportion to their population.
They have gone to vast and absurd expense to
drag all this trade to Sydney and Melbourne
respectively. Having got it there, the next
ambition was to take it all to one railway station,
so thiit when it emerged from the station it might
all rush down one street and raise Sheol. And
when the natural consequences of this policy
come along, great astonishment supervened.
S. M. Herald (23/4/’O9) explained why N.S.W.
banks are sitting rather tightly on their cash bags
just now. Here is some of the explanation :
If a man has a property worth, say, £lO,OOO, and he
commits himself, without consulting his banker, to an
additional area that is to cost him another £5OOO, he
cannot be surprised if he finds that the money is not
forthcoming, good as is the security offering. A year or
so ago th£re was a feeling among the more thoughtful of
financial men that the pace was becoming a little too
fast. Producers were beginning to take up land beyond
their capacity to effectively work it. They were paying
prices which at times were regarded as beyond its pro-
ductive value over a series of years, on the basis of
average prices. This sort of thing might have been
allowed to proceed too far, and, if it were only properly
recognised, the country is really indebted to those
bankers who were wise enough to call a halt.
This suggests one thing pretty clearly—that a lot
of the land settlement about which N.S.W.
Government has been skiting so loudly of recent
times wasn’t real settlement at all. It was merely
land-grabbing by existing landowners who reckoned
their names were good at the banks, and who
were so darned greedy that they were willing to
pay (or owe) any old price for land “beyond their
capacity to effectively work.” In other words,
they wanted the land just to sit on while waiting
for unearned increment at the expense of the
genuine land-seeker. Also, the Herald’s remarks
make it plain that the sooner tbe Federal Pro'
gressive Land Tax happens the better—it wil
help to knock out the “ prices that are beyond the
land’s productive value,” and the person who
monopolises land which he cannot “effectively
work.” Meanwhile, as the Herald remarks,
perhaps the country is really indebted to the
bankers who are putting their feet down on the
land-speculation process.

“ After the Opera,” a brilliant little play
of the Grand Guignol type, was staged at Sydney
Royal on Saturday night. It lasts less than half
an hour. Yet into that brief space is crammed
the tragedy of four lives. Like so many modern
French dramatic works, its construction is well-
nigh perfect. Each incident leading up to the
denouement dovetails into the next with con-
vincing precision, The adaptor has translated
the jargon of the Fauborg into that of Mayfair
with admirable skill. It is a faithful picture in
miniature of an easily-conceivable happening in
modern life, and, as such, is full of artistic value.
Lady Lulu Devas, a rake with an elderly
husband, wishes for a liason with Captain Follett.
The three return to the Devas’ home from the
opera. The husband is called away on business.
Lady Lulu prevails on Follett to come back to her
when the domestic coast is clear. Follett is willing
to “bolt,” as he puts it, but resists her suggestion of
an intrigue. However, since man is but human,
he is on the point of yielding when a sound is
heard in the passage outside. The guilty pair
jump to the conclusion that Devas is there.
Follett would stay and see the thing - through, but
Lady Lulu fears what she calls “the scandal,”
and drives him out. The door opens. A burglar
creeps in. The rays of his dark lantern rest on
the jewels the speechlessly-horrified woman is
wearing. He closes with her, strangles her, and
robs the body. Meanwhile, Follett has dropped
into the street below, and the arms of the police. •
As he is explaining, desperately, Devas appears.
He has missed his train. Bewilderment and horror
dawn in Follett’s eyes. If not Devas, then, who is it
that is with Lady Lulu ? Frenziedly he urges the
others into the house. They find the dead
woman, but her assassin is gone. In the midst of
his anguish Devas is shaken with a gust of rage
and jealousy against Follett. In a powerful
scene he accuses the latter of the murder. Just
here the burglar is dragged in. He has confessed.
Maddened at being cheated of his revenge, Devas
shoots at Follett, but his arm is struck up ; and
the curtain falls.
■* * * *
The acting of “ After the Opera ” is up to the
play’s high dramatic level. Miss Ola Humphrey’s
Lady Lulu Devas is in appearance the typical
cosmopolitan “smart” woman that may be seen
at any great European or American restaurant,
any night; and she portrays the neurotic unbal-
anced temperament of a species of that sort of
woman with a sure touch. The Follett of Cyril
Mackay is a skilful creation. In a host of
invidious situations he does not jar once. Henry
Kolker is impressive as Devas ; and the minor
parts are soundly acted. “After the Opera”
will precede “An Englishman’s Home” till
further notice.
** * *
On which subject “D.” adds this note: —
Assuming that “After the Opera” was a Grand Guig-
nol play in its native French, itis interesting to speculate
on the changes it has undergone in the process of being
rendered fit for British consumption. Probably its
original name was “ Aprbs le Bal de l’Op6ra.”
The orchestra plays “Valse Caressante” as the
curtain rises, and Lady Lulu says “The music
has got into my blood,” “ Valse Caressante” is not the
sort of item that is supplied at the opera in Paris,
or elsewhere. It was, however, a few years hack
a regular feature of the “Bal de l’Opbra,” the
“Bal Tabarin,” “Maxims,” the “Rat Mort,” and
other places where fair women and merry men
foregathered. In a Grand Guignol production the
lover would not have successfully resisted the wife’s
blandishments. Modern French playwrights don’t credit
the existence of Josephs in their midst. In the Gallic
version the lover would have “ fallen ” decisively, and
soon. He would have made his escape, in all likelihood,
in an inadequately clothed state. Above all (at the
Grand Guignol), the tragedy would have been
made complete. The Gaul doesn’t believe in spar-
ing the feelings of His audiences. He loves to
send them home in a thoroughly harrowed state.
Therefore in Paris the real murderer would not,
I think, have been discovered. None hut the ragingly-
jealous husband would have dreamed that the unlucky
philanderer were other than blood-guilty. The final
picture would either have shown the Parisian equivalent
of Follett dead, slain in despair by his own hand ; or else
displayed him manacled and on his way to certain death
at the blade of the guillotine. They present these pro-
blems in more ghastly, and also, possibly, more lifelike
fashion in France.

No abatement is discernible in the public
interest that is taken in “Jack and Jill ” and
their doings, at Her Majesty’s, Sydney. The
said interest is almost as overwhelming as the
daily papers averred, a few brief weeks back, that
the (general) enthusiasm on behalf of Mayor
Taylor’s Dreadnought Fund was; and it is in-
finitely more enduring. Jack’s mother will cap-
size backwards out of her rocking-chair, to the
intense gratification of all present, till further
notice.

“ In the Ranks ” is going strong and well
at Sydney Palace. In mitigation of the some-
what harsh words used concerning the production
last week, The Bulletin is fain to state that it is
attractively staged, and, further, that for the many
who like this sort of play it is just the identical
sort of play they will like. The which is attested
by the chronically congested state of the house.
“In the Ranks” is to prevail till Tuesday next,
when a three nights’ run of “A Message from
Mars” will wind up the Allan Hamilton season.
“ The Hypocrites ” intend to put a period
to their loathly doings after Friday next; and in
the place where last they lied and schemed
(Sydney Criterion, to wit) “Lucky Durham ” will
Re presented. “Lucky Durham” is by the late
Wiison Barrett; and it is said by those who have
met it to be replete with thrill, emotionalism and
other pleasing circumstances.

‘ ‘ Akenehi ” writes :
If the Sydney Stage Society could get another Sylvette
as charming and convincing as Miss Madge Mclntosh was
in the Society’s production of “ Fantasticks,” I would
earnestly advise it to give “Fantasticks” a further run.
Nothing brighter, more whimsical or more poetic has
been seen in Sydney. The production of the piece, and
the playing of some of the amateurs (in conjunction with
that of Miss Mclntosh), would have been creditable to the
seasoned actors of the “ Firm ” itself. It would be disas-
trous to entrust the r6le of Sylvette, the Juliet of Rostand’s
delightful joke, to any but an accomplished professional;
but if the Society can snare or borrow one to fit the part,
then I reiterate that it may make some more money by
further performances. No doubt it wants more money.
Every society and person wants more money. Besides,
hardened playgoers, who witnessed last week’s matinee
of “ Fantasticks,” have ever since been strewing Sydney
with notes of admiration, which is the best form of advt.
And all who hear and were not present are asking, in simple
Anglo-Saxon, “ Won’t they do it again?” “Les Roman-
esques,” or “ Fantasticks,” as the translater makes it, sets
out the story of two wily old fathers who conspire that their
respective son and daughter shall marry, and thus throw
down the wall dividing the two properties. To their
children the old men pretend an undying feud, and they
forbid the meeting of youth and maid. But the young-
sters are “ discovered ” one on either side of the wall
making ardent love. Later, hired bravos come to carry
off the girl, but the youth puts the horde to
hired flight with his sword. He is now greater
than Csesar in his damsel’s eyes. The disguises
of the bravos—their enormous hats and cloaks, their
effective posing by their leader and his injunction
to them to keep strict silence whilst he roars a song to
the moon (the moon is an extra in his bill)—the Stage
Society carried out all these things to admiration. The
fathers fall out, betray the plot, the lovers break off their
betrothal for a time, but all ends happily. Even the
bravo’s bill is paid. Miss Mclntosh (in Juliettish hair and
robes) was naturally the success of the piece. Mr. Leslie
Casey, as Percinet, the young lover, had a difficult part,
but lie gob through creditably and looked the pretty boy
all the time. Next to the star as successes were Messrs.
R. B. Orchard and W. E. Vincent, who played the
paternal pair. Mr. Gilhooley (and his moustache that
didn’t and wouldn’t) would have been better for further
rehearsals.

The Amateur Orchestral Society celebrated
its eighteenth birthday season at Sydney
Town Hall last Thursday. It had amongst its
crowded audience the Governor-General, and
unless that potentate is music mad he must have
regretted for the first time his acceptance of the
G.-G’ship of this continent. Schubert’s Sym-
phony in C, which takes an hour’s hard labor to
accomplish, was given as the second half of the
programme, and it came in after an hour of Beet-
hoven, Wagner, etc. His Ex.’s attitude during
the Schubert melody signified either deep artistic
reverence or profound dejection. Miss Muriel
Barton brightened things by raising her mezzo-
soprano in Elizabeth’s prayer from “ Tannhauser.”
«* * *
The weirdly named Bengough commenced
his season at Sydney Centenary Hall on Saturday
night. The Bulletin owns up to a kindly feeling
for Bengough, the little, old, lame humorist
with the cheerful manner and the sweet voice.
His is an old-time entertainment of humorous
songs, recitations, sweet old refrains, and excellent
crayon drawings—the kind that the present
generation knows but little of and takes little in-
terest in. He is supported by Philip Newbury
and Miss Rosina Buckmann, with that charming
pianist, Miss Lilian Delany, as accompanist.
Rosina Buckmann’s voice is a polished and
bevelled shriek, and Philip Newbury, despite all
his excellence, is not the singer he was, or else
this writer is not the hearer he used to be. Beni
gough as a reciter is excellent, and his humorous
stories are well delivered. As a crayon artist
he is decidedly good, and on Saturday
night he produced pictures of J. C. Williamson,
Conductor Slapoffski, and Oom Bill Lyne that
were veritable speaking likenesses. But the bad
acoustic qualities of the hall, and the perpetual
chatter of over- and under-dressed females, who
apparently went to the show to prevent other
people enjoying it, handicapped him enormously.
What is wanted at entertainments of this sort is
a licensed smacker, who should be empowered by
Act of Parliament to come round in burglar shoes
and hit the conversational lady with a dried
eel on the part of her which seems to be
left visible for the purpose. The half-dressed
female would appreciate the force of the smacker’s
remonstrance when nothing else on earth could
impress her. It is probably due to her influence
that this sort of entertainment has gone out of
fashion ; and it is certainly due to her exasperating
presence that most of the men who attempt to
live by it die young, like Mel. B. Spurr.
»* * *
To those who have not yet seen Wirths’
Circus, The Bulletin can only say, with Omar
Khayyam, “Oh, make haste, for the elephant is
on the wing.” The show will give its last Sydney
exhibition on Saturday night, and then away to
the country towns north and north-west. The
most impressive feature, of this combination is
one which does not bring any revenue. Writer
saw it once by accident when taking a short cut
through the slums of Darling Harbor. Down the
long, silent street suddenly appeared a weird pro-
cession of gaily-painted cages, drawn and pushed
by elephants. An occasional roar, a muttering of
confused growlings were the only sounds, and,,
although there were men there, they were not to
be seen. The elephants were the moving spirits
of that procession, and while the writer tried to
think of how many whiskies he had before
missing the last tram, the tail end of the
procession disappeared round a corner. The slum
street was itself again. There are several things
that make Wirths’ a show out of the common.
One is the excellence of its horse-flesh. Also, it
is a show of infinite variety which makes a point
of bringing each time something more reckless and
hair-raising than it did on the previous visit. How
long it will stand the strain of this sort of struggle
is hard to say ; but it must reach the limit some
day, or something will break.

George Carey, who had 40 years’ experi-
ence on the boards in this country, is most favor-
ably remembered by the forebears of the present
generation for his work with the Brough-
Boucicault co, in 1895-96. His best character
performance was as Mr. Watkin, in Sydney
Grundy’s “Sowingthe Wind ”—Mr. Watkin, the
well-meaning, meddlesome, crusty bachelor, whose
advice to his friend, Mr. Brabazon, had turned
out all for the worst, and who was crumpled up
with regret at the finish, when the cynical profli-
gate, Lord Petworth, contemptuously told the old
cronies what fools they were. The Australian
stage has accomplished nothing more effective in
the way of moral teaching than the little address
delivered by Boucieault (as Lord Petworth) to
Titheradge and George Carey—the shrewd, blase,
honest libertine, vicious and unashamed, re-
vealing to the remorseful veterans in virtue the
fact that stupidity is sometimes the worst of sins.
George Carey played any number of respectable
old gentleman parts in his time, but this, doubt-
less, was the only time he had to bow his grey
head in self-abasement before the villain of the
piece. Another notable part was his Mr. Bullamy,
in “The Magistrate,” the last time that Brough,
Boucieault and Titheradge played in it. The late
Herbert Flemming was Bullamy the first time
they played in it.
** * *
Julius Knight has a good, sound addition
to his list in “The Breed of the Treshams,” which
has met with much favor at Adelaide Royal, and
will be a safe card to play anywhere. Knight
marks artistically the evolution of the hero from a
somewhat gentlemanly devil to a somewhat devil-
ish gent’eman. The only blot on the play is that
which marks most of the romances of the younger
writers, and especially of the Americans —a ten-
dency on the part of brave men to talk too much,
to talk cynically and sneeringly, and score cheap
verbal triumphs. The season ended with two
nights of the evergreen “Beaucaire,” in which
this sneering is pushed to extremes ; and
now Adelaide is getting up-to-date in matters
of melodrama. Owing to private reasons which
he found sufficient, Bland Holt kept away from
Adelaide for years, and so it happens that this
city had half-forgotten Walter Baker, and had
never seen Miss Frances Ross at all. Now they
have the good luck to be here for Cup Week, and
are being welcomed warmly. Tom Wootwell is
at the Tivoli, and to the pictures at the Olympia
and the Exhibition is now added a fresh lot at
the Town Hall, where Spencer’s Theatrescope
shows Harry Lauder earning an improbable sum
per week. Even the new Empire has a picture-
fake (it is called a “ miorama ” there), but mostly
it relies on a bustling “all-star” variety show at
popular prices ; and it seems to fill a long-felt
want.
* *
“The Man from Outback,” which is from
the same Australian factory as “The Squatter’s
Daughter,” was produced at Melbourne King’s for
the first time on Saturday. The story it has to
tell centres around a gang of cattle-duffers, who
duff with unwearying garrulity. The theme is
played on one string only. Sydney Winton,
mildly sinister and adorably bearded, is the chief
of the combination, and manages by singularly
intricate conspiracies to divorce a dreary, elderly
gentleman named Maitland from several head of
beef. Maitland possesses the usual historic cos-
tume of an African explorer—a Bilandersonian
heirloom, by the way—as well as a tiring articula-
tion, and a daughter (Mona) of the fashionable
stock size in irreproachableness. Dave Goulburn
flows into the painted perspective as a bloke in lan-
guorouspursuitof work. Daveuniteswith purity of
character the poetical kiss-curls of Roy Redgrave.
He is engaged to reside in the Boundary Hut,
where two former occupants have been corpsed by
some unrevealed brand of violence. Dave’s duty
in the hut is to encompass the ruin of the duffers,
and thereafter he just wallows in trouble. By the
machinations of the duffers, he is found with an
inexplicable stolen horse, and astoundingl.y evades
arrest by the interference of a comic mock police-
man. He is rescued from incineration in a blazing
hut by a segment of a menagerie in the shape of a
horse and hound. Later he struggles with Iron-
bark Jim, a rotund misdemeanant, and just
dodges eternity ; while Jim, borne down by the
dead weight of his own stomach, rolls into the
abyss of a property chasm. In addition, Dave
receives an accidental evening visit from his girl,
and is accused of misdemeanor. Finally, however,
he breaks up the gang, saves everybody, does
everything. Mainly this is due to the comic Bailey.
Roy Redgrave plays Dave Goulburn brightly and
stylishly. Lawrence Dunbar wears a very nice
beard as Sydney Winton—indeed, if there be any
breeding in beards, then Dunbar’s beard is royal.
Ned Duggan is portly and aldermanic as Ironbark
Jim. The part of Mona Maitland is quietly—
rather too quietly —played by Miss Kate Gair.
She appears to be trying for effects by nuances,
whereas, at the King’s, effects are only ob-
tained by elephants. Miss Fanny Erris and
Miss Lillie Bryer are supportable as Daisy
Somerville and Eliza Mac Whopper respect-
ively. Miss Fitzmaurice Gill plays Wild Kate
with a strident, bellowdramatic enunciation and a
wealth of striking attitude in top boots. The
scenery is plentiful and effective.

For one more week at Melbourne Prin-
cess’s, the decorative Irish widow, with an Irish
regiment in attendance, will continue to set the
voice of scandal buzzing in Bath, or in as much of
that city as divides its time between the pump-
room, the barracks and the boudoir. Lady Bab
Flyte has shed a trifle of her superabundant red
eyebrows since the first night, and the frequent
fencing of the interrupted duellists is even brisker
than ever ; also the leading civilian—who is more
warlike than the soldiery, continues to improve on
acquaintance. But “Sweet Kitty Bellairs” is
still in need of compression. The situations are
too prolonged, more especially as one situation is
obviously designed to bring up another. The
play suffers from a slight excess of Nellie
Stewart’s trials and dilemmas; yet, considering
that the house was pretty full on the third Satur-
day night, the sufferings of the play are probably
rather desirable than otherwise.

At Melbourne Royal, the last 12 nights of
“The Girl Behind the Counter” began last
Saturday with another crowded house. The
music of that vague, uncertain comedy has settled
down into its easy lilt, and is going better than at
the beginning. Also the chorus—to call them
choristers would be to bring them before the
general understanding in cassocks and surplices
the chorus folk are walking through their duties
with mechanical precision. They are a tuneful
gathering, and they always nod their heads at the
right moment, or swing their hands rhythmically,
and otherwise conduct themselves with neatness
and intelligence. It is in the first act that the
music goes strongest. A concerted number—
“Shopping”—by the full strength of the rank
and file is a dainty example of melodious
manoeuvres. A Flower Duet between Miss
Lincoln and Harold Thorley fills the air
with violets and other
nosegay ingredients.

Ere The Bulletin’s regretful announce-
ment meets the public eye of Melbourne, there
will have been a change of bill at Her Majesty’s.
“ Dobson’s” has gone out of business, and Diana
has ceased to break the law of the emporium
which forbade her to make audible comments on
the magnificence of her salary. Melbourne has
seen the last of the Dobsonian dormitory, where
the girls cast aside their back-hair and conversed
in their “ petties.” “ Diana of Dobson’s ” was a
charming story of capital and labor, notwith-
standing the fact that it introduced clever Miss
Emma Temple in a couple of appalling apparition
characters—firstly as the respectable virago who
bullied the moulting shop-girls in their lair, and
secondly as the disrespectable old fowl who
roosted on the seat
a-nigh the korfee stall,
and contributed
largely to the humor
of the final act. Be-
cause it was daintily
instructive and wholly
free from conventional
characteristics, the
piece was one of the
lights that fail. Also
the, management, per-
haps, made a mistake
in venturing a little, four-
act, unusual comedy in
the ordinary advertising
way, instead of putting it
forward as a deli-
cate specialty, and
making a short job
of the performance
instead of drag-
ging it out.

At Melbourne Olympia West now reels off
a yard or two of the exploits of a crime-chaser
known as Nick Carter. Nick contrives to rescue
a lone, abducted female by the aid of the bio-
graph and the abductor’s criminal carelessness.
In comparison with a Melbourne trap, he is
a demigod of sagacity. In addition to the exposi-
tions of Nat’s unalarming genius, West also shoots
on to the drapery pictures of “Waterfalls in
Winter” and “The Rope-Making Industry,”
besides a couple of mimodramas.
** * *
At Melbourne Glaciarium “The Cap-
tain’s Downfall” flickers on to the elephant’s
serviette, and proves to be an incoherent and
rather amusing yarn of a sea captain lured to an
opium den by the charms of a substantial siren.
Also the sheet becomes loquacious on “ Cingalese
Village Life,” in which may be seen the suspected
origin of the directoire gown; and it has some-
thing to say on “Caspian Sea Ports” and “A
Peep into Nature’s Realm.”
** * *
At Melbourne Opera House several people
made reappearances last Saturday, and the
Kellys—or should it be Kellies? —happened for
the first time. They are Jack and Miss Kelly.
Jack is commended to notice as being own brother
to the deceased gentlemen whose effigies are old
institutions at the Waxworks across the road.
He wields stockwhips, and lashes a cigarette from
Miss Kelly’s lips. Also he fires a pistol with
unerring aim, and is said to be "an enormous
success everywhere,” his alleged relationship to
Kelly of the Waxworks being a triumph in itself.
Less enormous successes are the nimble Laurels,
Cissie Curlette, Dale and O’Malley, G. W. Hun-
ter, and the Wheelers, and still less bulky is the
success of Charles Faning. The Wheelers, by
the way, deserve favorable mention for their new
baby motor, an excellent little model built by
themselves. The vehicle motes with great
rapidity on its hind legs, and its wild career is an
interesting work of art.
** * *
Albert Norman, after long service as a
gentlemanly villain on the Australian stage, has
struck very bad luck in Melbourne, and is
temporarily down and out. Recovering from a
bad spell of sickness a couple of years ago, he got
back to business for a short time, and then the
disbanding of Bland Holt’s co. saw him out of
harness again. Meanwhile all his savings had
been swamped in a misfortunate hotel venture.
An ailment which renders it impossible for him
to memorise a part keeps him off the boards in
his hour of adversity. The Bulletin holds that
a benefit for Norman would be only a fair thing.

“ 0.K.,” his letter :
Donald Mackinnon, Vida Goldstein, and a small number
of other Melbourne folk have started a mild agitation fora
Repertory Theatre Go. which shall produce a series of the
old classic English plays, and “ some of the more modern
literary dramas ”—in short, plays of the kind which are
caviare to the general, and which no manager can afford
to stage in the ordinary way of business. The would-be
promoters of the Repertory 00. have got J. O. Williamson’s
approval of their ’proposal, so their circular states.
Which, presumably, means that if they can fix up a com-
pany and guarantee the expenses (on a subscription basis)
of occasional performances of high-class works in which
comparatively few people would be interested—at the
outset, anyway—J.O.W. will accommodate the experi-
menters with occasional dates at his theatres, and the
practical assistance necessary to an effective show.
The cultivation of a refined public taste in stage
entertainment is a question of financial concern to
J. 0. Williamson, whereas it is a m itter of merely
personal amusement oi® sentimental aspiration, or both,
with the unprofessional advocates of a Repertory 00. The
production of even moderately “literary” plays is a
losing game in Australia these times. Nothing would
suit the managers better than a brisk public demand for
the “good ’’plays that are either old and free from copy-
right tax, or new and light, as regards the cast of
characters. There are great possibilities of profit in a
brilliant comedy, or comedy drama, that requires only
ten actors, two interior scenes, and a small orchestra—
but they are only possibilities. They no longer resolve
themselves into facts, as in the palmy days of Brough and
Boucieault. A conversational play by Bernard Shaw, an
Ibsen drama, a revival of “ Magda,” or even “ Two Gentle-
men of Verona ” (which can be played by 10 principals and
a few supers) would suit J. C. Williamson a lot better than
“ Jack and Jill,” or “ The Duchess of Dantzig,” if he could
get pantomime runs out of those intellectual entertain-
ments. It goes without saving that he approves of “ a
proposal to provide for the formation of a Repertory
Theatre Oo.” in Melbourne. Who is going to make the
the provision ? The writer would suggest an appeal to
Imperial sentiment. A number of plays that are occa-
sionally staged in the dear Cold Mother Country under
the patronage of the educated few seem to be crying out
for production in Australia. The enthusiastic old gen-
tlemen who were lately so ready with offers of £lO,OOO
towards the cost of Dreadnoughts would do a service to
the Empire by putting up the money for an all-the-year-
round of “ Sbakspearian and Old English Comedy Plays,
etc.,” in Australia. Their sympathy with the proposal is
bound to be immense, and if they will write out cheques
for the amount of it, the Repertory Theatre Co. can at
once proceed to business. After all, the greatest deeds of
patriotism are the privilege of the wealthy.
“ Old Lawyer,” on a Constitutional point:
The determination of the High Court that the Labor
troubles at Broken Hill (N.S.W.) were, owing to their
association with the troubles at Port Pirie (S.A.), matter
for the operation of the Federal Arbitration Act, supports
the opinion that, had serious violence occurred at Broken
Hill, and had the Federal Government so willed, it could
have interposed the military there without waiting for an
invitation from the N.S.W. State Government. The
assumption of Wade’s advisers was, and is, that the State
Government would, in such a case, have to apply for Federal
aid, pleading inability to maintain order, but just as the
Federal Government could have applied its Arbitration
power on its own volition, so it could have exercised
military authority on its own volition. It can exercise
that authority even against the will of the State Govern-
ment. During the time Cleveland was U.S. President he
sent troops to quell labor riots in Texas, though the
Governor of that State protested against the action as an
invasion of State frights. The Constitution Act obliges
the Federal Government to protect a State from domestic
violence, “ on the application of the Executive Govern-
ment of the State,” but when the violence threatens to
involve the peace of another State the Federal Govern-
ment may intervene without waiting for an application.
Its military jurisdiction is in that respect analogous to its
Arbitration jurisdiction.
Victoria has heard of another of ex-
Premier Bent’s irregular practices. The regula-
tions governing the establishment of agricultural
high schools provide that any district desiring a
bucolic academy must find half the cost of erection
and equipment, and at least 20 acres of land. Some
of the districts put up the stipulated sum, but
Wangaratta got a school costing £4OOO, though it
put up only £500; Warrnamhool, in Premier
Murray’s electorate, got a £2500 structure on a
contribution of £6OO ; and Shepparton, in Minister
Graham’s electorate, was given a £5400 edifice,
though its donation totalled less than £lOOO. Of
course, neither Bent, Murray, Graham, nor
Bowser saw anything improper in this—but one
understands why Thomas the Jobber complained
about the ingratitude of members.
The O’Brien family, of Waverley (Sydney),
has scored one win in its fight for legal recognition
of its private cemetery. Waverley council has
been suing the O’Briens for arrears of rates on
the property, and Judge Backhouse has found in
favor of the O’Briens on the ground that there is
no distinction at law between private and public
burial grounds. He also remarked that there is
no statute to prevent the private burying ground
being filled up with deceased O’Briens, and ap-
parently, if the O’Briens liked to include out-
siders, there is nothing to prevent them burying
the general public also at so much a head. If the
decision is good law, which is open to doubt, legis-
lation is needed to deal with private cemeteries.
If the O’Briens are exempt, there is no reason
why any casual landholder should not build a
small mausoleum in the centre of a large estate,
dedicate the surrounding area as a “cemetery,”
and thus escape rates till a rise in values made it
worth his while to revoke the dedication and rush
the property into the market.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“S. Weller”: The stage manager jettisons detail with
pleasing effect betimes. In the Dead wood Dick melo-
drama now in course of perpetration at Her Majesty's
(Melbourne), an opportune blizzard blows along and holds
the stage for some seconds ; but as soon as the bush-
ranger-hero addresses the heroine, the gale realises that
it is only a super, and straightway softens down to a mere
draught. While the wet willy willy ”is doing its worst,
doors and windows open and slam by the aid of too appar-
ent strings, and one pictures the switchboard boy getting
hot and untidy making the “ oil” lamps flicker. Yetinthe
midst of all this atmospheric clamor bravely stands a
lonesome candle that does not flicker a flick! It is this
sort of thing that gives an added joy to serious drama by
providing it with a touch Of the magical or Chung Ding Soo
element.
“ Johanna ”: A fine touch in “ Diana of Dobson’s ”is
the performance of Emma Temple as a sour and severe
forewoman, who threatens to “ repot you to Mr. Dobson
in the morning.” And the downfall of Abdul Hamid
wasn’t more remarkable than the spectacle when that
sour femal retires in disorder and scandalised amaze-
ment at Diana’s reply, “Damn Dobson!” Thus dots
sudden wealth upset a once well-disciplined mind.
“Micawber”: A “ Poverty Point” par. (B. l/4/’O9)
written by me receives a few corrections from Chung Ling
Soo. The magician produced evidence to prove himself the
original performer in England of the present style of
business—a style particularly his own, which has
received many feeble imitations. Soo states :“I never
worked for Foo in my life. I was doing my own original
show' in London when he came over. To advertise him-
self he commenced talking adversely about me. I didtdt
mind that; but when he started to run down my show, I
waded in, and sent him back to China under even time.
I love my life, and spend about 14 hours in every 24 at
the theatre, practising and inventing new material.
I am 48 years of age, and have been in the
business all my life. My wife and I have been
married for more years than we care to count
but I was about 22 when I hitched on. For
many years I wmrked for practically nothing, and
when I did get in the boom I dropped out once with a
dull thud and lost many thousands of pounds. Now' I’ve
got the biggest one-man show in the world. It embraces
12 people, all of whom have been with me for some con-
siderable time. So many different versions of my life
have been given by the various newspapers that I hardly
know myself which is correct. If you are in Liverpool,
they will show' you the house in which I was born. The
sanie in Birmingham and Manchester, and the same in
many other counties and countries.”
Will Dyson’s portraits of actresses—caricature or other-
wise-will perhaps attract a more general interest to his
forthcoming Melb. show of black-and-whites than his
pungent collection of politicians. Sometimes his theatri-
cal sketches made the subjects rather mad, and in other
cases they were accepted with philosophical approval.
Nellie Stewart protested warmly against a portrait that
was intended to be a pleasing advt. if not a flattering
likeness. A caricature of Frances Boss was an awful irri-
tant to the lad}'. On the other hand, Ada Dwyer laughed
gleefully over Will Dyson’s picture of her Mrs. Wiggs (of
“The Cabbage Patch”), and Margaret Anglin confessed
the truth of his conception of her Katharine in “ Taming
of the Shrew.” Miss Anglin’s remark on the subject was
characteristic of that brainy actress. She said the cari-
cature in The Bulletin “confirmed her gravest sus-
picions about herself.”
“ One Who Pays” : Just as Melbourne has been gasping
for a flesh-and-blood drama, there! comes along this
“Diana of Dobson’s.” Depend upon it, this is' the
exact kind of play that has been contemptuously rejected
in thousands by managers. It might go round all
the managers, be even read, and then cast out. The
authoress has no knowledge of stage technique. The
most interesting things are two talks on Economics. A
pamphlet play, but how hard it hits. Imagine a Pinero
with his “ Pish—Pshaw ! ” over the MS. How utterly
absurd are all the four curtains. Fancy ending a
play with a hungry man and woman devouring rolls and
swilling coffee on the Thames Embankmentl A drama-
tist needs no technique at all. He or she has only to
fling a palpitating episode of human life on the stage.
Just a chunk of raw meat will do. But the performers
require technique, as is abundantly evidenced by the fine
acting of Miss Brune, Mrs. Brough, Kingston and all the
others. We will see Pinero’s deftly carpentered “ House
in Order,” and see the nice, orderly wood. And—oh,
rejoice!—there is not one hug in “ Diana of Dobson s.’
Love is not absent, but it is properly and naturally qourte-
ous I thought of the two comedies by Beaumarchais, “The
Barber of Seville ” and “ The Marriage of Figaro.” These
were acted in Paris just as the French Revolutwn was
weltering. Marie Antoinette acted Suzanne, in “The
Marriao-e of Figaro,” at an amateur performance in Ver-
sailles. Said someone, “Are you aware that thjs is a
savage and merciless satire on the upper classes ?
“ What does it matter?” said she, with a laugh. Biting
is “Diana of Dobson’s.” It is like Barrie’s “ Admirable
Crichton,” but his polished dialogue does not tell
like the unadorned Saxon of Cicely Harhilton.
The ideas are quite naked. The moral is Mira-
beau’s “Mankind is divided into workers, beggars
and thieves.” Lord Bridgwater said to Lord Thurlow
in the 18th century, “Ido so love Bath, but
miss my London morning newspaper.” “Damme,
sir,” said Thurlow, “ain’t you aware that these con-
founded newspapers are cutting under all the privileges
of our order?” The free and unconventional drift of a
play like “Diana of Dobson’s” and “An Englishman’s
Home” is derived from M. Antoine’s Theatre Libre in
Paris. A reformer like him finds Othello’s occupation
gone when all the theatres start doing away with that
odious person known as the playwright, as opposed to the
dramatist.

AN EVOLVED APE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A highly civilised ape is being exhibited at the London
Hippodrome. He eats his dinner like a man, finishing up
with a cigarette. He wears a full suit of clothes, has
learned to sew, and, though tie has a valet, is hot above
mending his own underclothing. He can write his name,
and has a bank account, signing his own cheques in a
laudably neat hand. —News Item.
This is a tale of a chimpanzee
That wears apparel like you and me ;
He combs his whiskers and oils his locks,
He curses his collars and darns his socks,
And, though he has mounted the social stair,
Disdains not to cobble his underwear.
Though far from the slums of tatters and dirt
He sings, while he patches, “ The Song of the Shirt. ”
You may see him abroad by his valet bedecked
With the latest in vests and a tie correct;
The twirl of his cane you would ne’er forget,
And the dandy puff of his cigarette.
He can carve his own dinner and pour his wine
And fold his napkin with air divine.
He tips the waiter as well, no doubt,
Receiving his stick ere he saunters out.
He has money, at least so I understand,
And in elegant paw (beg his pardon—hand)
He signs his cheques like a rich nabob,
For this ape’s not one of the common mob.
He has pastimes too, and is partial to sich
Fads as are aped by the idle rich.
For he’s boy and girl, and monkey and man
And savage and angel and Caliban.
For Orthodoxy a difficult nut,
For Evolution a triumph, but
A puzzle no less is that chimpanzee
Who has climbed the boughs of the human tree.
Does he love the bipeds he’s got among
Or the forest folk where he did belong?
Does he never list to the forest call
And sigh for the days before his“ fall ” !
For he then was a monkey simple and pure,
And what he is now, he isn’t quite sure.
A mixture of urchin, and monkey and man,
Of savage and angel and Caliban.
VICTORIA.

SOCIETY
PENFOLD'S
WINES
At the recent annual meeting of Sydney’s
Alfred Hospital, Professor Anderson Stuart,
chairman of directors of the concern, made a few
remarks that give victual for consideration. To
stimulate economy there has been brought about
among the sisters and head nurses a system of
emulation in the saving of drugs and food. Dur-
ing its first year the system reduced the expendi-
ture, by £420, and last year it brought about a
further reduction of £BB. This “emulation ” was
due to the virile brain of Mr. Epps, the secretary ;
and it had been such a success that it has now
been applied to every branch of the establishment.
It has been worked out so carefully that it is now
known which group of nurses eats the most loaves
of bread, and this enables the management
to call for “explanations” from large eaters.
It’s a splendid scheme from the standpoint of
the secretary and treasurer ; but, unfortunately,
there are other things to be considered in the
management of an alleged great hospital than the
saving of expense. (The absolutely certain way
of abolishing expense altogether would be to close
the hospital right up ; and by the time the build-
ing material and land had been sold the balance-
sheet would show a profit.) The lives and com-
fort of the patients and the health and happiness
of the nurses are entitled to as much consideration
as the saving of cost. In fact, there are some
people .who maintain they are of even greater
importance. Under a system which aims
at “bringing about emulation among the
nurses in the way of economy,” and which
requires an explanation from the nurse whose
economy isn’t sufficiently awful, it is only a
question of time and pressure till both patients
and nurses suffer. The staff is a large one, and
where any large number of people are gathered
together there is sure to be at least one criminal
skinflint among them. In fact, there is evidence
that she is in the P.A.H., seeing that this pre-
cious “emulation” saved £420 the first year, £BB
the second, and, judging by a table of monthly
comparisons, is likely to save £7OO additional the
third, mostly in the matter of food and drugs.
These figures would suggest that the criminal
skinflint is making the pace very fast; and in the
face of these disclosures it would be well if, before
passing any item for the subsidy of this concern,
the N.S.W. Assembly carefully analysed its
death rate, and obtained some information about
the working hours and health of the staff.
An Adelaide law court has had to decide
a hard thing. An intending bride sent her trous-
seau to the railway station by carrier ; but when
she ivas a bride, and arrived there herself, the
outfit was missing, and she had to go honeymoon-
lng uncomfortably in “ what she stood up in.”
Most of the goods never transpired again at all,
and the lady sued the carrying firm for their
value. After settling how much that was,
Jjhe Judge tried to settle who should pay.
lne railway, it seems, refuses to give re-
ceipts to carriers, so a custom has arisen
of dumping parcels down in the great barn
tha,t receives them, and drawing the attention of
a clerk at a more-or-less distant counter by point-
ing a thumb in their direction. The Judge said
this was a fool of a custom, and the railways must
invent another ; then he proceeded to place the
loss on the carrying firm, for giving way to it. So
future honeymoons in S.A. may hope to be
attended by all the frilled circumstances that give
them extra value.
Melbourne’s snobocracy, perhaps, was
originally responsible for the foolishness of apply-
ing the title of “Janet, Lady Clarke” to the
widow of baronet William Clarke. When Vic-
toria’s first baronet went the way of all flesh, and
was buried with Masonic-military processional
honors, the mind of Toorak was greatly concerned
with a discussion as to the proper way of dis-
tinguishing the old Mrs. Clarke from the new
Mrs. Clarke, wife of the Rupert who had
succeeded to his late father’s baronetcy. The
local newspapers fanned the burning question,
and out of this conflagration there flamed the
official announcement that the widow was thence-
forth to be styled Janet Lady. The absurdity had
no precedent to go upon as regards baronets’
widows in Britain, where baronetcies are num-
bered by the hundred. Mrs. Clarke and her
worshippers had seemingly got the notion of this
unique title from the fact that a very notorious
old woman used to figure in print as “ Maria,
Marchioness of Ailesbury.” For which there
were good and sufficient reasons, according to the
sacred laws of etiquette. There were at one time
two other—in fact, three other—existing Marchion-
esses of Ailesbury than old Maria aforesaid. And
etiquette rules that whilst the wife of a reigning
marquis is called the marchioness and the widow
of his immediate predecessor is the dowager
marchioness, the surviving widows of any pre-
vious predecessors shall stick their old baptismal
name m front of their old titles, because they have
ceased to be dowagers. Marchionesses and
duchesses are marked for this special distinction.
Widowed countesses, baronesses, viscountesses, or
the ladies of mere courtesy lords are never other
than dowagers, anyhow. As for the widows of
baronets, they just remain as they were. They
don’t even attain the awful dignity of “dowager.”
Janet Lady was lifted about four steps higher
than her dignity.

Quite a lot of persons in authority seem to
have no more initiative than Eve had clothes.
Though Melbourne’s Receiving House, for persons
on the border-line of sanity, has been open for
patients quite a while, O’Callagan’s police just
kept on running-in suspected lunatics, and the
honorary magistracy kept on sending them to gaol
for medical attention. At last, when the J.’sP.
had gaoled a lunatic with £5 in his pocket—gaoled
him on a charge of “no visible” of all things—
the under-secretary forcibly reminded them that a
receiving house was a place for suspected lunatics,
and not a stable or a dog repository, as they might
have supposed. Also, someone lately asked Rail-
ways Minister Billson how it was the Vic-
torian State hadn’t followed N.S.W.’s example,
and tendered for some of the work incidental to
MENTAL ARITHMETIC .
the building of our new destroyers, a lot of
which could be done at the Newport workshops.
The only excuse the tiresome Billson could think
of was that “ the matter had not previously been
brought under his notice.” Doesn’t Billson think
of anything unless it is dragged under his notice?

the local sheet adumbrates mysteriously in the
words “ a convivial gathering.” The town clerk
left the justice hall without a beer-stain on his
escutcheon. Not so Creed. Five shillings were

The Rev. Carr Smith, of Sydney—the
man who said bitterly that he had been a failure—is
getting out of official harness just when a prospect
of obtaining a little more domestic comfort comes
in view. The rectory of St. James’s is
an archaic abode, with the bedrooms
running into one another, and the
internal arrangements belong to
the Dark Ages. If the incumbent
got tired when half way through
the preparation of his discourse,
and wanted to relax his mind by
swinging a cat, there is no
apartment in which he could
whirl the animal round his head
by the tail without it digging
its claws into the wall and
turning upon him to bite his
ear. The premises are situated
in Phillip-street, and are part
of the structure known as
“Starkey’sßuildings.” They
have no yard, and they abut
on to a ginger-beer factory,
where at all hours of the
night horses are heard stamp-
ing, corks popping, and bottles bursting. Arrange-
ments are being made to get new diggings for the
shepherd, but apparently the apostle Smith won’t
stay to enjoy them.
The coming of the taxi-cab gives a hint to
the Melbourne hansom cab-driver that ere long he
will be compelled, like his humbler brother of the
waggonnette, to resort to suburban weddings and
funerals as a means of livelihood. The decadence
of the Melbourne cabman began 40 years ago,
with the introduction of an organised ’bus service.
Great was the indignation of the Jehus of that
period at the invasion of their happy hunting
grounds by the “ painted boxes on wheels.” The
more lawless spirits amongst them did not hesi-
tate to offer personal violence to the new-comers
in the shape of wilful collisions and bombardment
by stones, while harassing prosecutions were in-
stigated against the ’bus-drivers for “plying off
the stand.” Those of the cabmen that had some
common-sense accepted the inevitable, and made
a creditable show of opposition to the ’bus com-
pany. For some years the Royal Mail corner
of Bourke and Swanston streets was the principal
rendezvous of the threepenny lines of cabs that
plied to the various suburbs. Here they were
under the control of a timekeeper, armed with a
cheap American clock and a whistle. But the
coming of the trams in ’B5 Was followed by the
disappearance of the threepenny cab, and the
once familiar “’Wood, ’Wood, ’Wood,” or “’Ld
Til, ’Ld ’lll ” of the touting cabby bound for Col-
lingwood or Emerald Hill, is now no longer heard
in the land.

“ Jeroboam ” writes :
At the annual meeting of the Prince Alfred Hospital,
Professor Anderson Stuart complained bitterß that the
Government had declined to remove the “ general beds ”
from the Coast Hospital to the Prince Alfred Hospital.
The refusal to go on with that proposal—made by Car-
ruthers—is one of the few things that will stand to the
credit of Premier Wade on the Day of Judgment. For
Prince Alfred Hospital is a concern which receives
patients and collects subscriptions under false pretences.
As a matter of fact the place is not so much a hospital as
a clinic—a training establishment attached to the school
of medicine at Sydney University ; and patients are ex-
pected to submit to be mauled about by students in
search of experience. I once took a lady relative there,
and received a bitter complaint that she had been sub-
jected to examination by something like half-a-dozen
students. When protest was made, an official brutally
informed me that I should have taken the lady to
a hospital that was not attached to a training college for
medical students ; and the official was as brutally told
that if he, or any other official, had explained that
patients were to be submitted to indecent assault, the
lady would not have been left there. Prince Alfred hos-
pital authorities do not ask permission; nor do they
explain when patients apply for admission that they will
be expected to act as “ subjects.” And the explanation
given to me was that if this was done there would be no
patients to subject, as women especially would not
enter. At the annual meeting the various speakers
fluted considerably over the good work that was done ;
but the fact that it is a place of treachery to which poor
people are lured on the promise of hospital treatment,
and are then mauled about by students, was never once
mentioned. Also, the fact that women have been ejected
at short notice from the hospital for refusing to sub-
mit to this sort of wholesale inspection, was also care-
fully omitted.

POPULARITY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We meet him sometimes in the bar
The man who is so popular.
His liquor always is three-star ;
He also smokes a good cigar !
He has, sometimes, a motor car,
A joy beyond our means by far!
He shouts ! His purse is globular.
His credit always stands at par !
On his escutcheon is no scar,
While ours, of course, is smeared with tar 1
Alas, how very sad it are
That we are so unpopular !

309-311 GEORGE STREET,
SYDNEY,
The unaccountable death of a month-old
l.aby has resulted in a young couple being com-
mitted in Melbourne on a charge of murder.
Dentist Edgar Wilson, a husband of five months’
standing, dwelt with his wife and the infant—
their first—in Toorak, and while he was away
pulling tusks the wife attended to her housework
and the offspring. On Easter Monday, according
to their narrative, she fed the baby at about
2 30 p.m., then put it to sleep in a clothes basket.
At 3 o’clock the husband returned, and had a bath.
Then the pair indulged in music and singing till
5 p.m., when they bethought them of the infant.
They went and inspected it—and found it
dead. Having considered matters for a space they
left the deceased in the house and trammed
out to North Oarlton to the lady’s mother. She
desired them to go and see a doctor, and discon-
nected herself from the episode. They then rode
out to St. Kilda and looked for the husband’s
father, but he wasn’t in. Next, they visited the
medico who had attended at the birth of the child,
told him of its death, and asked for a certificate.
This he naturally refused, and referred them to
the Coroner. It was then 9.30 p.tn. There was
so far a surprising casualness and unusualness
about the decease of the couple’s first baby, but
the matter became more than casual when the
post mortem, disclosed the fact that the in-
fant had its skull and three ribs broken.
Now a month-old baby could hardly climb out of
the basket by itself, and thus acquire the injuries,
still less could it climb back into the basket after
it was dead ; yet it was found in the basket. The
father and mother allege that they can’t account
for the damages; yet they also admit that they
alone had access to the child. It was a poor
casual sort of baby, and its luck seems to have
been out.
* * * *
The downfall of Abdul the Damned is
another instance of the inability of tyranny to
attract decent men to its cause. Abdul spent
some millions annually on his spy system, and
was supposed to have evolved a whispering
machine and an automatic listener that beat all
records. Yet at the last it seems to have turned
dog on him. Salonika is not very far from Con-
stantinople, and it would appear to be impossible
that a whole army corps could keep its political
opinions secret. Yet Abdul seems to have been
entirely ignorant of the resources behind the
Committee of Union and Progress ; and the only
inference is that his spies had filled him up with
false information, and generally let him in. The
closing scene was a very common one—the
fallen monster deserted by everyone ; his harem
in flight; his servants pillaging his palace; and
himself, without one supporter left, waiting the
coming of his captors ! That sort of thing has
been seen over and over again. Hardly a Plan-
tagenet died but had his disrespected corpse left
to strangers. Shah Jehan’s body was stripped of
its jewelled clothing and left naked on the floor
of his palace. Louis the Well-Beloved could find
hardly anyone to bury him decently. The trouble
is that the tyrant surrounds himself with men
who are willing to sacrifice anyone to their per-
sonal gain, and when the time comes they sacri-
fice the tyrant, and the only reason why he is
buried at all is that it is easier to do that than to
salt him down. Abdul’s gang seems to have
sacrificed him for a solitary fiver a-piece—and he
was not even dead.

An amusing aftermath of the Easter
manoeuvres in Victoria is a controversy as to
whether a certain captain was “ officially dead
when he was killed, or whether he was only
officially stunned or sandbagged and was entitled
to recover? The gallant captain says that he was
never dead, officially or otherwise; in witness
whereof, he is still alive. His opponents allege
that they killed the gallant captain on sundry
occasions; and that against all the rules of the
game of being dead he went on fighting after he
was defunct. It appears that early in the pro-
ceedings the captain’s forces were defeated, and
ruled out of action as officially buried. In fact,
they were disqualified. But notwithstanding
this, the captain, later in the day, attacked a
company of the Scottish Regiment, and was again
routed and killed with great bloodshed, watershed
or cowshed or something. The Highlanders
claimed the victory, but as investigations raised
the idea that the gallant captain had been done to
death on sundry previous occasions, the claim was
disallowed. They couldn’t fight with a corpse.
It seems that you are not allowed to kill a man
more than once at these manoeuvres ; or if you
inadvertently kill him a second time it doesn’t
count, because he can’t be buried twice. But the
gallant captain is evidently the sort of man who
wouldn’t consider himself killed if he was blown
up with dynamite. The military authorities aie
considering the question of taking a coroner with
them next year to sit on the alleged corpses and
keep them dead.

“Landsman” writes :
Re “D.M.W.”(B. 15/4/’O9) and the lost Penguin, a
reference to the Australasian Shipping Register reveals
the fact that she was “ repaired” in 1883, that is when
she was under 20 years old. Since then, 26 years have
elapsed, and the Register, which records lengthenings
and other alterations to vessels, preserves a gloomy
silence on the subject. A careful search through the
same contribution to belles-lettres shows that only one
older passenger-boat is in commission—'the little Storm-
bird (dating from ’54), which runs between Wellington
and Wanganui, and is about the size of some of the Sydney
ferry craft. The Howard Smith’s Derwent, of the same
antiquity as the Penguin, is now, I think, a collier.
Vessels like the Belleisle and Egmont, which are ex-pas-
senger carriers of the Penguin’s age, have been con-
verted into hulks. The Brightside (born in 1865) runs
even to Manly no more. Yet the Penguin plodded on and
incidentally earned dividends. , Was the Penguin painted
and smartened up as a cheap investment? I have
still a mild thirst for enlightenment on the point. “Many
newer boats were far older than she ” is a vague remark
that floats round like a corpse on the tide. Personally, X
should feel safer on a more recent craft than on
“D.M.W.’s” prospective Penguin of 65 years of age.
Among the oldest things on the Register are the Augustus
and Proteus, now lighters, dozing at Geelong, built in
Java before Waterloo, and I presume of teak, which wears
wonderfully; the old whaler Waterwitch (1820), and
the Royal William, cutter, devised in Hobart in 1833.
The Union Company, as part of its sentimental attach-
ment to old vessels, I suppose, has the hulk Gazelle,
whose keel was laid on the M xlabar coast in ’37, and a
scrap of the warship Nelson (1809).

The Sydney Telegraph said
this (21/4/’O9) apropos the squabble that was
raging in its columns at the time between Hughes
and Joe Cook, M’s.P. :
Political feeling in England is probably more intense
than it is with us, and at times becomes altogether too
virulent. But even so, the politician of the House of
Commons, with generations of Chesterfields as an
example, seldom or never forgets the dignity due to his
representative office, and his speech is almost always
carefully considered, cultured, and restrained. This is
an example we might well copy.
Shall I be out of order, and
liable to a charge of unre-
strained and vulgar conduct, if
I condemn the above as hum-
bug, not to say dashed rot ? In
the memory of most of us,
members of this allegedly
“restrained” and “cultured”
House of Commons howled
“Judas” and “traitor” at
one another across the floor of
the House, and then closed and
free fought savagely under the
astounded Speaker’s very nose.
Nothing of that sort has
occurred in the Australian Par-
liament to date. There have
been individual scraps, but the
football scrum in politics is a
different thing. In the cur-
rent London files I note that
L. J. Maxse and Sir John
Brunner, M.P. for Northwich,
have been having a fiscal dis-
cussion. Maxse accuses Brun-
ner of being an “import from
Europe ” (Brunner’s father was
a Swiss parson); Brunner re-
torts by calling Maxse a "liar.”
If our parliamentarians want
to accuse their fiscal opponents
of truth-perverting, they at
any rate usually disguise the
phrase more or less. There is
nothing in the Australian
Parliament to approach the
brutal incivility with which a
certain class of British Tory
M. treats his fellow-member
who is a Laborite. The modern
Labor member—where he is of
the advanced school—is in the
same position socially in the
House of Commons to-day as
the Irish member was when
Parnell was in his prime. In
those days a certain Ulster
M.P., on the frequent occasions
when he had dined over-well,
was wont, as a matter of habit,
to reel to his seat in the House
and hurl at the “Irish Gang”
thedeadliest insults. Yetno one
protested. Even the Irish—as gossiper Lucy notes
approvingly in one of his volumes—took no notice
of their assailant. They held it to be “bad form ”
to blame a man for what he did in his cups.
Apropos, this is one of the few directions in which
our ordinarily well-behaved House may learn a
lesson in decency from abroad. N.S.W,
sends to Melbourne a youthful and ill-
bred Parliamentarian of the Anti-Sosh
persuasion, whose pleasure and pride it is
to “throw off” unceasingly at any political oppo-
nent whom he suspects of being under the weather.
It is a thing that decent-minded men don’t do.
The performance casts no particular reflection on
the perpetrator thereof (on the principle that it is
impossible to dull the appearance of mud), but it
tends to lay the Parliament wherein it occurs open
to the reprobation of the multitude.

A. new method of kissing the Bible has
been introduced at Sydney Central Police Court.
. It has the great advantage of giving a fresh page
to each swearer, but even that will not allay the
suspicions of the germ-fearing witness. Any
book that is handled and licked —even if there is
only one lick to a page—by the usual run of police
court swearers, quickly becomes not alone a
disease-bearer in parts, but insanitary as a whole.
The only method of satisfying the scrupulous
about the use of books in court is to abolish books
for swearing purposes. They do no good in any
case. A man is no more likely to tell the truth
after kissing a book than after kissing a girl—in
fact, if she is a nice girl, the average man would
swear much more reliably on her than on the
book.
In the N.S.W. Government Gazette of
April 21, amid a lot of flummery, much “God
Save the King, ” ,and many proclamations signed
by Acting-Excellency G. B. Simpson, appeared a
somewhat novel notice. It stated that the
Administrator of the Government, with the
advice of the Executive Council, had approved of
a regulation whereunder the Curator of the Aus-
tralian Museum is authorised to impose a fine not
exceeding ss. on any employee guilty of repeated
carelessness or neglect of duty. It is, of course,
necessary to maintain discipline, but the necessity
exists in all other Departments as well as in that of
stuffed birds and hairy dead monkeys. And if
one departmental boss is made a Court of summary
jurisdiction, why not all the bosses ? Then again,
is there any guarantee that the business will stop
at mere fines? May it not develop till the boss is
authorised to sentence his subordinate to im-
prisonment, on bread and water or otherwise, in
the cell attached to the office ? And from that it
seems a natural and logical step to the flogging of
clerks and other officials. The summary jurisdic-
tion which has been given to the chief of the
stuffed bird job is a matter that may lead to a lot
of developments.
Gentlemen who wish to look smart should
pay a visit to Wild’s Hairdressing. Saloon, where
six first-class artists are engaged. The establish-
ment is under Vice-Regal patronage. Address,
30 King Street Arcade (upstairs: entrance from
George or King Streets).*
THE HOUSEHOLD BRIGADE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
What will our readers say when we tell them that our
highly-trained artillerymen are at this moment being
employed in some sort of domestic duty in the household
of the Governor-General? A more utterly foolish use to
which to put our trained gunners one can hardly
imagine. Nor is it rendered less foolish by calling
soldiers who do domestic work “ orderlies.”— Melbourne
Herald, 15//il'o9.
Although the heathen rage and roar clean round Aus-
tralia’s coast,
And bang upon our half-closed door, a wild, unholy host,
Fear not, faint heart —at Guv’meut ’Ouse a steed of pedi-
gree
Is groomed, these days, by Bill and Jim; they’re learn-
ing gunner}'.
The clouds of war may cloak our skies, and yet we do not
care,
For, grafting at the Guv’nor’s place, with neatly-parted
hair,
We see our Bill at saucepan-drill, a bathbrick in his hand,
A tried and well-trained soldier of the Kitchen-gunners’
Band.
When presently we hear, like bees, the live lead over-
t head,
And see around us scattered far our torn and tangled
dead ;
When sinoke and dust and curses fill the atmosphere
with gloom,
And Bill Australia, brave in death, goes shouting- to his
doom,
His Semi-Royal Nibs, who runs the Household-gunners’
class,
May be at hand to cheer them as they bite the dusty
grass.
Yes, some day when our soldiers hear the Monkey’s
opening shot,
His Nibs may be on Southern soil—and then, His Nibs ...
may ... not!
When presently (and soon, mayhap), though Guv’nors
wax and wane,
We hear the threatened thunder-clap, and shut our ears
in vain—
When presently the armored ships come down from Dai
Nippon,
TheG.-G. may be here, and... p’r’aps... he simply. ..
may... be.. . gone.
But, here or not, his household yet will hold the fort for
us,
And from their stable-toil come forth to slay the Monkey-
cuss.
They’ll dust across the cow-reserve, our men of pail and
mop—
But, as they sweep a-down the scene, say, will the Monkey
stop ?
