ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“ Jobber ” : J.D.F. errs when he says
(“Queensland To-day,” B. 15/4/’O9) that “you
never see him [the horse] yoked with a mule or. a
donkey.” In parts of N.Q. dozens of the things
fools back are to be seen pulling cane trucks to
the mills, in company with mules. I'm horse
right through, but at the same time must put in
a word for the mule. As an honest puller who
goes right up to the collar all the time he deserves
recognition.
“Stony ” : On market mornings, between
last and first trains, hundreds of vegetable-laden
carts roll from the gardens of the Brighton dis-
trict, near Melbourne, to lay their verdant offer-
ings at the feet of the metropolis. Later on,
having sold his green goods, Jim Jam loads up
with stable manure, and departs for Brighton once
more. This practice has been followed for many
years. The ARGUS has just awakened to the fact
and has printed a letter about it. The polite
letter-writer refers to the “disgusting practice of
the market-gardeners in bringing from town a
return load of rotting stable manure in the same
carts as they use to carry their garden pro-
duce to market.” The correspondent is also
“convinced that these carts receive no other
washing than that given them by occasional
rain showers.” If the Board of Health cannot
prevent the continuance of such hideous practices,
perhaps a few copies of the Argus, judiciously
distributed among Jim Jam and his cousins, may
have the effect of causing them to see the error of
their unsavory ways. As the Argus has so often
reminded its readers. Private Enterprise merely
requires a polite hint, if irritating conditions
exist, and those conditions will be voluntarily
removed. Therefore, let letter-writing reformers
live in bright hopes of a new manure-arrangement,
even though they die in despair—or of typhoid—
in the meantime.
A look into one of the out-of-the-way
corners of Australia :
Lately wandered over a large part of Kimberley (W. A.),
from Derby to Wyndham, covering 640 miles. On the
Fitzroy River I visited Upper Liveringa (Kimberley Pas-
toral Oo.) and Noon Ranbah (Emanuel and Oo.) while
shearing was in progress. Upper Liveringa was putting
through 110,000 sheep ; 28 white men on the board, with
a white man pressing. Gins acted as rouseabout.s and
picked up ; all the other work Was done by blacks. No
accommodation is provided for the shearers ; they sleep in
tents, here, there and everywhere. At Noon Ranbah the
board consisted of 14 whites on one side and nine blacks
on the other. Half-caste piccaninnies picked up, blacks
rolled, pressed and loaded waggons, while a bevy of fancy
lubras did the yarding. At one station there are 18 half-
caste children, some voung girls budding into woman-
hood. What is the future of these? Only one thing—
prostitution.
After traversing the Fitzroy for 200 hules I crossed the
river at Fitzroy Crossing, where there is a pub, store,
and police station. For 50 miles I passed through magni-
ficent downs country, with Flinders grass and spinifex.
This, properly w'atered, would carry immense herds ; now
it is practically stockless. From here to Hall’s Creek you
pass through 120 miles of likely-looking gold-bearing
country without a habitation all the way. At Hall’s
Creek I went to the old diggings, Brockman and the
Rub}'. At each place there are a few fOssickers. Durack’s
mine at Ruby was putting through a crushing with
a one-stamp battery, employing two men, _ and a
cyanide plant was about to be erected. I Should imagine
the tailings would go more than the crushing.
At various places natives are supposed to be provided
with rations by the police, for whioh they receive Is. per
head a day. t watclied the feeding Of these poor,
miserable, emaciated beings dressed in rags. Each one
carried an old jam or other tin, into which a policeman
dumped some “ bubble bubble,” which is merely paste.
Some received also the bones of a goat. The police at one
place recently bought 160 goats for £3O. And this is what
a paternal Government is paying for.l No tea or anything
else; a piece of tobacco may or may not be given on
Sunday. A nice penny somebody must be making out of
this!
East Kimberley musters about 300,000 cattle, mostly
owned by Copley and Patterson, Emanuel and Co., and
Connor, Doherty and Durack. Copley made a great deal
here. Bought the Ord River property with 45,000 head
at 30S. a head. During the vear sold 7000 head at £lO
per head. Then bought the Sturt and Margaret
properties, with 25.000 at £2. This year they ship 12,000
south. Kidman will have to look out for Copley.
There are some 100.000-acre men in East Kimberley
who do all right. But the cattle-ring holds them and
gives them any price it likes. A beast is bought here for
£5 and sold in Fremantle for £lO. This cattle business is
good biz—for the ring.
“ Combo-ism ”is the order of the day. Everyone (more
or less) has a sable “companion.” On the road to
Wyndham you meet some curious turnouts and see some
funny sights. Ebony damsels in pants and long turkey-
red gowns accompany every conceivable conveyance.
You get Miss Ebony at every meal hour. In fact, the
only topic from jackass to jackass is ebony girl. But
drop the curtain. #
“ Edyson ” : I have just heard from a
youth of Richmond (Melb.), who, in the absence
of a stiddy job at his own game, was per-
suaded to go on the land, young man. He
took a billet with a cocky farmer at
moderate rates and keep. His rail-
way fare, a registry-office fee, and
a trifle of expenses were to be
deducted from his income as
it fell due. On the fifth
day he was put to man-
age a mad harrow agi-
tated by two nags of
incompatible temper.
The horses bolted, and
in the struggle the youth
in charge got his right
hand crushed. While
the injured member was
being done up in a
bundle, the irritated
cocky was putting a
horse in the trap. “Drive him
in to the town,” he said sourly to
Mrs. Cocky, “and bring out an-
other bloke if you can get one.”
The youth was driven in, and
simply dumped off the trap near a
country station, eand left there,
penniless, sick with pain, and
faint from loss of blood. When
found by a humane grocer, he
was lying by the road, almost un-
conscious. He is now a hospital
patient in Melbourne, and is told that he
may not be able to use his hand for six
months. Still, the shocking scarcity of agricultural
labor in the country districts is due to Socialistic
legislation, and that scarcity is ruining our
primary industries, and rapidly driving capital
to the regions that lie around the head waters of
the Amazon.

“ Mallacoota ” : A bushvnan lately showed
me a handy way of keeping a fire alive. We were
going out back from the hut for a three .days’hunt
after cattle. My mate reckoned it would be
pleasant to have the tire burning when we came
home. So he went out with a tomahawk, and cut
a chunk of bark from the south side of a white box
tree. This he covered over carefully with hot
ashes and a few coals. When we came back on
the evening of the third day he scraped away the
covering of ashes, and the box bark was just nice
red coals. Bark on the south side of a tree —the
side furthest from the sun—is thicker and more
sappy than northern bark, therefore best for this
purpose.
If your complaint is incurable you must
bear your sufferings with patience, but if they can
be quickly and permanently cured, why let them
go on? Rheumatism, sciatica, neuralgia, tooth-
ache —all these are among the many ailments that
can be cured by Chamberlain’s Pain Balm, and
since they are most wearying—and Chamberlain’s
Pain Balm only costs Is. 6d. per bottle —why go
on suffering ?*
THE SWAGMAN'S JOKE.
OLD LADY: “I don't think I ever saw a more wretched-looking creature. Why don't you
wash yourself !”
TRAVELLER: "Too, busy, lady—too busy scourin' the country."
“Lad”: Although I long ago learned
from experience that goats are impervious to the
effects of strychnine, I have just discovered that
the same fact applies to the iguana. A friend of
mine—a rabbit trapper —camped on the Tumut
River (N.S.W.), was much troubled with the
visits of a “gohanner” to the commissariat de-
partment of his camp. Besides these losses the
camper one day missed about 20 rabbit skins.
The trapper laid a bait with a mixture of strych-
nine. The “go ” devoured the bait, and came up
for more next day. Again and again the rabbiter
laid the poison for him, but the animal proved
quite strychnine-proof. The trapper then filled a
defunct rabbit with enough of the poison to kill a
bullock, and the animal ate that lot also, and was
around next day with a smile on its countenance.
After that the attempt to poison the “ gohanner ”
was given up. The expense was too great. I
think a fight between a gohanner and a chemist’s
shop would be worth seeing, the chemist’s shop to
have two yards start.

“ M. W.” : Talk about snails for slowness !
The “gum grubs” beat’em bad. Tramping over
the hill-track for my mail the other morning, I
happened on a heap of 60 or 70 of them travelling
from one eucalypt to another. They moved so
slowly that I thought I’d time them. Made a
mark, and on returning found they had crawled
just 14in. in three-quarters of an hour. Perhaps
they might have made better pace singly ; but they
hugged each other all the way like lovers in the
dark. Walking the same track in the evening
I found the heap on a messmate bole, about 6ft.
from the ground. They were tracking slowly up
with much show of exertion. I wondered what
their weight-carryingcapabilities were, so fastened
a box of matches on to the hindmost “grub” with
a postage stamp. They kept going without any
extra show of exertion, so I used up another post-
age stamp and stuck my pipe to the matchbox.
They didn’t even look round to see who was
pulling. I used up fourpence in stamps, and
directly they had my %\ b. plug and a bush-
knife trailing on behind them. Then a bull-ant
said insulting things to me through a break in my
boot, and in the excitement of applying bracken-
root juice and of dislodging another bull from a
loose portion of my pants, I forgot all about the
other'nature study. But I’ve got an idea now
for breeding grubs to haul redgum logs to the
sawmill for me.
“ Wang ” and liis python :
I was reading recently that somebody had found out
how to make spirits out of grass trees. Which served to
remind me of the first time I ever saw one of these trees.
There had been a flood in the Moorabolta Creek, on the
banks of which my boss had his domicile. I had lately
gone through a course of adventure stories, in which
snakes and crocodiles of huge dimensions played promi-
nent parts.
A day or two after the flood had subsided I was stum-
bling along the banks of our Amazon, reading of the ad-
ventures of a party of buccaneers in the wilds of South
America. In the tale pythons 50ft. long occurred at fre-
quent intervals. On turning a bend in the creek I dis-
covered, high and dry on the bank, a huge sinuous object
covered with scales.
I gazed on the awful thing for a moment, and then tore
frantically home to the boss. In answer to his inquiries
re my unusual hurry, I imparted an incomprehensible
narrative, of which a huge scaly reptile formed part.
The boss armed himself with an axe ; I loaded up with a
pick, and then I guided the expedition to the scene of
horror.
Approaching the bend we took off our boots so as not
to disturb the creature. Then we tip-toed cautiously
along with our weapons ready.
From behind a sheltering red-gum I peeped, and saw
that the monster of iniquity hadn’t moved.
I reckoned it was too gorged to move. All the boa-
constrictors on the banks of the Amazon were helpless
when gorged.
I rushed bravely at it, and smote hard with the pick.
The boss seconded my efforts with the axe. The reptile
never even wriggled.
Tnen the boss started to chase me with murder in his
eye. For the scaly object we had attacked was nothing
more than a grass tree that had been washed down from
the forest.
The boss confiscated my stock of adventure yarns that
. night.
THE BEETLES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
From small beginnings mighty structures rise ;
The puling babe becomes the prophet wise,
And microscopic seeds put tendrils out
That, growing, clutch the earth and probe the skies.
So small inventions bring about reforms,
And catchwords oft create politic storms ;
A trumpet-call sets armies on the inarch ;
A phrase cools all a nation’s blood, or warms.
And thus a weed, brought from some Old Land lake
By some romantic wife for old sake’s sake,
Prospering largely in a newer soil
May fierce possession of a province take.
And thus some harmless tenant of the mead
Transported to a virgin land, and freed,
May bear such increase under gentler skies
Till it usurp the land in very deed.
So knew not Jackson, though no dullard he,
And heard the tale of pests from sea to sea ;
Forgot experience bought at countless cost,
Nor calculated what results might be.
He found, while travelling, on an island bare,
A curious beetle, monstrous, weird and rare,
With mandibles of steel that riddled wood
However tough, nor left a shred to spare.
“ I wish,” said Jackson, “ we had some of these
To clear our country of the cursed trees ;
Methinks I’ll take a few and try them there.”
He caught some dozen of the scarabees.
On Jackson’s farm the timber was a curse ;
Slumps, trees and logs ; and all around was worse.
The labor of destroying eucalypts
Was still the ancient problem of the purse.
But now—he set his beetles on the task ;
“Only dead wood, and lots of it, they ask ! ”
He said, and watched them masticate his logs.
He could sit down and see them work, and bask.
First a huge red-gum stump he set them on,
And lo ! within an hour the stump was gone,
And they had started on a grey-box log ;
With huge contentment Jackson’s visage shone.
Within a month, much had their tribe increased,
Nor had their hunger ever flagged or ceased,
Full half the trees on Jackson’s land were gone,
The neighboring blocks he slyly bought, or leased.
They moved, the beetles, in a steady line,
Cleaning up every log that lay supine ;
Not a dry chip was left, nor yet a root;
Of stump or solid tree remained no sign.
One night, as Jackson chuckled in his bed,
He heard the rafters creaking overhead ;
A thousand heads popped through the riddled floor.
He dragged his clothes bn hurriedly, and fled.
Returning just at daylight, Jackson found
His house a seething ruin on the ground ;
The sheds were tottering, and the fences down,
And infinite destruction raged around.
’Twas then he sa\£\that hungry multitude
Ate not alone the'uead and solid wood,
But everything save metal, earth and stone,
All things organic came to them as food.
And, worse and worse, with horror Jackson found
They left some subtle poison in the ground,
For not a seed would sprout, or plant take root;
And barren desolation spread around.
Then with winged feet he to a neighbor sped,
Crying assistance in his growing dread ;
He found the beetles there before himself,
And all the household from its ruin fled.
So the wide ring of desolation grew.
Up to the township on the hill it drew ;
And men, in utter fear, were hurrying back,
While others for some hoped assistance flew.
At length a man of readier wit was found—
“ Dig a wide ditch, and hem the plague-spot round. .
Fill it with burning logs by night and day
And keep the beetles to the stricken ground ! ”
And so men worked as never yet before,
With axe and spade, and kept the flames a-roar ;
The heavy waggons rolled by day and night,
And all the country-side was laboring sore.
As Jackson hid within a hut hard bj r
He watched the battle with a bloodshot eye ;
And ever in his beard he muttered hoarse,
“I thank the Lord that species cannot fly ! ”
At last, by dint of working day and night,
The grimed men triumphed in that arduous fight.
The hungry hordes hissed dying in the flames,
And sudden end o’ertook the sudden blight.
Now Jackson has a little farm out West.
His strenuous life his knotted hands attest.
He grubs and burns the timber, as of old —
And thinks the old laborious methods best.
VICTORIA.
C. G. A. COLLES.

THE SERF.
PARSON : “Are you a freethinker?”
WAYBACK : “ Wall, it's 'cordin' wher ’ t’ missus

“ Wang” muses on strange foods :
The other day I was given a flagon of “bulled tea”—
tea, to wit, that has been warmed up several (not to say
dozens of) times. Somehow the awful flavor of it brought
back to me the days of my dead j-outh, when I used to
devour 'most anything with aviditj'.
I and my companions thought in those happy times
that the seeds of the common marsh-mallows were ex-
cellent provender. We used to call them “bread and
cheese.” Also, we approved the leaves of the flowering
stock. They were hot, and had a remote resemblance to
water-cress.
Wild cherries we scoffed whenever and wherever we
found them.
Another dessert item was the she-oak apple—a cross
between a bitter aloe and a paving-stone. Cranberries,
ripe or otherwise, we punished as though they had been
guilty of crime. The bark of young elm trees we esteemed
a good substitute for chewing-gum. And then there was
wattle gum. Ah, that wattle gum ! Ask any bush kid
his opinion of it. I used to think the heavenly menu
would be incomplete lacking wattle-tree gum. There was
a small tuber we were wont to pull up out of the ground
and eat raw. It had something the flavor of a diseased
onion. We knew it as the “ Chinese onion.” I have fre-
quently eaten samples of all these edibles in the course of
one day—and seen my young friends do the same. For
which reason I think that Nebuchadnezzar will hold no
points as a digestive marvel over the Australian kid when
the Last Trump has sounded and we all sit yarning in
Heaven about the dashed amazing things we did when the
earth was alive.
The Pills Laxo-Tonic have none
To outrival them under the sun.
A permanent cure
They’ll effect, this is sure;
It begins when you’ve taken but one.
LAXO-TONIC PILLS, and Is. 6d.«

“ Cqalopolis ” : Some very remarkable
aboriginal cave-paintings and sculptures exist in
the valley of the Glenelg River, in North-west
Australia, about 60 mile3 inland, and about 20
miles south of Prince Regent’s River, in a very
rugged tract of country. The figures consist of
representations of human heads and bodies, ap-
parently of females, clothed to the armpits, but
all the faces are without any indication of mouths.
The heads are all surrounded with a broad kind of
head-dress or halo, and one of the figures wears a
necklace. The drawings are executed in bright
red on a clear white ground, the clothing marked
with a red pattern, and the broad hat or halo in
some of the figures is done in blue, red and yellow.
The figures are nearly life-size, and the largest is
on the sloping roof of a cave, appearing to look
down upon the visitor. There are also some draw-
ings of kangaroos, far more finished than anything
done by modern aborigines. On the roof
of another cave was found a paint-
ing, 10ft. high, of a figure dressed
in a loose red garment from neck
to ankles, the hands and feet
well executed, and the latter
apparently covered with
shoes. The white face is
mask-like, showing the
eyes only, around which
are circular concentric
bandages, the inner one
yellow, the outer red, look-
ing something like a broad
cap and outer bonnet. On
the upper part of this are five
letters or characters having an
Asiatic aspect. Although poorly
1 executed, these figures all have a re-
fined appearance, quite out of keeping
with Australian nigger art. Very near
one of the caves is to be found on a large
vertical sandstone rock a well-executed
human head hollowed out to about
deep in the centre, the head being 2ft.
in length and 16in. broad. The singu-
larity of it is that it is perfectly European
in type, both in form of head and
features. The only other paintings which
appear to have a similar character are
those discovered by Captain Flinders on
Chasm Island on the north-east coast,
and which have been preserved in a
sketch by an A.R.A. who accompanied
Flinders as artist. These form a long pro-
cession of rude human figures, all appar-
ently clothed in long garments of pale red.
Near the head of the procession is a tall
male figure, with arms out-stretched
and holding a stick, and towards
the middle is an equally tall
female figure. There are. also
some turtles and a swordfish
pretty well drawn. Near the
caves on the Glenelg River are also to be
found two curious large square mounds. These
are formed of loose stones, but are perfect
parallelograms in outline, and are placed due east
and west. They must have been heaped up with
great care, since they are finished to a sharp ridge
with triangular ends, just like the roof of a
building. Both are exactly the same length,
22ift., but they differ somewhat in width and
height.. The third mound was opened, but
nothing was found inside it but a quantity of fine
sand.
“ Itan ” : Bathurst has enough pests of its
own, including the Big Estate Incubus, without
accusing the place of the vegetable burr. It is a
Scotch importation, brought out in the tails of a
pair of Shetland ponies in the ’forties by pastoral-
ists Ford and Cummings. On arrival, the hair of
the animals was matted with the spiny seeds, and
they were combed out and allowed to drop or fly
round anywhere. Now most of Australia knows
and curses the Bathurst burr.
* * * *
“ Argo ” : “ Jimmie Pannikin ” is a more
or less faithful chronicler of the vicissitudes of
Outback, but I have not yet noticed that he has
written anything about the extreme heat. I
therefore hasten to do so. At Birdsville (Queens-
land) last summer I was having dinner with a
friend. The housemaid entered the room, and
was instantly reduced to a heap of ashes. My
friend, noticing my consternation, explained that
it was quite a common occurrence out there, and
unconcernedly ordered the cook to “ Sweep Polly
up—and bring in the pudding.” “Jim-Pan”
may have heard of many such instances.
“ I can sincerely recommend Chamberlain’s
Colic, Cholera and Diarrhoea Remedy to all who
are attacked with bowel complaints,” writes Mr.
George Adams, of Albury, N.S.W., “for it is a
wonderful medicine, and does all that is claimed
for it. Luckily when out in the country a few
days ago, I had a bottle with me, for I met a friend
doubled nearly in two with colic. I only gave him
one dose, yet it effected an immediate cure. I
always keep a bottle of Chamberlain's Colic,
Cholera and Diarrhoea Remedy by rue.”*
“ Mallarapoonie ” about a horror :
We were travelling down a far western river, in the
front end of the Big Drought, with 1200 stores for Her-
gott. Everything went swimmingly, so to speak, until
we struck a 70-mile drj' stage south of the border. A
moonless night on a trackless lygonurn waste forced us
to camp the third night without water. We double-
banked half-night watches that night, as the cattle were
restless and famishing. The first half-night passed rest-
lessly enough, with many deep complaining's from the
herd and disturbed upheavals of beef on camp. The boss
and two other stockmen relieved my watch at midnight,
and soon after we had turned in a soft night wind came
wafting from the sou’-w.est across the miles of lygonurn
flats bearing with it the odor of dankness from some
scarcely dried-up swamp.
Immediately a deep low moan from the famished herd
greeted the gentle breeze, and a thousand horned heads
were lifted to sniff the tainted zephyrs. Then arose the
dull rattle of many cloven hooves slowly on the move,
and presently a stern “ Whoa back, there 1” and the
quick snarl of a stockwhip came out of the moonless
night.
We listened. A sharp order rang across the cattle
camp above the sullen moan of thirsty brutes. Soon fol-
lowed, out of the dust and darkness, sounds significant to
a stockman’s ear —the click-clacking of cloven hooves on the
run ; the strong, quick tramp of a galloping horse ; a volley
of stockwhip cuts ; some curses ; and then a dull, muffled
roar drowning all. The cook sat up in his blankets under
the dray, and listened. “ They’re gone !” he regretfully
remarked. “They’re gone; an’ we’re bagmen on th’
river in th’ mornin’.”
1 gave an order to collar the first available horses, and
follow on, and, jumping on a spare night horse, I lit out
after the receding herd. I came up with the pande-
monium of curses, whip-cracks, and sullen bellowings in
little more than a mile, and sat for a moment on my horse
surveying the scene by the faint starlight. It was a half-
dry gilghi among the lygonurn flats to which the cattle
had rushed in the agony of their thirst. But not a beast
had drunk. That blackguard rush had carried the leaders
in and had whipped the 4ft. of blue silt, slime, and stag-
nation into the consistency of porridge, through which
the herd now moved, goring, trampling, slaughtering,
bellowing in their impotenev, while scores were down in
it, smothering, Writhing, struggling. It was a terrible
fight we fought there against agonised brute force before
we drove them Lack and rounded them on a plain. Dawn
showed a pitiable sight. Some 300 gored and trampled
beasts, some bogged but living, some dead, lay in and
about that sludge hole. It took us till 10 a.m, to haul
out the survivors, with the dray-horses and chains, and
we left 137 great carcases to fester in the sun

“A.H.8.”: It was my first experience
amongst sugar cane. Hence I was greatly sur-
prised to be wakened up at four o’clock in the
morning and told to come and have “ something
to eat.” I never dreamt, let alone imagined, that
sugar-farmers were generous enough to wake a
fellow up in the middle of the night and give him
a feed. And as I sat down to a steaming mug of
tea, a big plate of salt-junk and a loaf of home-
made bread, with a basin of fresh butter in the
distance, I was highly delighted with things in
general. But it killed my enthusiasm stone dead,
and damned the reputation of that sugar-cockv-
fellow for ever when he told m« I would find a
cane knife outside,

VOICES OP THE NIGHT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh, the sweet, the dreamy languor, when the city’s
throbbing clangor
At the chiming strokes of midnight dies away,
O’er my spirit it comes stealing as those solemn strokes
are pealing,
Which proclaim the death and burial of a day.
Ceases now the dreadful clatter, leaving but the soothing
patter
Of the hoof-gear of a slowly pacing “John.”
In the place of noise and vi’lence comes the brain-
refreshing silence
Prom which all nerve-racking elements are gone.
Then I wait the joys of slumber, -when no cares my mind
encumber,
And my dreams are blest with crowds of visions bright.
Peace unfoldeth like a brolly ; then I find my hopes are
folly,
And there come the many voices of the night.
First, the mice behind the skirting start an acrobatic
flirting,
Till they weary of the game and give it best;
Then my next-door neighbor’s bantam, like a shrill,
accusing phantom,
Chucks the burden of his heartache off his chest.
Every soulful choral feline for my garden makes a bee-
line,
There to warble out her anguish to the moon ;
Hour by hour persists their yowling, varied only by the
howling
Of some mongrel to whom death would be a boon.
Hark ! that tiny note that’s shrilling, till the air is all
a-thrilling,
By its sound my restless tossing is increased.
Was there ever music sweeter than the anthem of the
skeeter,
Hastening eager, with his loins girt, to the feast?
Can it be the row is over? Oh, my spirit is in clover.
For I yet may snatch some fragment of repose.
Give me but another minute to get fairly settled in it,
And softly will I sink into a doze.
Fool! The rabble, sharp and urgent, of the flying cow-
juice merchant,
Nips my nascent aspirations in the bud ;
And the early morning paper, ’twixt the door-mat and
the scraper,
Strikes the floor of my verandah with a thud.
How my head and limbs are aching as the new-born day
is breaking,
And the sunlight through the window starts to peep.
I may fling aside the curtain, for ’tig absolutely certain
I’ll not get another blessed wink of sleep.
VICTORIA,
KARAN.

No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MSS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
“ Adelaide ” : It is alleged (B. 25/3/’O9)
that the N.S.W. Bench is “a model in the
matter of propriety to the other Benches of Aus-
tralia ! ” No, not to the Bmich of S. A., which has
nothing to learn in that respect. Nothing what-
ever ! (World’s competition invited, and color
line not drawn.) It is simply impossible to be
more proper than Chief Justice Way, or Justices
Boucaut and Bundey, who sat with him as a
Bench, unbroken, for 20 years—another record ;
or than Gordon and Homburg, who have latterly
been made Judges. True, Gordon was, while at
the Bar, probably the finest poker-player in Ade-
laide—if there is any harm in that; but even that
mild dissipation he gave up on ascending the
Bench, just as a divinity student may give up
dancing on taking Orders, and for the same
reason. No. As Ichabod Bronson says, in the
Field of Moral Endeavor, no Competitor can Shake
a Stick at us.
THE DREADNAUGHT NIGHTMARE.
He bad already taken part in four Dreadnought meet-
ings in N.S.W., and they went with a swing.— BRUCE
SMITH.
After-effects of a niglit-out with B. Smiths, Laud
Mayors, and a few title-hunting citizens.
Enabling, of the Vic. Upper House, lately
told his fellow members of P’rnu Council that his
grandfather had been the originator of the Eight
Hours inovement, but his father had been a Con-
servative. Thus it takes three generations to run
the gamut of positive, comparative and superla-
tive—Democrat, Tory and Rich Landlord.
“Lantana”: Alderman Thomas Glissan,
Mayor of Ulmarra, is turning his robes of office
out to grass this month. He has worn the gaudy
rags for 16 years without a break —probably a
record for N.S. W. Everyone is sorry Thomas is
going. But he is firm. It is understood that he
fears if he doesn’t desist resolutely and at once
from being a mayor he will get to look like one
about the face, a contingency to be dreaded by
any man with a sense of the ridiculous.
Mr. Marmaduke Webster, who died re-
cently at Orange (N.S.W.), bad seen some life
in Australia. As a youth he came to Mel-
bourne in 1853, attracted, like many others,
by the gold discoveries. He made his way
to Bendigo, and for 20 years there was not a
rush of any note at which Marmaduke was
not present. “Old Yorkey,” as he was
called, held at various times interests in
many valuable claims. Eight years of his
life were spent in. Maoriland, and while
there he took a hand under the ad venturer
Von Tempsky against the Maoris. Return-
ing to Australia he tried his luck at Mt.
Wheeler (Q.) and Gulgong (N.S.W.). His
last gold-seeking was at the disastrous Mount
Browne (N.S.W.) rush, and on the track to
that remote horror he and a companion
narrowly escaped death from thirst. When
he returned from Mount Browne he gave up
gold chasing and settled at Orange, where he
resumed his ancient and peaceful avocation
of carpentering.
A. A. Dangar, one of N.S. W.’s £lO,OOO
Dreadnoughters, supplied to the S. M.
Herald (30/4/’O9) a column-long letter which
should be nailed in a prominent position
in every bush school as a horrible example of
how not to write the English language. A
sample sentence :
Following on New Zealand’s offer of a Dreadnought,
the idea in the first instance in this State was that the
Commonwealth Government of Australia should offer a
battleship ; that is, that the six States should send suffi-
cient money to the- British Government, say, £2,000,009,
at least, to build one, and if that had been acted upon at
once with decision I believe the money would have been
readily forthcoming, mainly through private subscrip-
tions, all that was wanted being an energetic lead from
the Governments, when the bulk of the people of the States
would have responded, but Mr. Fisher said “No.”
And again :
Mr. Wade hestitated (for valid reasons well explained
in the metropolitan papers of 3rd March), and now, after
much loud talk, we are in a fair way of belittling our-
selves in the eyes of the world, whereas if it had been
otherwise, and even without Canada, Africa, and India
following suit, and each presenting a Dreadnought (this
very possibly might have come about), the moral effect
of such an action would have been enormous, and simi-
larly the best insurance fund that could have been
effected for us.
A. A. Dangar, anti-Socialist, is a living instance
of the disadvantages of his own doctrine. Had
N.S. W.’s Socialistic State schools been in going
order in his youth, he would perforce have had
the basic principles of English grammar ham-
mered into him, to his own greater glory and the
comfort of those luckless editors on whom his
illiterate efforts are at present unloaded.
By the Oldest Inhabitant: —
Janet Mrs. Clarke, who died in Melbourne last week,
was the second wife of the first baronet, William John
Clarke, and William John was a son of William John
Turner Clarke—known in the ’fifties as Big Clarke—who
owned landed property in all the States. The deceased
lady was the daughter of Peter Snodgrass, a pioneer of
Port Phillip, who was a member of the first Parliament
elected after separation from New South Wales. Kilmore
was the constituency which he represented. He was
elected Chairman of Committees in 1853. Peter was the
son of Colonel Kenneth Snodgrass, who led the “ forlorn
hope” of the 52nd Regiment at the storming of San
Sebastian, and who was acting-Governor of Van Diemen’s
Land between the departure of Colonel Arthur and the
arrival of Governor John Franklin. He occupied a
similar position in N. S. Wales between the departure of
Governor Bourke and the arrival of Governor Gipps. The
Colonel died at his home at Raymond Terrace in October,
1853. His old mansion on the corner of Kent and
Bathurst streets, Sydney, still stands.
Captain Cook, whose call in at Kurnell,
near Sydney, has again been the subject of the
usual weary annual celebration,
left his widow fairly well to do.
Mrs. Cook passed the balance of
her life in a cottage at Clayton,
near London. She devoted some
strands of a notably luxuriant head
of hair to the making of a watch-
guard, a very common vanity of
the period, and a jeweller—J.
Farrier, of London—applied the
finishing touches. That watehguard
Mrs. Cook presented to Mrs. Elliot-
son, the wife of Dr. Elliotson, a
neighbor, and Mrs. Elliotson passed
it on to her daughter, who carried
it to Australia. It descended from
her to Mr. A. C. Curlewis, of Mel-
bourne, who passed it on to Mr.
H. R. Curlewis, of Stanmore,
(N.S.W.), and he finally passed it
into Sydney Museum, where it
now rests. It is only an indirect
sort of a Cook relic, but it is
more interesting than Cocky Car-
ruthers’ weary clatter about the
deceased mariner.
Again the Oldest Inhabitant: —
Mr. J. T. Wilshire, of Neutral Bay, Sydney, well known
in philanthropic circles, died last week, aged 72. He was
a Sydney native, and the son of a Sydney native. His
grandfather was James Wilshire, who came out as com-
missary’s clerk early in the last century, and in 1805
wedded Miss Esther Pitt, of the early Hawkesbury Pitt
family. Leaving the commissariat, he established a
tannery on Brickfield Hill, the business being continued
by his sons right up to the ’sixties. Mr. J. R. Wilshire,
the second son of the commissary, was one of the first
aldermen of Sydney, and was second mayor of the city.
He also represented Sydney in the first Parliament under
Responsible Government, having as colleagues Robert
Campbell, Charles Cowper and Henry Parkes. The latter
resigned early, and was succeeded by W. B. Dailey. In
the late ’fifties Mr. Wilshire had a seat in the Legislative
Council. He died in 1860. While mayor he gave a fancy
dress ball in the Victoria Theatre to 700 guests, the first
of its kind in Australia. The recently-deceased Mr. Wil-
shire at one time represented Canterbury in the Legisla-
tive Assembly, his colleagues being J. H. Carruthers,
“1.0.G.T.” Hutchison, and “L.0.L.” Wheeler. Two
children of James Wilshire and Esther Pitt, his wife, still
live —a son, born in 1822, and a daughter, who dates from
1824.
Melbourne possesses a quaint creature in
the shape of one Edith Turnbull, an elderly lady
whose anger vents itself on windows. If Edith
happens to have looked on the foaming brown wet-
ness of the beer, or feels out of sorts, or if anybody
reflects on her appearance or her costume, she
indulges in no unseemly epithets or coarse,
jocookly abuse. She doesn’t even hit her derider.
She just wrecks a window. Edith was run in the
other day, charged with her ninth known window,
and decided to take it out. It was the ninth time
she had taken it out.
“AN ENGLISHMANS HOME."
Victoria’s Eureka veterans are rapidly
passing in their checks. Jim Heffernan submitted
his documents the other day, aged 81. He was
one of the first on the spot after the robbing of
the Mclvor gold escort near Heathcote, and
assisted at the big demonstration in Bendigo,
when the miners burnt their licenses and defied
authority. He fought in the Stockade, and beat
Hotham’s myrmidons in a subsequent three days’
scoot for liberty. He was long a Kyneton cele-
brity, and, like many of the Eureka men who did
not go in for politics, he ran a hotel and published
good liquor thereat.
The deceased Archbishop Snath didn’t
spend his whole time fighting with cabmen. One
matter which he attended to with a lynx-like eye
related to marriages between Australians and
foreigners. He interviewed foreign consuls per-
sonally when a marriage of mixed nationalities
was pending, and directed his clergy to scrupu-
lously fulfil all the conditions which foreign law
imposed. There are many ways in which the
fickle alien may marry one of
British birth, and yet, in the eye
of his own country’s law, remain
unmarried and free. For instance,
if a Belgian espouses an Austra-
lian and goes back to Belgium,
either with or without his bride,
a record of the ceremony is re-
quired to be made in Belgium
within three months after his re-
turn, otherwise the marriage is
illegal. French law provides that
a French subject cannot contract
marriage without having pre-
viously published in France the
notice of his or her intention, and
it raises elaborate trouble about
consent of parents. Similar enact-
ments exist in respect of the
marriage laws of Argentina, Aus-
tria, Brazil, Chili, Denmark,
Mexico, Portugal, Russia, Spain,
Sweden, and other places. In
England, at one time, the multi-
tudinous foreigner who repudi-
ated his English wife amounted
to a scandal. There is no doubt
the departed Primate did much to check that sort
of thing in Australia.
“Vigilant” writes:—
Donald Mackinnon, who is being boomed just now as
the rising hope of the Victorian Liberals, is compara-
tively a novice in politics, for he only entered the As-
sembly in 1900. He is a lawyer by trade, a Conservative
by nature, and a big squatter by inheritance; so there
isn’t much room left for Liberalism. As a matter of fact,
Mackiunon’s liberalism is as mythical as G. Keid’s anti-
Sosh. He is a greater trimmer than Swinburne, and is
the eminent “Liberal” leader of a solid party of one.
As a lawyer Mackinnon has more claims to recognition.
He did good work as coadjutor to the late George
Higinbotham (afterwards Chief Justice) in the consolida-
tion of the Victorian statute law in 1889, and his services
were recognised bj' Parliament. Donald was born just
50 years ago, in the Western District of Victoria, where
his father was one of the first to take up pastoral areas.
He married a daughter of the late Judge Binney in 1891,
and there is a large family of small Mackinnons.
A cablegram from Turkey says that it has
been decided not to put ex-Sultan Abdul Hamid
on trial. It would be a dreadfully superfluous
thing to do in any case. So colossal and notorious
a murderer, with 33 years’ accumulation of dead
bodies around him, hardly requires trial. Hang-
ing is the only event his case calls for.
“D.” describes some lawyers:—
It would be hard to conceive two men more unlike than
the Bar leaders who wrestled at Albury (N.S.VV.) last
week over the fate of the awful Mann. Lamb is tall,
pale, clean-shaven, ascetic-looking. Gannon is short,
rubicund, moustachiod, and a bon viveur to the finger-
tips. Lamb is cold, acidly-logical, and precise as to his
mind. He possesses the deliberate ratiocinative intellect
that is to be looked for in one whose position is assured
among the half-dozen finest card-players in Australia.
He disdains oratorical fireworks. If he can’t drive his
point home by solid argument, he lets it go at that, and
retires to the fastnesses of his chambers with a pro-
found pity for the jury’s brain-capacity. Gannon
is eager, vital, flamboyant, unconventional, full of
racy humor, a master of appeal to the feelings
of the good men and true in whose hands lie his client’s
fortunes. Lamb is a lawyer of a high order. Gannon
doesn’t, specialise in this line. He has an invaluable gift
for picking up the law of a case in the shortest possible
time from another ; but he won’t delve it out for him-
self. Lamb is not a gregarious animal. He doesn’t
fraternise with solicitors as such. Briefs took a long
while coming his way, solely owing to his unconquerable
hatred of fore-gathering with potential brief-givers whom
he didn’t fancy as intimates. His aloof temperament in
a community where Bar-work goes largely by favor has
ever militated against his banking account. Now
he is in the satisfactory position that Gentlemen
One have to brief him, though they may never
have revelled a revel with him in their lives.
“Jim” Gannon would get enough work and to spare
if he were far less competent than he is. Everybody
knows and likes him, and he knows everybody. There is
no man at the Bar, with the exception of G. H. Reid, who
can take more liberties with Judges. Even Darley, 0.J.,
was wont to unbend and smile when Gannon jested. It
is not Lamb’s way to crack jokes in the execution of his
duty. To him, the loud cachinnrtion of the unthinking
juryman, usher, or what not, is utterly revolting. In
two things alone do these counsel resemble each other.
Each is a fine speaker in his own style, and they are both
keen racing men. Gannon is an owner and Lamb is an
A.J.C. steward and committeeman.
“ Miles ” : A fresh edition is just out, and
to hand, of W. Jerrold’s life of Red vers (or
Revers’d) Buller. This gladiator was a rich man
ever since 1874. owner of the fine estate of
Downes, at Crediton, Devonshire. - Yet he drew
his pay for all he was worth, and fought
through 10 campaigns in three continents. Little
Melancthon Burton Spurr (Mel. B.) used to say
that Buller was thought more of in South Africa
than any other British general. The most popu-
lar song after the war was “Cheer up, Buller,
My Lad ! ” His career must be read in connec-
tion with the feud between Wolselev and Roberts,
like that between Fisher and Beresford. When
two angry lions roar, along Afric’s sunny shore,
the second votes the first a bore, vide
Duffy and O’Shanassy. Roberts was stung
by Wolseley being called the Only Gen-
eral. Wolseley and Buller were the keenest
chums, though utterly different. Wolseley was
the typical French officer, while the grim, stolid
Buller was the German sort. His services in-
cluded India, the Anglo-French Expedition to
Pekin, Canadian Rebellion, Ashanti War, Kaffir
War, Zulu War, first Boer War, Tel-el-Kebir,
Suakin, and Khartoum (Gordon relief expedition).
He would probably have been Coinmander-in-
Chief if the Rosebery Cabinet had stopped in a
few days longer. As it was, he rose to all the
other best posts. He was with Evelyn Wood
when Carey came up with news of the Prince
Imperial’s death, and he foretold that Colley
would meet with disaster.
“ The Undersigned” writes :
Melbourne knows its lone Scandinavian-looking pave-
ment traveller, who has figured more than once in Thk
Bulletin columns. This is not about him. Another
oddity appeared some months ago, apparently of the
same nationality. His lurk is a standing one. For an
hour or two every morning he stands on what has come
to be called the Swanston-street Bridge, opposite the
eastern entrance to Flinders-street station. He is always on
the same identical spot of ground, and he looks intently up
Swanston-street. He has changed his appearance several
times. First he wore a moustache and full beard, the
latter trimmed square. Later, the beard was clipped to
a point, in the manner which we used to call a la Boulan-
ger ; then closer, after the fashion affected by fat-faced
men of the present. The beard next disappeared, and he let
the side whiskers grow Ala Franz Josef. He clipped them
back to sideboards, and trimmed the moustache right
back. Now the remainder of the moustache has been
removed, and he wears just the small sideboards such as
I cultivated when I was rising 18. When he first came
under my notice by reason of his attachment to his
pitch, I took it that he was a tramway man, perhaps on
the “ spot.” But he would soon become too well known
for this theory to hold. Then I thought he must be a
pressman, counting the number of cyclists crossing
Princes Bridge in a given time, as the basis of a
par. But, from the time he has spent on his
lookout, that would not be a payable proposition.
The railway men about the station corner and the police-
men on point duty know nothing about him. He looks
as if he might be anything, from a professional chess-
player to an ex-lighthouse hand. Possibly he is the hero
of an Anderson mellow-scream, just released after
serving a long sentence for the crime he didn’t commit,,
and is now lying in wait for the fashionable villain, Damon
Puff, who put him away and then fled to Footscray with
his best deceived girl.
THE IMPORTING CRAZE a GAIN .
The authorities of the Anglican Church propose to ob-
tain the new Archbishop from England. —Daily Payer.
In the contest for the seat in South Aus-
tralia’s Uppish House, which Agent-General
Kirkpatrick has resigned, there are two con-
testants —Theodore Bruce, the much defeated, a
member of the Tramways Trust, and A. W.
Styles, secretary of the Tramways Employees
Association. Styles is Mayor of Thebarton, and
has considerable municipal experience ; he is also
secretary to the Federated Butchers’ Union.
Bruce has been Mayor of Unley, but at present is
out of a municipal job. He has often tried to
push Tom Brice out of politics, but, so far, has
failed. He is like so many more of S. A. s National
Ass. nominees, an auctioneer. Auctioneering is
the unproductive sort of job which appeals to the
National Ass.
A TALE OF AN “AD.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Now Benjy M'Bingy, my hero, had lashins of loot to his
name,
But Loot, by itself could not plenish the soul that was
greedy for Fame ;
'Twas Fame—ever Fame—that Meßingy was yearning to
glean for himself—
But a Fame he was anxious to garner without any wasting
of Pelf.
But all of a sudden the country (or part of it) burst into
flame,'
That Deutschland (whose Kaiser is Wilhelm) should dare
take a hand in the game.
The game that Bull played with such profit—the game
that means Boss of tiie Sea,
The game that brought most of us hither and made us a
Land of the Free.
And lo—through the papers one morning we learned that
our Bengy Mcß.
Had planked down a cheque for ten thousand —a princely
munificent fee,
So that we should hustle the builders and raise up a war-
ship or two,
A gift to old Britain from children whose bosoms were
bulky and true.
And patriots whose acres were many, whose waistcoats
were noble and white,
They went in the wake of Meßingy, and threw down
their stuff in the sight
Of men who went crazy with wonder, of papers that
bellowed afar :
“Behold the largesse of our noblest, and see what a
nation we are ! ”
And as the months passed, and subscribers gr&w weary
of sending their tin,
We calmly sat down, and we reckoned how much the
big fund had got in,
And learned, to our great satisfaction, our land had a
stiff upper lip,
For the cash we had got would suffice for— one-fortieth
part of a ship.
So we sent back Meßingy his dollars, and guessed how
Meßingy would smile,
That youth who was yearning for honor—that Hebrew
without any guile,
And never a vessel we builded to help the land over the
sea,
But when the next Birthday was on us—his name was
Sir Bengy Mcß.
And as to the dangers of Britain—we’re hoping she’ll
lend us a loan,
So that her dear children may raise her a bouncing big ,
boat of her own ;
And as to Sir Benjy—he’s famous; his soul is exalted
and glad—
O GREAT IS THE VIRTUE OF PUFFING, AND MIGHTY THE POWER
OF THE AD. ! ! !

The
WILD CAT
COLUMN
Premier Joseph Ward, of Maoriland, has made
a statement which is calculated to cause the Dominion to
st up and think hard. He says that the revenue lor the
past year was £9,001,185, and the expenditure £8,785,513.
That means that the surplus is down to £215,672, and
considering the number of items of ordinary expenditure
which Maoriland debits to loan account, its annual sur-
plus requires to be about £700,000 to keep things even
reasonably square. Dor the year which ended March,
1908, the paper surplus of Maoriland was over £BOO,OOO,
so the paper or parchment or bogus surplus of Ward’s
islands has apparently dropped about £600,000 inside 12
months. Seeing that even with a paper or delusive or
sham surplus of £SOO,OOO the debt increased, and the
inability of the allegedly reproductive public works of
Maoriland to pay interest on the debt increased,
and as a consequence the burden of taxation
increased, the prospects of Maoriland, with only
£215,672 of paper surplus, are gloomy. In this
respect some remarks made by Premier Ward are
significant. Maoriland has, for a good many years past,
borrowed steadily and persistently about as much money
as it could lay its hands on. It has borrowed in London
and Sydney and Melbourne. It has borrowed whatever
small sums it could obtain locally. It would have bor-
rowed in Parramatta or Geelong if it could have found
money there. It would have borrowed in Hell if such a
thing were possible. And now Premier Joseph Ward has
spoken severely to the local banks because they haven’t
enough money waiting to be borrowed. He says that he
“has come to the conclusion that the bank-
ing institutions are unable to afford sufficient
facilities to the community owing to the in-
crease in land settlement and trade; it will
therefore be necessary for the existing banks to largely
increase their available funds, or other banks will be
allowed to enter into competition.” That seems to repre-
sent Joseph Ward’s last frantic attempt to keep the loan
money boqm going. Maoriland, under his insensate and
furious guidance, can’t keep afloat even by its tremendous
public borrowings, so Joseph urges the banks to find
more money somewhere or anywhere or in any old place,
and lend it out on Maoriland’s inflated property values;
and if the banks don’t dig up the money from one place
or another he threatens them with competition. Where-
he proposes to find the competition he doesn’t say.
At present there are doing business in Maoriland the
Bank of N.Z., the National Bank of N.Z., the Bank
of N.S. W., the Union Bank of Australia, and the
Bank of Australasia. The only possible reason that can
be assigned why the Commercial of Australia, Commercial
of Sydney, the two Koj’als (of Melbourne and Brisbane),
the Colonial, National, and Victoria, of Melbourne, the
Adelaide, the Westralian, the two Tasmanian banks, and
some of the smaller institutions don’t open offices in
Maoriland is that they don’t think the business would
pay. Probably they think that values are so badly in-
flated through the borrowing policy of Joseph Ward and
other people that Maoriland is a good place to keep out
of. It is all very good for Ward to threaten to bring in
these or other banks to dissipate their cash in advances
on the loan-inflated property values of Maoriland, but the
banks might refuse to come. Most of them have, at one
time or another, struck booms and bursts in their own
countries. They may not want to come in at the begin-
ning of a burst and the horrible tail end of a boom in
Ward’s country.

This bank is going ahead very carefully. In his speech
at the ‘half-yearly meeting, the chairman excused the
slightly-shrunken profits in these words: “The profits
do not perhaps show the increase that could be wished
for, but that can be largely accounted for by the fact
that our advances have been somewhat limited, chiefly
because of the improved financial position of many of the
bank’s constituents who have not required so much
assistance recently, and because we have refrained in
many instances from lending on what in some agricul-
tural districts may also be termed boom values.”, Evi-
dently the management does not think the gilt on
Queensland’s gingerbread is all 18-carat. One of the
results of this policy may be seen in the following table

able precaution has been taken.

Slackness of trade is the very reasonable excuse advanced
by the management for the depressing- result of six
months’ toil. The charging of a certain amount (not
specified) of the cost of development work direct to work-
ing cost of the colliery has also been a contributing
factor. The North Side, which was sealed up in Septem-
ber, ’OB, in consequence of the old fire area showing signs
of being troublesome again, was opened up in January of
this year, and found cool enough to be workable. It is
now once more in going order, and the management
estimate that it will increase the mine’s 300-ton daily
output by 100 tons—“ when trade improves.” The de-
benture debt is now £2500 ; also the co. owes its bankers
£4016, and sundry creditors £lOO5. Against these items
it 'has book debts amounting to £767. After doing a wise
and good financial deed last term by writing off £906 of
flotation and preliminary exes, out of £2245 of profits, it
is hard lines to be hit by a dull season and a moderately
large Joss., Among coal mines, the Heddon-Greta is a
sort of smudgy Job, who is afflicted with fires instead of
boils.

The world continues to go very well with this concern.
Although the operation of the Early Closing Act and a
further concession to the Council are said to have re-
duced the consumption of gas in shops and for street-
lighting purposes, the sales of “ gas, residuals,” etc., are
set down this year as £10.078 ; last year the receipts
from this source totalled £8752—0r £1326 less than for
the term just ended. However, by way of further gild-
ing the refined dividend, the management is encourag-
ing the Townsville housewife to use gas-cookers, and the
Townsville housewife taking kindly to the notion, the
clank of the penny meter is beginning to be heard with a
cheering frequency. The new idea is expected to return
a considerable profit when it gets going properly. The
item, “ Freehold property, plant and mains,” has moved
up during the year from £26,423 to £27,99s—which is a
jump of £1572; also, £lOOO has been written off plant.
There is a credit balance at the bank of £127; against
this, sundry creditors amount to £249. The accounts
are not as explicit as they were in March, 1907, and, as a
rule, one line of figures has to do the work which was
formerly divided amongst four. This accounting reti-
cence seems to overtake some cos. in the days of their
bounding prosperity, and it inevitably suggests the small
boy who has become possessed of an extra large slice of
plum cake,and desires to avoid publicity until the rich-
ness is safely .stowed. •

The report states that ten new waggons and a number of
new skips have been added to the plant; it also breaks
the news officially that a new boiler and electric plant
have been ordered and that coal-cutting machines are
being installed. From these preparations it can be
gauged that the co. has a confident eye fixed on the
future : and the future has never yet wholly turned dog
on the Wickham. Since 1905, when the original mine
was worked out and the Cessnock property secured, luck
has dog’ged' this co. even as a hungry hound follows a man
with a parcel of sausages. Most mines consider them-
selves fortunate if they have one life; but, after a long,
profitable—and improvident—existence, this one Is
gradually fighting its way back to the firing line. There
is a long stringof lean accounts between the 12J Pref. and
6 per cent. Ordinary dividends of 1901; but, when one
contrasts the position of the co. to-day with its pre-
carious foothold at (sa.v) March, 1905, it is obvious that
Providence is occasionally good to coal mines. The fact
that the old mine was allowed to die with its book value
still standing in the accounts, means that there is a dead
horse of great size to work off, but the following figures
show that a considerable reduction is being made in the
animal:—

As will be seen, the debts have increased by £8225 in the

£22,233. The profits for the half-year ending March, ’99,
were £343, which is about half the amount for the term
just ended. The Tasmanian Loan is a well-seasoned
institution ; it has had a fairly long- start, and it travels
on a moderately even keel; if Tasmania were a pro-
gressive country it would probably be a large, bustling
affair by this time ; as things are, it seems to get along
at an extremely gentle jog-trot.

The riddle of Mort’s balance-sheet is the reserve fund,
which moves about without any explanation. As stated,
the net profits on the half-year are £6596; but, in the
above table, the unexplained additions to the reserve
since June, ’OB, amounting to £1730, are added thereto,
which makes £8326. For the Dec. ’O7-June, ’OB term,
these unexplained additions amounted to £3309 ; this
whs, however, an exceptionally large increase. Pre-
sumably they are governed by the final results of con-
tracts; possibly they only represent the co.’s humorous
way of doing things. Anyhow, it is a most unbusiness-
like arrangement. Any business man expects the new
balance-sheet to start where the old one left off. The
reserve fund has no more right than the dock itself to go
away either in whole or in part without leaving a printed
explanation of its departure ; and, on the other side of
the case, large sums have no more right to break into
the balance-sheet without explanation than large men
have to break into the manager’s house. To return to
figures, if the profits declared in the balance-sheet are
alone taken into consideration and the reserve fund
mystery is disregarded, these are the amounts :
June, 1903 .. .. £6392 June, 1906 .. .. £6237
Dec., 1903 .. .. 5971 Dec., 1906 .. .. 81U
J.une, 1904 .. .. 4868 June, 1907 .. .. 7949
Dec., 1904 .. .. 6169 Dec., 1907 .. .. 8482
June, 1905 .. . . 5201 June, 1908 .. .. 6401
Dee., 1905 .. .. 5934 Dee., 1908 .. .. 6596
The irritating brevity of Mort’s balance-sheet reaches its
top-note when it compresses things like this: “Net
profits for the half-year, after deducting miscellaneous
and working expenses, depreciation, etc.” The amount
written off for depreciation might be put in brackets, if
it isn’t thought deserving of a whole line to itself. The
last line of the second tabie shown above represents a
very slight improvement on the figures for the preceding
half-year. When the stated net profits are taken in con-
junction with the unexplained additions to reserves (as in
first table) a drop of £1384 is revealed. The co. is in
150,000 ordinary, and 50,000 preference shares ; also, it
owes £148,300 on debentures (130,000 of the £150,000
ordinary capital was transferred from reserves in 1899).
In connection with the depreciation puzzle, it is interest-
ing to compare, “ Property, plant, and stocks, less depre-
ciation,” at June, ’OB, with the same item in Dee. of the
same year. The December figures indicate a shrinkage of
£5910; but as it maybedue to the using up of stocks, it is
not a guide to go by.

The general dulness now pervading the coal business has
dealt the East Greta a heavy blow in the waistcoat, and
profits have come down with a run. The coal and goods
traffic on the co.’s railway is also stated to have materially
decreased. The advantage of having something consider-
able up your sleeve is shown by the East Greta’s present
behavior, for, instead of sitting dolefully oyer a depleted
cash-box, it has paid its habitual dividend with an easy
conscience, and, in addition, has written £2OOO off plant,
and charged £3231 for depreciation direct to profit ami
loss-making £6372 for the 12 months covered by this and
the previous report. As mentioned some time ago, the
co. is exceptionally Well placed to see a rainy day
through ; and, now that the moisture is descending, it
isn’t very low-spirited. Its report, too, speaks hopefully
of the future without any perceptible break in its voice.
Considerable additions have been made to the railway roll-
ing stock (the co. now owns 14 locomotives alone), and a
complete electric plant that will make for more economical
working, and give profits a leg up is almost ready to start.
The only thing wanted now is a brisk demand for coal,
and the report is hoping; for that as hard as any document
can.

The drop in profits for the past term is due to a slightly
increased expenditure ; the receipts this half-year totalled
£1544, as against £1358 for August ’OB. The disburse-
ments, however, this time climbed up about £303, thus
wiping out the gain in revenue, as well as slicing off a bit
extra. The Volum Co. is just over 21 years old ; it is one
of Geelong’s best-known institutions, and it goes along
paying its 10 per cent, dividends with admirable regu-
larity. At the same time, it doesn’t ever seem to forget
to write down plant and rolling stock. The regular prac-
tice in this regard seems to be to write 5 per cent, off
plant, and 10 per cent, off rolling stock each half-year.
An occasional writing- down of freeholds is the explana-
tion of the larger amounts shown in the third column of
the above table. A cheerful note in this concern’s
accounts is that dividends do not ever seem to have
retreated. In 1899, 5 per cent, cheered the shareholder’s
purse ; in 1902, 8 per cent, was the order of the balance-
sheet ; and now it seems to have settled down to a steady
10 per cent. The co. has gradually acquired a good deal
of property with the undivided profits—the supposition
being that property in this instance means pubs.

* * ■» *
Sydney is having an epidemic of embezzlement
among solicitors. Hardly a week passes without some
Gentleman One heingcalled upon to show cause ; and the
hole in the trust funds is something appalling. One
solicitor who recently fell in with a loud splash has no
less than five destitute widows on his conscience. Some
day N. S. Wales may get a Government that isn’t domi-
nated by lawyers, and the first act of that Government
will be to establish a public trust office and devise legis-
lation by which solicitors who have the management of
estates will be compelled to submit to a bi-annual audit
by a Government officer. The man who leaves his estate
to the care of a private trustee is a fool ; but the one who
leaves it to a solicitor is a straight-out criminal, who
conspires to leave his widow and children destitute.
There are in N. S. Wales two trustee companies
which have a considerable capital as security for the
faithful performance of their duties, and why on earth
any man prefers to leave the affairs of his widow and
children in the hands of a private solicitor with a dingy
little office many stairs up, and with no visible capital
except a brass-plate and a very worn mat, in preference
to giving the job to a strong company which has some
capital to lose, is a mystery only explainable bv Carlyle’s
utterance concerning “mostly fools.” But, anyhow, it
is the duty of the Government to protect the people
against swindling solicitors just as much as against
foreign invaders, so it is urgently necessary that the
Public Trust Office should be established without delay.

Some more flattering tales are being printed
about the Lusitania, alias Goodenough, at Menzies
(W.A.). Here is the latest: “ The work done lately has
considerably augmented the ore reserves. . . . A winze
put down from the surface disclosed fair average values
for the 60ft. sunk.’’ But, as already paragraphed, there
is another side to the picture which shows the property
in its true colors, and stamps it as a delusion and a snare.
The Bulletin hopes that the Melbourne crowd which is
being pressed to take an option on the property will have
a squint at this side of the picture and judge for itself.
* * * *
Reported from London that Kaufman has
pulled right out of Phillips River (W.A.), and left the
Hirsch people to paddle their own canoe. Latter are
showing plenty of enterprise and mean to make the best
of things, but small ore deposits and heavy pumping
costs are likely to tell a disappointing tale in the lone-
rum A sea was met with in the Mount Catlin at 200 ft.
or 300 ft. from the siirface. Remembrance of the ruinous
cost then entailed in keeping down the water no doubt
explains the reluctance of the management to go on sink-
ing in this property, or to get much below water level in
any of the other holdings.

The local syndicate of ten which got hold of the
North Boulder (VV.A.) leases and plant apparently stands
a good chance of making money. Enough machinery is
said to be on the property to recoup the new owners
what they paid for the whole caboose, while a patch of
lSdwt. to 20dwt. ore which the co. left behind in its
hurry is now being taken out. Already 8., M. and Co.
have been asked to quote for the treatment of a trial
parcel of 1000 tons or more at the Oroya Brown Hill mill.

Golden Horseshoe (W.A.) has just got itself
talked about by the intersection of 7ft. of 15dwt. ore at
2000 ft. This body is identical with the Horseshoe No. 3
lode, which is a continuation of the Ivanhoe east lode.
In the present instanc , the ore was readied by a cross-
cut off the long south-east crosscut started some months
ao-o from the bottom of the Ivanhoe South Extended
shaft, and subsequently extended across the Horseshoe
lease.’ The main workings in the latter lease still require
to be" deepened a few hundred feet to reach 2000 ft.
* * * *
Boulder Deep Levels (W.A.) is being tested at
300 ft. by means of a drive on Morrison’s lode from tiie
workings of the adjoining Hannan’s Star. Some ore
worth 725. per ton has been broken out of the face to go
on with ; but there isn’t any guarantee that this grade
will be maintained. No work has been done in the Deep
Levels above 500 ft., and the attempt made to find gold at
the laiter point was a very feeble and half-hearted one.
All the crushings so far taken out of the property came
from between 900 ft. and 1100 ft., and a reserve of at least
40,000 tons of payable ore is still reckoned to be standing
between these two levels.
Ivanhoe Co. (W.A.) isn’t at all put out over the
poor values met with in the crosscut at 1970 ft. Driving
has been started on the lode, and once the break in the
country is passed through—which, it is estimated, should
only be a matter of 30ft. or 40ft. in each direction—the
management is confident of getting the usual grade of
ore. The 2120 ft. level is being opened up as speedily as
possible, and the shaft is to be taken down to 2270 ft.
without delay 7.

The word that came from London via the West
(B. 25/3/’O9), and asserted that Hoover and Govett were
going to setup in business as mine-managers, proves to
be incorrect. 1 This paper sprinkled the atom of informa-
tion with the cold water of doubt when it first arrived,
and it isn’t vastly surprised to find it was wrong.
» " * » »
Shareholders wore a funereal look at the
Golden Bole (W.A.) meeting, which, under the circum-
stances, wasn’t out of place. In a lugubrious sort of
speech from the chair, J. W. Fimister mentioned that
the cash in hand on that day was £5200, and that this
surplus was likely to be added to at the rate of between
£2OO and £3OO a month for a few months longer. If,
however, some new shoot of gold was not struck in the
meanwhile, he thought the co. would then have to go
into crape and buy a poor man’s funeral with a mule in
the shafts. Disappointment was expressed at the recent
loss of £7OOO on the Kohinoor South option, after which
cigars were handed round as a sort of comforter, and a
little later on the proceedings terminated with a drink at
the nearest bar.
* # » *
On December 31, 1908, the Ivanhoe (W.A.) was
estimated to liave 934,089 tons in sight above 1820 ft., of a
gross value of £2,321,065. After allowing for cost of pro-
duction, loss in residues, etc., this gave the mine a
dividend value on that date of a shade under £5 per share.
Great Fingall (W.A.) is turning its attention to
West Africa. A four months’ option has been secured on
the Tarkwa Main Reef, in the Wassau district, Gold
Coast Colony, and should the deal be completed, a new
concern, with a nominal capital of £210,000 in £1 shares,
is to be formed. Great Fingall will be allotted 75,000
shares at par, and the Tarkwa Main Reef will be given
60,000 fully-paid shares for nothing. This will
leave a reserve of 75,000 shares, on which the Great
Fingall will have an option at 255. for three years. The
shares to be received by 7 the Tarkwa Main Reef will admit
of the exchange of one share in the new co. for every
three shares held in the old. The property comprises
165 acres, and has a banket formation running through it.
The ore in sight is estimated at 2J million tons, and tiie
co. also owns a two-sevenths interest in an adjoining
property of 378 acres, which is also reckoned to have
a few million tons of formation. Somehow it hasn’t
been able to make a living out of either mine.
* * * *
Hainault Co. (W.A.) got hold of some 12dwt.
ore at 850 ft. the other day, and the market' rose out of all
proportion to the value of the development. The values
met with are supposed to be a continuation of those
driven on to the boundary at 1000 ft. in the adjoining
South Kalgurli lease.
Associated (W.A.) got a little further ahead of
tiie mill during the year ended March 31. On that date
the reserves were estimated at 483,517 tons, containing
241,268 fine oz., as compared with 430,414 tons, contain-
ing 219,7930 z. for the previous year. The gain on the 12
months’ development over and above the ore treated,
which amounted to 125,754 tons, was therefore 53,103
tons. At the present rate of output the ore in sight is
now equal to about 3J years’ supplies. General Manager
Roberts contemplates a visit to London—his first—in
order to let the Board see him in the flesh.

“Oof Bird” writes re par. (B. 15./4/’09): R. G.
Gladstone, auditor for the Flag Gold and Copper Co.
(W.A,), has something to learn about mine accounting in
tiie West. All the bigeos. over there charge expenditure
on main shafts and levels against revenue as they 7
go along. Some concerns certainly 7 do doctor
their financial statements so as to bring them in
line with the system defended by Mr. Gladstone ;
but, to put it bluntly, mine accounts are not honestly 7
set out unless every item of exp nditure incurred during
the period they cover is included and debited against
current profits. As regards the present position of the
co., every word of Mr. Gladstone’s dazzling statement has
been taken down in writing, and may 7 , unless officially
contradicted, be used as evidence against the Board at
the next half-yearly meeting.
*.* * *
A disinclination to undertake the pioneering
work in coke manufacture probably accounts for the slow
rate of progress which the industry makes in Queensland.
The rapidly-increasing demand warrants some action by
the local colliery proprietors, as is shown by the returns
dealing with imported coke during the past five years :
1904, 6478 tons ; 1905, 9823 tons ; 1906, 22,661 tons ; 1907,
34,013 tons; 1908, 58,079 tons. During 1908 the total
quantity manufactured in the State was only 10,684 tons.
There is no doubt about the quality of most of the locally-
produced coke, and it is difficult to understand why a bid
is not made for the trade of the State.
■*■***
A Cloncurry (Queensland) correspondent writes:
Tilings at the Mount Elliott mine have been lively of
late. Manager Corbould and his employees have had
exciting times. His mine staff walked off—or, at least,
took the once-a-week mail coach—at about two hours’
notice. Then his engine-drivers left in a body, followed
by some of the company 7 ’s teams, which consist of a large
number of waggons and some hundred head of horses.
When the manager wanted to know why such powerful
horses could not cover more than four miles per day lie
was left with about a hundred pairs of winkers to hold.
In all, about 100 men have left or been discharged, but
the management appear to keep things going, and places
are being filled. Metallurgist Read, who designed the
smelting plant, and who was to have run the same, has
left. The plant is very much cramped for room, but
as all tiie foundations are in, and excavations made,
it will have to go forward. Large bodies ol ng>-
grade copper ore are showing in the Nos. 1; ->
3 levels. The 400 ft. level is in the primary sulphide ana
shows a splendid body 7 of copper ; but it is siliceous, and
at the present time no known fluxes are in the district
with the exception of some small and quite insufficient
deposits. The upper level ores —oxides and enriched
sulphides—carry such a very great percentage of copper
that it will require a large p rcentage of iron and sulphur
to form a matte for the converter plant that has been
erected. The question is : Does the mine ore carry' the
necessary flux? The company has been looking for flux,
so it is to be hoped that the new smelting and converter
plant is not going to be a misfit. Men who know this
country sa.v there is no flux, and that it will be impossible
to obtain a supply until the railway has been established.
The Cloncurry-Mount Elliott line should be completed b.y
about the end of the year.

A discursive man writes from the North : “ As
regards Cloncurry. Had lung yarn with man who has
just seen several of the mines, said man being highly
qualified to express an opinion. He confirms my 7 view
that Elliott is a very blue duck indeed. Reckons that
the bores traverse the lode at a very flat angle, and in a
line almost parallel to the strike. Hence the reported
thicknesses are moonshine. There’s no doubt at all that
the ‘ mineralised slates ’ are the same ore body 7 as the
quartz pyroxene gangue of the 285 level sulphides, arid
the ealcite on the 285 is a local replacement. Similar
replacements occur in the Great Australia mine. . . .
MacGregor has got real good-looking sulphides, but they
are secondary. The zone of impoverishment is fairly 7
extensive, so that when the primary ore is cut the values
must drop considerably. Still the mine is looking better
than it has done, and if they 7 were not such a tarnation
long way 7 from the rail and in hilly 7 country things might
warrant a smile. As it is, they will be wise who defer
smiling till the true primary zone is opened up....
Hampdens are sinking like the schoolboy 7 with lagging
steps, who crawled unwillingly on his wav. They don’t
hanker after their ore in depth. ... If only 7 they 7 will put in
a Gulf railway, I feel sure that we shall hear con-
siderable of the Nor’-West. Gad, it’s a great land!
One day there’ll be maize all along the river flats, and
from the dams they 7 will build on every mountain river—
dry sand in summer, but torrents in the rains—the stored
water will go down to the closely-cultivated patches of
black soil that flank the remains of the Cloncurry plain.
We won’t see it, but it makes even a pessimistic miner
dream dreams and wonder when God will send the man
to do the work that will make him Queensland’s national
hero. ... I won’t predict ” (this is a sudden drop back
into prose) “ concerning the chances of the Towers mines
getting the Day Dawn extension. Personally 7 lam hope-
ful—its contagious up here —and feel inclined to fancy
their chances. But when I reason it out —well, that’s
another story 7 . Still Ido think that Blocks will get
home. I like Clarke’s as well as anything. If any of the
Brilliants get the Day 7 Dawn reef the scrip will hustle
considerable.”
Chillagoe (Q.) has started a good deal of mining
development near Einasleigh, and this work cannot fail
to be costly 7. A lease about which more reports should
be forthcoming has been purchased 10 miles from the
township. Chillagoe was perhaps a little too generous
with mine reports in old boom times, hut nowadays share-
holders only get a side peep at a mining development.
For instance, they 7 know mighty little about what is
going on at the big copper show (the Daiutree) 35 miles
up the river from Einasleigh. This, on the surface, was
from 10ft. to 40ft. wide, with good streaks of ore inter-
spersed among a lot of poor stuff. Debenture interest,
depreciation, and generally 7 heavy and continuous capital-
expenditure devour most of the railway 7 profit. Inci-
dentally, of course, everything depends on mining; so
Chillago 7 is doing the right thing in purchasing fresh
properties. So far Chillagoe smelting w'orks have not
proved a great success—recoveries are poor and costs
high—but the trouble has been exaggerated. For in-
stance, the statement that Mungana’s £222,000 of metals
for 1908 all went to pay costs leaves out of account the
big reclamations that had to be paid when the slump
happened.
