Tommy Burns, a tin show near the Mount Gar-
net (Q.) railway, has reported by telegram to the Sydney 7
Stock Exchange that a dividend of £5 a share has been
declared. A dividend of £1 was expected, so if £5 turns
out to be a true bill, shareholders may consider th’-ni-
selves lucky. It is intended to keep a reserve of £2OOO,
which reads rather queerly in conjunction with the big
dividend ; £2OOO seems a small amount with which to
develop and equip a mine. A crushing of 243 tons of tin
stone returned 26 tons of concentrates, assaying 71 per
cent, metallic tin, and 10 tons, averaging 55 per cent.
This gave the syndicate £2llO clear of crushing ex-
penses, nearly £9 per ton of stone. The open cut work-
ings are said to be capable of producing 11 tons per day 7,
so another crushing may be expected shortly 7 . The work-
ings show an improvement on i he western side, and a face
10ft. wide includes two veins of tin oxide considerably
intermixed with rook. Tommy Burns is not far from
Dalziell’s and Gilmore’s, but geological conditions are
quite different. All that can be said in its favor at pre-
sent is that a big tonnage of rich ore near the surface is
not to be despised.
* * * *
East Mexican. Charters Towers (Queensland),
according to an official report, is driving through a more
favorable formation at the 810 ft. level. The chance of
striking a solid reef is much better than it was, which is
quite enough to cause a leap in shares. The manager is
extending an old level which was driven many years
since. This level has now been taken in 150 ft., and it is
only for the last 6ft. that the formation .has shown signs
of improvement. At present th ■ drive is hearing nearly 7
due east, or parallel to the main Day 7 Dawn reef. The
vertical depth of No. 5, which was extended into the East
Mexican ground from Mills’ United, is 1363 ft., so the
810 ft. formation is a long distance overhead. The Day
Dawn P.O. and other leases adjacent to Mexican’s eastern
boundary 7 have an improved value, and there is very little
doubt that the finding of a solid reef would cause a big
move in share values, because the Day Dawn P.C. has
been one of the best dividend payers on the field. There
is certainly a great deal in the history of Day Dawn de-
velopments, from the early days of poverty and struggle
when it was sinking the first shaft up to now, to
encourage persistent search on the part of the East
Mexican for a payable reef.

Stannary Hills (Q.) is settling down to toil now
the strike is over. Tin mining wasn’t looking too good, and
the old Stannary was hard up, so tiie delay was partly com-
pulsory 7 . The general policy just now is to continue
sinking the new shaft, which is down 432 ft., and to open
up larger ore reserves. There is also a show known as
Central Hill, which is to be worked as an open cut. The
ore assays o'B6 per cent, tin oxide, and might, perhaps,
give a mill recovery worth 155., so cheap mining and
crushing are essential. Improvements have been made to
the mill —which, unfortunately, is too far from the mines
—with a view to reducing costs by one-half. If such a
reduction is possible, the fact gives the old system, about
which everyone seemed proud, a very hard knock. No
doubt costs in general can be reduced, but the main
trouble will be to improve tiie output of tin. It is said
that tin mines don’t pay 7, but here is Stannary expecting
enough profit out of a monthly output of about 60 tons of
tin concentrates, containing, say, 40 tons of metal, to
pa.v dividends on more shares than the population of
Sydney 7. Another threepenny call was mentioned at the
meeting of shareholders at Adelaide.
After a long and expensive voyage through the
Courts, the Tornado mining case finished its journey in
the High Court at Brisbane the other day. The appeal
of John Moffat against the finding of the Queensland
Supreme Court that the Sheppards were entitled to the
whole of the proceeds of the ore taken from the Tortiado
mine before May 2, 1907, was allowed ; and the judgment
of the District Court that they were entitled only to the
proceeds of what was taken out by ten men working six
days a week was restored, with costs against the Shep-
pards.
* * * *
The Bulletin’s forecast of the tight position
the O.K. (Q.) copper mine would be in as a result of the
adverse judgment in the recent lawsuit has been realised.
Theco. has found it essential to mortgage its copper and
plant in order to find the £lO,OOO which the Court re-
quired it to put up prior to an appeal. It would there-
fore appear that O.K. has been running at a loss during
the half-year, although perhaps the accounts —through
charging’certain things to development, and calling de-
velopment a capital expenditure- may show otherwise.
Had the development work of the past half-year revealed
any' extensive body of ore, such a proceeding might be
justified to a limited extent, and it will be interesting to
hear General Manager Shepherd on the situation in the
half-yearly report, which is now about due. O.K. is
admittedly short of money—and good judges say also
short of payable ore. With a lawsuit to fight, a jury’s
finding to upset, and more capital wanted for the purpose
of finding pay ore, O.K.’s cake looks rather like the usual
mixture of flour and water.

We hear much concerning the Cooktown coal
fields just now, and matters have got so far that the
Northern people have subscribed the best part of £loooto
test the field by boring. This is to be subsidised by an
equal amount from the Government. Although certain
geologists have reported favorably, the latest opinion of
the Government geological survey is not very encourag-
ing. There appears to he no thickness of coal
worth speaking of, and anything less than 2ft.
will not pa.v. Anything approaching this so
far, with perhaps one exception, is of very
poor quality. In a moment of enthusiasm £6OO was
collected on account of a report that 2ft. of good coal had
been located, but this on closer examination turned out
to be less than Ift. The general conclusion of the Govern-
ment Geologist is ttiat there remains little coal to be
proved, and that “ the prospects do not appear to be
sufficiently good to warrant any large outlay.”

The downward course of the North Mount Lyell
(Tasmania) has always been doubtful, but good develop-
ments at 1000 ft. have caused an optimistic feeling about
the 1100 ft. level. A good deal of theorising has been in-
dulged in, because, in sinking the main shaft, the folia-
tions in the rock were noticed to underlie to the west.
The change has been from an easterly underlie to the
vertical, and then to the west. A change of this kind
may' be expected to influence the position of the ore body
at TIOOft. At the 1000 ft. level developments have
been good. One of the south drives has proved
225 ft. of payable ore, and another drive also to
the south is proving the contour and dimensions of this
large ore body. Some rich assays have been obtained,
but it will take a good deal of rich stuff to sweeten the
large half-yearly' output of 200,000 tons. Mount Lyell
gives employment to 2000 hands, but a good deal of time
is lost in the open-cut workings at this time of year.
There are about three-quarters of a million tons of fluxing
ore available in the open-cut workings, and very much
larger quantities underground. A start is to be made to
mine pyritic ore below the open cut, and this opening of
these deep levels in the parent mine is a matter of some
consequence.
George F. Bryant, of the Cathcart, Ararat
(Vic.), trumpets a solo as sweet as that of Harry Stone-
ham. “I have no hesitation in saying that present
developments point to the fact that you have a most valu-
able property which, when properly drained and worked
on systematic lines will return good profits for your out-
lay 7.” It means a pretty big order to make up the slab of
capital spent off and on by investors on the Cathcart
ground. Even with the present co., £45,000 has been
sunk from calls and gold.. George has nice, patchy
gold to cut into, but stock-exchangeites question the
width of the gutter and the ease with which dirt can be
taken out! On this point George speaks with as much
confidence as a German general the day before the battle.
Give the wash time to drain, and it will come out cheaply 7,
says he. “Give me time” is also the tune to which
llie bears of Cathcart are dancing to-day. The market is
badly over-sold, and every' rich patch George alights on 1
is a hot brick. The public, which doesn’t like the bears,
laughs over the animals’ jumps, and asks for more of
the performance. This it is likely to get, if George’s
hypotheses be correct.
The Alpine Gold-Mining Co. at Reedy Creek
(Vic.) presents its fifth half-yearly report. The balance-
sheet isn’t wildly cheerful in itself; the gold obtained
during the six months to 31st March was worth £1333
3s. Id., and the outgoings were £1445 17s. 3d. By col-
lecting £l7 14s. 2d. in calls, and by 7 reducing the bank
balance from £97 14s. 2d. to £2 14s. 2d., the concern got
to the end of the half-year alive, but £2 14s. 2d. isn’t
much to close any half-year with. At the same time,
there is said to be much hope for the Alpine. It was
floated on the strength of the old Alpine reef, but it found
quite unexpectedly another lode, and some of the recent
small crushings have gone IJoz., or even 2oz. They 7
were very 7 small certainly—s 4 tons at 20z., and 73 at
ljoz.—but it seems that 300 tons had to stand over for
lack of water. The dam is now full, and the baby plant
is pounding gaily 7 on the 300 tons, and it is hoped that the
first div. is not only in sight, but close at hand. There are
only 22,000 shares issued, so a humble £550 would pay 6d.
per share. n'-kot"

New Black Horse, Egerton (Vic.), isn’t to be
mixed up with the old Black Horse of Martin Louglilin
fame. That show petered put when old Vickery went to
the place where mining men have calls other than those
of mines to attend to. Still, the atmosphere is pretty
warm round the New 7 Black Horse owing to the Board
having swapped mine-managers in a hurry and then hav-
ing quarrelled with local men for not paying calls. The
co. is working a little away from the old mine, and
trial lots went high. Thi n a crushing of 120 tons
fizzled out with only 7£oz. Much blue talk was heard over
tiiis yield, as a preliminary “fair sample of the slopes ”
had assayed at the rate of 38oz. to the ton. Directors
propose further calls, and intend to smash into the stopes
to get the slab of gold that missed the miners’ pick when
tiie breaking down was done. They have 15ft. to play
with, and although they iiave cut company with their
late manager, they don’t hesitate to give him as an
authority that Bft. is pay able. With such yards of confi-
dence in tiie mine the B ‘ard ought to stop slinging abuse
at the yokels who pasted them with scrip. Rather-hould
they offer thanksgiving that they hold so much more of a
ffiine which “is to equal its famous namesake, the old
Black Horse.”
Russell’s Reef, Lauriston (Vic.), has the mis-
fortune to belong to a zone of saddle country, where the
quartz and gold have been deposited in tiie anticlines
instead of at the apex of the folds, as at Bendigo. Why
this should be so even Geology Dunn, with all his
saddleising theories, has failed to state. The fact stands
also that gold in the bottom of the troughs isn’t
in quantity like that in the quartz at the top of the folds,
nor is the quartz itself so extensive. Consequently,
Lauriston mining is as fitful as a maiden at spring time.
At Russell’s £57,500 of capital and £134,000 of gold have
been expended for a return to shareholders of
£40,000. There might have been more, but Lauris-
ton mining is not yet of the vigorous order
that rips ahead with shaft sinking and builds up
reserves of stone. Tiie battery anti-dates Methuselah,
and so costs amount to 4s. per ton, when with proper
appliances they ought to be 50 per cent. less. Mining
charges are high. First, because of haphazard work
thar. has to be done with the stopes close to the battery.
Next, because of the disturbed state of the country.
There is always enough gold to tempt the investor, arid
just enough belief in the market over the chance of a rise
to keep Russells from drifting far into the land of mining
derelicts. - - #

Two Traction Engines are being made for the
Victorian Agricultural Department, to the design of Mr.
Kenyon, the engineer-in-chief, for use in reclaiming the
heavy grass-tree lands of the Heytesbury forest. Special
difficulties have been discovered in the pioneer work, and
these engines are the outcome of the experiments, and
the experience of the Austral Otis Engineering Co., of
South Melbourne, who have this work in hand. These
engines are the heaviest and largest traction engines in
the Commonwealth. They are fitted with vertical water-
tube-boilers, built on the modern sectional method for a
pressure of 2001 b., and capable of developing' 100-h.p.
The wheels are chain driven, and are each 7ft. in diameter
and 4£ft. in width. The total weight of each engine will
be about 24 tons.* Id

Ever notice the smell of drugs
in a chemist’s shop pretty
strong, isn’t it? —it’s not a nice
atmosphere to work in, and we
chemists have to stand it for
such long hours that you can’t
wonder we’re worn out long
before close up time.
There’s an old saying that
“ few doctors believe in their
own physic”—well, a chemist
is much the same as a doctor in
that respect, for with my shelves
full of stuffs to cure everything
under the sun, including “that
tired feeling,” l turn to a cup
of good hot tea myself—because
l find it brisks me up, and there’s
no harmful reaction afterwards.
freshly made cup of R o bu r
at 1 1 a.m., and another at
3 p.m., with those 1 take at
my meals, keeps me pretty
alert during the day 1 know
no tea that l like so well as
No. 1 Grade Robur it’s
absolutely pure, and very cheap
at that money, at least, that’s
my opinion.

“BLACK & WHITE
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, May 4, 1909.
My Dear Artnt,—
It was a proud procession (Messrs. Marich and
Southwell headed it) that walked into the Bank
of N.S.W. on Saturday morning to make the
biggest individual deposit to the Hospital Satur-
day Fund. One of the proud party stubbed his toe
crossing the road to the bank, and remarked, as
did the Spanish hidalgo, “ This comes of walking
on the earth.” Mr. Shackleton was with the pro-
cession, but he dragged his anchors, so to speak. If
he were proud of the cheque Mr. Marich carried, he
successfully concealed the fact. The exact amount
on the document was £514 10s. 6d., the same being
the proceeds of the Shackleton lecture at the
Town Hall. Messrs. Denison Miller and Alfred
Milson. who received it, all but burst into tears of
gratitude. The Icebreaker was asked to motor
round to look at the collection box, but he grimly
declined. The Box had blocked his path ever
since he left his club in the morning. In fact, the
night before—very late the night before —whilst he
was the guest of a large collection of pressmen, two
premature feminines with a too-sudden box tried to
get into the assemblage. Legally, it seems. Hos-
pital Saturday commences one instant after mid-
night, and collecting is allowed whenever the last
stroke of Friday has struck on the Post Office
clock.
A kindly resident of Gunnedah, who declines
publicity and who will certainly save his soul
alive, sends me a cheque for five pounds for the
Hospice for the Dying at Darlinghurst. He
writes: “Your recent reference to this charity
strongly appealed to me. Its devoted promoters
deserve every assistance in their noble mission.”
Lord and Lady Dudley are at Gurrandooley at
the time of writing. There is a certain lack of
originality in this move on their part. Every
vice-regality since the beginning of things
has gone to Gurrandooley, for is it not
the headquarters of the immense Osborne
family, which owns more sheep than any other
family in Australia, and also—during race-weeks
or other festive occasions—the Australian Club !
Gurrandooley, which is over against Bungendore,
is an extensive, rambling old place, resembling
more than most big up-country houses the typical
Australian homestead of the untravelled Briton’s
dreams. It’s present occupant is Mr. Harry
Osborne, the eldest scion of the “Pat” Osborne
house. His wife was one of the numerous and
fair Madden girls of Melbourne.
Lord Dudley’s team of cricketers engaged a
band or troupe of Parliamentarians last Thursday,
and, in the racy phrase of the flannelled oaf with
grass stains on him, climbed all over them. The
Trumper of the afternoon was Dr. Skey, who toils
in a medical capacity at Garden Island. The
doctor is a left-hander— “ strong,” lam told by a
sporting friend. “on the off, and no mean hand
at pasting them to leg.” (What this means I
know not, but I give it for what it is worth.)
Anyhow, this player compiled a century in quick
order, and also bowled with much precision. He
is married to one of Melbourne’s Miss Smiths
and the lady was on the spot to register a discreet
cheer in recognition of her relative’s triumph.
I should admire to know who was responsible
for the furnishing of our harbor-side Government
House. The same person, I opine, whose home
was described by Lord Rosebery as a combination
of 50-guinea couches and 30-shilling oleographs.
As it chances, there are no oleographs where the
Dudleys abide at present, but there are a host of
oils, which, for all-round villainousness, beat the
average mechanically-fashioned picture out of
sight. For example, the “ Lord Carrington ” who
glimmers woodenlv in the hall. The maroon-
colored drawing-room couches and things ought to
be either dynamited or re-covered, and so dis-
guised. However, there may be a subtle wisdom
at the back of all this. The awfulness of the
furniture and pictures and things that pervade
the dwelling is calculated to drive the Ex-
cellencies of the moment to gaze from them across
the bay. Urban aldermen and so forth haven’t
been able to strew crude-colored sofas about the
harbor or hang calamitous portraits in the
Botanical Gardens, and so these fair spots are
always in a state of beauty to rest the tired and
outraged vice-regal eye.
There was a dance for the young people who
attended the Shackleton lecture at Government
House last week. It occurred after the explorer
had told all he knew about the Far South regions,
and lasted till the smallish hours. The sitters-out
were provided with food for conversation in the
shape of a stove, pots, pans, and sledge, tin cups,
and other hardware appertaining to the hero of
the evening. The goods lay stretched on the
verandah outside the ballroom, mute evidence of
what a comfortlessly, primitive job Poling is.
Dr. Henry Shackleton, M.D., the father of our
recent visitor (he left us on Sunday night) practises
near London. In expressing to an interviewer
his delight at sonny’s achievement, he mentioned
that his Ernest was born in Co. Kildare, Ireland.
Also that whilst on a voyage to South Africa in
1900 in the Tintagel Castle he wrote a book called
“0.H.M.5.”
One who signs himself “ A Horrid Man ” sends
this:—
The women graduates of Sydney ’Varsity left their
husbands and kids last Saturday to argue with the cold
remains of Friday’s dinner while they themselves sat
down to an eight-course collation in honor of themselves
at a city restaurant. About 200 M. A.’s, B.A.’s, M.B.’s,
etc., assembled to take stock of each other. Looking at
the list of those present, it is clear that the Sydney
’Varsity, with its higher education of women, has not
produced one lady that stands out far above her fel-
lowesses. The Uni. has produced she-graduates in Arts,
Science, Law, Medicine, and Dentistry. Some of her
daughters have decent Government biliets, up to £4OO a
year. Others have comfortable practices. Some have
married well, fair or rottenly, and some not at all. But
not during the 25 years women have been graduating has
one really shone out as a winner. The secret seems to be
that the most (.promising and brilliant get married, and
so rock the cradle instead of shaking the universe. I
suppose the girl who isn’t smart enough to snare a hus-
band isn’t likely to otherwise distinguish herself. For
goodness sake don’t say who wrote this or I will have to
clear out.
The Sep. Levys have almost settled down in
C.-J. Darley’s old house. Mr. Theo. Marks, who
undertook the formidable job of setting it in order
for them, has altered it almost out of recognition.
The narrow hall that was the fashion in the
remote period when this aged place was built has
been transformed into a wide, high, smoking
room ; the dining-room has been put back, as
to period, a hundred years or so; that is
to say, what was once Early Victorian
has been made Georgian. Parts of the
house have been pulled down and others built
up. The present occupants have it on a ten
years’ lease only, but their activity in making it
habitable, and the fact that the Darley family is
now permanently located in England, suggest
that they mean to acquire it for their own
ultimately.
The eldest youngster of Pa Uhr Smith, of the
Australia, marries a daughter of Dr. Quaife next
Thursday. Smith, junior, is a black-and-white
artist, and the bride-elect has artistic leanings.
The Charles Lloyd Jones pair will quit their
Mosman home, with its all-round sea-viewage, and
abandon their motor launch, and desert their other
desirable possessions next week for a two-years’
absence in dear, cold England. Mrs. L. J. is a
sister of the bride-elect mentioned higher up.
Miss Phyllis Clubbe, one of Dr. Clubbe’s
daughters, was chief witness in a cruelty-to-a-cat
case, recently heard at the Paddington Police
Court. She merits an approving pat on the head.
Out of any 20 maidens who see animals being ill-
treated rather less than Jth of a maiden has the
courage and resolution to do something in the
matter. There are in this community some
wanton torturers of dogs and horses and other
friendly creatures that mostly don’t dream of
hitting back, who should get all the punishment a
police court can inflict, and a sound boiling or two
to boot. Come to think of it, there is a S.P.C.A.
here, which girls with leisure time on their hands
might do worse than join.
Dorothy, the youngest daughter of the T. A,
Dibbses, of North Sydney, was married last week to
a man from Maoriland, one William Traill Ritchie.
The bride’s father, who has a beautiful billet near
the till of the Commercial Banking Co. of Sydney,
is one of Sydney’s most affluent citizens, and his
roll-top desk at the bank is a symbol of great
financial importance. The wedding ceremony
was fully choral and very frockv. Both the Dibbs
brothers, the late George and the present T. A.,
had and have a preponderance of girls in their
respective large families. The late beknighted
brother was very proud of his 10 daughters.
Concerning my remarks about the little copper
cross on the red ribbon, the V.C. that Captain
Hore-Ruthven wears, and which order I said two
Australians had acquired, a Vandemonian turns
his battery on me :
Your memory ha< played you false. State Frights!!
Where does my State come in ? Lieutenant Bisdee wears
the honor. A pension of about threepence a day goes
with it, but Bisdee has inherited considerable of the
earth, and is not taking any threepenny pensions. Be-
sides Bell, the Westralian, and Mavgar, the Victorian, Dr.
Howse, of Orange, has the V.C. bit of copper.
One of the Sydney Arts and Crafts girls is
making the cutest hatpins of stamped leather.
The weapon she turns out is a light affair that
won’t catch in your hair or net or veil. Most of
the ornamental pins have this bad habit, and then
you use language that would not be approved by
the Girls’ Realm Guild. The leather hat stabbers
may be got at the Roycroft Library.
The death of Charles Conder, the Australian-
born artist, would seem to have passed almost
unnoticed in his own country. He died in a
lunatic asylum in the prime of life. Expressions
of regret, made in first-rate prose and verse, have
been plentiful enough from first-rate artistic and
literary sources on both sides of the English
Channel. Mr. Laurence Binyon, the Saturday
Reviewer, gives the McCulloch Collection (the
collector piled his money bags in Australia) a nibful
of acid because it contains no Conder. “ Per-
haps, now that he is dead,” writes Binyon,
“the Chantrey trustees will realise that he ought to
be represented in the national collection.” His
place is pronounced as “secure.” and the state-
ment that “England can ill afford to lose such
artists as Charles Conder” ought to give rest to
any perturbed painter’s spirit. Conder made
what is called a “good marriage,” his wife being
heavily moneyed. His delicate and distinctive
work in fan painting brought him much coin of
his own, so the wolf never howled obtrusively on
the Conder doorstep.
Mrs. T. H. Kelly presided—in the absence of
Mayoress Allen Taylor—over a meeting called to
arrange details for the Dreadnought matinee to
given next Wednesday at the Royal. Thirteen
ladies responded to the 11 a.m. call to help to
build the battleship, but this unlucky number,
when the meeting was half-way through, was
raised to 15 by the arrival of two more crushed-
looking feminities. So the requisite £2,000,000 is
doubtless assured. The proceedings were too
placid for further mention.
Concerning the bouquet of “black carnations”
that the cable tells of as a presentation from the
Kaiser to ex-President Roosevelt, of Uncle
Samuel’s dominions, a friend of mine, who lovingly
tickles a flower garden with hoe, rake and spade,
tells me that “there are no black carnations.
The Emperor-given posy must have been very
dark crimson carnations. There are no black
flowers, and the only pure blue flower
is the blue salvia.” And no doubt scores
of persons are ambling through life under
the delusion that there are really truly blue
stocks, blue dephiniums and blue sweet pea, and
that the fairy flax is blue, likewise the cornflower
and the forget-me-not.
A man writes me :
At Harden railway station, on a recent Monday night,
22 newly-wedded couples started off from the same plat-
form on their 22 honeymoons. Since then four, if not
more, other weddings have eventuated in the locality.
Now- why is this amazing rush of matrimony at
the little town of Harden ?
“After the Opera,” the curtain-raiser that is to
pad out “An Englishman’s Home,” proved to be
familiar to some of us. It was published m an
American or French magazine, and is very
“fresh,” within the American meaning of the
word. A pretty young married woman returns
from the opera with her husband —returns to their
joint and several flat. He leaves her immedi-
ately to go a short journey by train. There enters
the Tertium Quid, who is there at the request of
the ladv, and not by his own inclination. He
is a putfeckly honorable person, and would be
satisfied to sit 20 paces from the siren and talk
Antarctica. It was all her fault, as Adam said of
Eve when he wrote to the local paper complaining
about the Fall, and signed himself “ Constant
Reader.” As a matter of fact, the lady does very
swift running. While their torrid phrases are bal-
ancing to corners, they hear what they take to be
the stealthy step of a burglar. The the
next few moments is terrifying. The lady’s law-
ful tyrant has returned. So she thinks, but it is
a burglar. The lover flees, the hubby returns,
the thrills intensify, and there’s a fine unhappy
ending.
Miss Ola Humphrey, as the lady who
would put her pink slippered foot through
an important commandment, wears a gown
that might give hints in scintillations to the
Aurora which ice-breaker Shackleton saw
South Polewards. The gown experts call its
color “quince.” Perhaps some quinces are for-
gotten on the tree long enough to allow them to
acquire a deep, mellow pink hue, but the small
boy has a good memory, and the quince that gives
him a bad pain and the quince the vegetable per-
son delivers at your back door for jelly-making
are sourly green. Ola Jane’s “ quince ” charmeuse
has the pink of coral and apricot in it, and the
overdress is of net smothered in silver, mother-of-
pearl and fish-scale embroidery. The train is
square, with wide tucks across the length that
swirls snaky-wise on the carpet. It is a gown
with an emotional significance. The silver lines
on the front are all on the downward and side-
ward slant, expressive of the young wife’s inclina-
tion to get off the straight and narrow path that is
not bordered with primroses, and on to roads that
are cut on the jag or the bias.
Senator Pearce, the Defence man, was at the
Royal on Saturday night to see Brown’s amazing
defence of Myrtle Villa, and to hear how shells
can whimper and scream in real stage war.
Bengough began his season of cartoonery and
raillery on Saturday at the Centenary Hall. He
made lightning sketches of Mr. J. C. Williamson,
Mr. William Lvne and Conductor Slapoffski, but
the audience especially rejoiced at his humorous
work. A crayon triangle becomes by a few
strokes a cat’s face. A few more strokes make
the animal a hen. Another ten seconds’ work and
the egg-mother is standing on the bald pate of a
dismal man and is wearing specs and a woman's
hat. The moral, the cartoonist-monologuist says,
is “Young men, don’t marry outside of Aus-
tralia. ”
Cartoonist Bengough has his chubby little wife
with him. A pmart frocker is she, who can wear
a white serge tailor-made with effect. Ben-
gough and wife look counter-irritants to pes-
simism. The bantling Optimists’ Club ought to
snare them as members. The comedian tells that
when in London he went to a big hall to hear Mr.
Lloyd George speak on some burning question or
other. The speaker had spoken for about three
minutes to a crowded audience when a girl arose
and alleged in a shrill voice, “You must give
votes to women.” She was a suffragette, of
course, and of course she was ejected, but what
has stuck in Bengough’s memory was the mode of
ejection. Three or four or sometimes six able
bodied varlets simply flung themselves at the centre
of disturbance and dragged the beautifully-dressed
and sometimes betitled woman from the hall
with no more respect than they would use in
shifting an intoxicated park-loafer. “It was the
palpable glee with which they went at their ugly
work ” that amazed the teller. All through the
evening the organised interruptions continued,
and so did the indecorous throwing-out of the
political female. Awhile later, Mr. Bengough
was discussing some Canadian trade grievance
with Lord Carrington, who until then was
unaware of it. “ Why don’t you protest? ” asked
Carrington. “Why don’t you memorialise us on
the matter ? ” The cartoonist swore by the maple
leaf that Canada had been doing so for years.
“ Well,” sighed the noble lord, “you must worry
us; you must start to heckle.” “ After the
manner of the suffragettes,” remarked the visitor.
“Yes,” was the lordly answer; “and they are
going to win.” He spoke the prophecy italic-
wise.,
Miss Marie Narelle, writing from I)alby, in
Queensland, mentions the rain as raining every
day. Her tour included a coach trip of 120 miles,
mostly over black-soil country. The little co.
will go north as far as Cooktown, then cross to
Thursday Island, and sing over to Java, India,
China, and Japan.
Mrs. Hugh Ward leaves presently for Perth
(W.A.), where her husband’s company is due at
the end of this month of May for the beginning of
its Australasian season. Miss Florence Redfern,
a pretty little party who has been with various
companies, has got a year’s engagement with the
Wurd-Palotta group, and goes with Mrs. Ward to
Groperland.
Miss Fitzroy, the actress who has been playing
the well-gowned and fashionable maternal pro-
genitor in that blush-raising play, “The Hypo-
crites,” is a persistent exploiter of the Princess
robe. Not “modified Princess” or “partial
directoire,” but a skin-fitting frock with the waist
in its very own zone below the ribs, and a 20-inch
waist at that. As a member of the audience re-
marked whilst watching the lady egg her son on
to desert his rightful girl and make a rich mar-
riage, “She couldn’t be maternal with that
waist.”
Chung Ling Soo, the Chinese juggler, was the
guest of Mr. Ernest Watt at Rose Bay Golf Links
lately. Mr. Soo is not pure dragon-on-yellow-
ground as many folk suppose. The legend goes
that one parent was Chinese and the other Scotch,
wherefore I presume that his real nam'e is either
Ah Macdonald or Duncan Chung.
Jack Ripperism in another form is abroad in
Sydney. A few weeks since some destructive
imbecile ruined a number of plate-glass windows
by scoring them with a diamond. Last week the
big oil portrait of Mayor Allen Taylor that hangs
on the stairway of the Town Hall was wilfully
damaged, and now two large oil seascapes at the
Australia have been ripped up, as if with a blunt
knife. Some person is badly in need of a term of
compulsory oakum picking.
Ada Crossley will raise her famous contralto
next Saturday night at the Town Hall. The
concert is to be in the interests of charity.
According to Mr. Filson Young, a recently-
arisen musical critic who writes with profound
self-confidence in the London Saturday Review,
the future careers of two singers have been assured
by the last opera season at Covent Garden. These
fortunate persons are Americans—Mrs. Frease-
Green and Mrs. Saltzmann-Stevens. Both are
pupils of Jean de Reszke. The critic writes, “We
get voices, it is true, from Australia ; but as yet
Australia is too far out of the beaten track to pro-
vide the kind of atmosphere in which a lovely
voice can develop alongside of a genuine artistic
perception.” I suppose some will understand his
meaning. I can’t. Then he goes on to say :
But from America, young:, uncorrupted by culture,
unsapped by tradition, now as a nation at the awkward
age and at the least beautiful stage of youth, comes the
first sign of what will some day be developed art —
namely, tuneful voices of women, j-oung voices, fresh,
untired, unspoiled voices—voices that, being impassion-
ate and unworn with emotion, are most easily bent and
trained to express passion and emotion in musical art.
One would think that might as justly be written
of Australia. However, re the two Americans
whom Mr. Young pronounces “ conspicuous suc-
cesses ” : Mrs. Green was Sieglind in “ Walkure,”
and Mrs. Stevens was Briinnhilde, and the latter
so distinguished herself by her conception and
singing of the r6le that she is now “in the front
rank of opera singers.” We’d better keep our
pyes on this pair, and see if they win out according
to Filson Young’s prediction.
Miss Eva O’Connor’s top note in “ The Minstrel
Boy’’took the fancy of a London Queen’s Hall
audience on last St. Patrick’s night. Though no
encores were allowed, a double recall was given
the Australian. Her second song, “ The Memory
of the Dead,” also had a rousing effect. Reference
to the corpses of patriots (their own patriots) will
always rouse a Hibernian audience to enthusiasm.
The Sydney soprano also had a good reception in
Birmingham, where she sang under engagement
to the Gaelic League.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, May 3, 1909.
My Dear Yarrik, —
It was a sad requiem that tolled at St. Paul’s
for the deceased Janet Clarke. It would be hard
to point to any society woman who was more
numerous in the land than the Cliveden widow.
She was president of the Alliance Francaise, and
chief of that now very tired Austral Salon. She
was at the head of the Salon from its early, vigorous
days when it indulged in more than artistic and
mild literature teas. The affairs of which the de-
ceased lady was a vigorous committee woman are
many as the sands of the sea. She had been an
“absent member” for a year before her death,
but she was not forgotten. Her illnesses would
have killed half a dozen women of ordinary
vitality. It is a point to cogitate over if half the
women who claw at the fringe of society, and who
cringed to this titled society woman, ever recog-
nised that she was a governess in her younger
days. And if they did remember the fact, one
wonders if it led them to regard the governesses in
their own purse-proud homes as women of the same
intelligent calibre as the much-adored relict of
Clarke, deceased.
Clieveden is a treasury of artistic things, and
the Clarkes were also keen on early Australian
books and relics. The battered and tinny col-
lection that was Ned Kelly s armor (and which
was shown to fire the fighting lust of every Aus-
tralian small boy) belonged to the Clarkes-; but an
earlier collection is an exhaustive armory of
aboriginal weapons.
The late Mrs. Clarke belonged to the Snodgrass
clan—names as well known as the Clarkes. Mr.
Snodgrass, the old man, was a gentleman of much
land. A son recently wrote his memoirs, and the
volume would have roused the sensible old gentle-
man to sardonic laughter. Mrs. Clarke in-
herited a sense of humor and balance
from her father. The brothers Snodgrass
belong to a carefully-selected circle. Evelyn, of
Reverend tendencies, was ever an ideal curate.
His drawing-room manners reached a pinnacle of
perfection, and he knew to a nicety how to bal-
ance his cup of afternoon tea and manage his
speckless hat and carry on a delicate conversa-
tion all in the same ten minutes without a traceof
an agonised look. Brother Douglas, with a high
falsetto voice and a brown-red wig, was always the
most gentle and tame of afternoon-tea young men.
Rupert Clarke’s daughter, Phyllis, who has
been with mommer in Mayfair collecting her
lingerie and purple attire, is to be married in
May to Reggie Powers. Probably the wedding
will not be put off, as it was intended all along to
have it as quiet as a wedding may be. It is to
happen at Rupertswood, Sunbury.
Pansy Montague—the “Milo” of the music
halls —has been received into tire perfumed bosom
of society in London. There can be no doubt
about it. She dined with the Bishop of London.
And it was not at a private table at the Carlton
(do Bishops ever do that ?), but in the benign
presence of the Bishopess, and the feed of daring
was done at the Bishopage or Bishopry, and
picked lambs from the flock adorned the occasion.
The guests were all duly chronicled in the
Respectable papers. The Church has embraced
tlie Stage in a discreet manner heretofore, but
this is a case of the Church fairly hugging the
Music Hall and the Pagan worship of Venus.
Bishops are so cushionly and comfortably
respectable, like the pews in a well-fed church.
The very plumpness of bishoply silken calves is
reassuring. State Governor Carmichael ex-
panded on the Bishoply subject at a
dinner at one of the Melbourne clubs,
whipped together for his benefit in the early days
of his State vice-royalty. His Ex. mentioned that
he liked clubs. Thereupon he drifted into
snatches about the clubs to which he belonged.
Mentioning one particular favorite in London, he
said, “We have bishops, even, for members.” It
is so comfortable to belong to a club that owns
bishops as fellow members, for on a late night it’s
advisable to walk home with a bishop. If you
don’t live in his direction, even, it’s good to make
a point of walking that way and taking the
bishoply arm. If the eye of the Law happens to
notice too critically the wobble of your walk the
shining calves of the bishop will so gently soften
his stare that he’ll perhaps think it’s the bishop’s
wobble, and put it down to a spirit of divinity.
Speaking of speeches, I suppose Gov. Car-
michael is one of the worst orators in Australia ;
yet he is worth listening to. His humor is so
dry, and it crusts every remark. He knows so
much, and has excellent things to say—but it
takes great ear-strain to follow the saying of
them. Most of his remarks are addressed to the
ceiling, and he speaks with an eye cocked medi-
tatively sideways. Or else he mutters to the rug
at his feet with a die-away sigh like the patter of
an ave. Occasionally he looks at his audience
with pathetic appeal, imploring release. He
crushes his hands together, and tries each knuckle
to see if it is dislocated. Yet all the time lie is
saying good things—witty things and remarks
that show culture and artistic feeling. Anyone
with intense application is rewarded for following
the dives and dashes of his elusive voice.
The Carmichaels appear everywhere with the
new-come English diplomats in hand, Sir Charles
Lucas and Mr. Pearson. Sir Charles is a fine,
big, burly Englishman, well groomed, with a
pointed beard and a continuous twinkle in his
eye; in both eves, in fact. Men find him a “good
sort ” —a man who has culture rather than culchaw
—and a clever talker. Mr. Pearson is a keen-
eyed, small, thick-set man, forever on the alert.
Punch McKinley summoned his intimates to
him the week before last, and instigated them
to throw the footwear of luck after daughter
Ethel, who said “I will” to the Rev. George
Ambrose Kitchen. The knot was tied by instal-
ments of clergy headed by Canon Stephen, and
with full choral solemnity. Maggie Stirling added
her big contralto to the booming of the organ. A
brother clergyman, Mr. Crotty, stood gallantly to
the bridegroom, and saved him from nerve crises,
brain storms and other ills to which mankind in
the marrying is subject. Kathleen Druner, sister
Ruby and small Betty Potter helped the bride
with her frills. The frills included a wonderful
veil of Mechlin, and the bridesmaids threw blue
and white into the picture. The bride is a clever
graduate of Melbourne University, and her hus-
band takes up the cure of souls. at'Werribee (Vic.),
the haunt of the Pursy Chirnsides. i.
Mrs. Tessie Reed has left for the land where
sonorous titles abide, wearing a look of business
in her eye. The handsome widow leaves many
lamenting lovers who would willingly have be-
husbanded her and paid her travelling expenses.
The abandoned ones retain no ray of comfort save
the faint glimmer of hope that is contained in a
non-committal farewell smile. The fair quitter
proposes to put in the London season with her
sister, who used to append the name of
JTryphena ” to this page.
Last week’s cargo of trippers to the Big Smoke
included the L. L. Smith, with his wife and their
clever daughter, Louise. The irrepressible •‘doc-
tor” has been feeling his age lately (although he
is still on the near side of 80), and a sea voyage
was necessary to blow the cobwebs off him, he
said. Daughter Louise goes with the idea of
getting some further and “finishing” study in
languages. Incidentally, too, she is to be pre-
sented at Court—for the second time. She was
brought under the august eye of royalty in her
kiddie days, when her dad was in London as an
Exhibitioner Commissioner or something, and the
fact must have been noted for future reference,
for when the Duchess of York (now Princess of
Wales) came to Australia she positively remem-
bered Louise Smith, and gladdened the girleen’s
soul by recognising her. The recollecting of
names and faces, with the aid of carefully indexed
notes, is one of royalty’s fine arts.
Commencement Day at the University on
Saturday brought out all the gay birds of para-
dise that float round Alma Mater when the profs,
have got ’em all on. The place shone with scarlet
gowns and ermine hoods and all that glory of color
that is forbidden at any other time to civilian man
except in his socks and, perhaps, his tie. Chan-
cellor Madden poured molten gold over the
occasion with his Irish tongue and touch of
the blarney. The students were not there—and
so descended Peace. Among the degrees and
honors, only one degree of Doctor of Science was
won—and that by a girl. Rosa Janet White
hooked the coveted honor. She is a lucky girl,
for that degree carries a hood of adorable purple.
The lady derived quite a minor piece of satis-
faction from the fact that the Royal Society of
London has included a thesis of hers (“The
Latent Life in Seeds ’’) in its libraries.
Two young gentlemen of the Gallery-Student
persuasion write on lady-like notepapee and
invite “kompany” to a view of summer land-
scapes in the Guild Hall. The type of picture is
one that will probably appeal to people during
this wintry snap of cold. The Aspiring Ones are
yclept Pen Boyd and Frank Crozier.
Balls are beginning, like a juggler’s trick. A
charity frisk in the Hawthorn Town Hall, to aid
the society of benevolent Hawthorn ladies,
drops heavily on Friday, June 4.
I hear a painfully true story of a recent bride in
another State—never mind which State. In the
first enthusiasm of a previous engagement the
lady had taken the extreme oath of allegiance by
tattooing the initials of her intended on her fair
young leg—the leg nearest to her heart. But the
engagement sustained a double fracture, and in
due course the really and truly future husband
loomed in sight. Then the initials of the discarded
one became a matter for heroic treatment. Ara-
minta put the case before a surgeon, who removed
the tattoo marks, and filled in the blank with a
nice piece of skin from another human subject of
the same sex. The grafture was a complete suc-
cess, and before the bride walked to the altar the
other week she assured her dearest friend that the
secret was perfectly safe. All the same, it has
leaked out.
Gwen Burroughs, a stage-struck maiden of Mel-
bourne society, who hit out an artistic note in an
amateur mummer show last winter in the screen
scene from “The School for Scandal,” has proved
her mettle some more. In spite of paternal ob-
jection the girl managed to get a walking-on part
in “ Kitty Bellairs.” with a chance understudying
of the Second Lead. Miss Meredith, who plays
Lady Bab of Bath in “ Bellairs,” got ill, and Miss
Gwen got her show. Lady Bab Meredith stayed
ill for two or three performances, and the hand-
some young understudy had time to make a large
footprint on the part.
There was a strongly-assorted crowd on the
vessel that bore apostles Chapman and Alexander
to this port. Fifteen “revivalists,” in addition
to those conspicuously vehement Christians,
helped to correct the worldly and fleshly influence
of a comic opera eo., the members whereof were
almost tempted to “ reach out ” for the invisible
Beyond by the inspiring example of Mr. Alex-
ander, who always ate banan \s with his porridge.
He, by the way, brought two secretaries with him
to minister to his commercial needs. Which ex-
plains why the “ Mission ” is so alertly earnest as
regards passing round the plate. Its persona]
exes, must be considerable. And again, by the
way, there were four Mormon elders on the boat,
who travelled without private secretaries, but
seemed very hopeful, it is said, of picking upsome
well-to-do disciples in these parts.
Wren has sold out his share in the Meynell,
Gunn, and Rupert Clarke combination, and he
blazes no more in the managerial box at the
show. But one place where Wren was in evi-
dence lately was among the roses. At the Royal
Show, his gardener entered a wonderful and
fearful design for a landscape garden. The work
in it was as elaborate and unnecessarily fatiguing
as a special grandmother patchwork quilt. It
was hideously conventional, and wedged with
squares and circles all worked out in petals. A
circle of scarlet geranium petals in the middle
distance was for all the world like a racecourse.
It is wonderful how hideous some carefully-tended
gardens can be. There is a scattered crowd
of expert gardeners from England who earn big
wages, and spend endless toil in perpetrating
these cut-and-dried garden-scapes. They are
invaluable to their snobbish owners, because their
former masters have been dooks or lords, and
they have spent whips of the lord’s money (or
been owed it) in sacrificing flowers to make a
pattern like a demented carpet. Another burning
light in racing haut ton has half an acre of stiff
suburban garden and lawn guarded by 30 or so
bronze storks, that bronzelv drink or catch worms
with a cast-iron poise of their numerous bodies.
It is a fearfully expensive form of ornament, and
looks to passers on the tram like one of the
plagues of Egypt let loose.
The Taits, who have been warming Picture
Parlors by hot water pipes over the frigid vault
of the Glaciariurn, will have to move along. The
skaters are coming back to frivol. On June 5 the
ice-rink opens for its frosty season. Professor
Lewis comes out again from the Cold Land, and a
hew star, Miss Baker-Edwards, a Canadian with
a gold medal for skimming thin ice and fanciful
figuring thereon, is to arrive in time for the giddy
whirl.
Will Dyson lecturettes on “ Black and White ”
and “ The Art of Caricature ” on Thursday, May
13, at the Australian Literature Society. The
affair will be the modest youth’s first appear-
ance on any public platform, and he is
in the , throes of agonised discussion whether
tp ■ wear his hair curled or have it droop
in pale artistic lines on his brow. Furthermore,
Mr. Dyson’s picture show happens on toward June
—probably just before next Parliamentary session.
It will be a heroic facing of criticism to have the
originals of his Parliamental caricatures standing
alongside their pictures. There is some deep,
dark mystery at present about the opener of Mr.
Dyson’s show.
From “Rosna,” in Adelaide :
The S.A. Art Society’s Exhibition was biown or washed
open and privately viewed on Thursday, amid the deluge.
A larsre but very drowned multitude saw the deed well
and duly done. Mr. Sowdon was responsible for the key-
turning, and handed out some very interesting advice on
Art criticism. It appears that a person who paints red
grass and a green sky is a colorist, and one who paints
blue grass and a black sky is an impressionist; but one
who paints blue sky and green grass is merely a realist
and of no account. There are 156 pictures in the
Exhibition. Hans Hevsen hasn't one, because he is
still rusticating in the wilds of Hahndorf. There
is only one Hambidge picture—“ Intermezzo," a dainty
study of a somewhat pale maiden. Will Ashton has a
dozen of his beauteous little Sydney pictures, which
(alas!) were not sold at his exhibition t’other month.
(It is better to be a tram conductor than an artist in
South Aus.) Bessie Davidson’s “Old Servant” is the
chief figure study and the largest canvas in the show, and
is a good piece of work with fine atmosphere about it;
also she has a good portrait of a cleric. Rose McPherson
shows a kiddy cuddling a teddy-bear, and some
clever still life and flower studies, chief of which is
a corner of a breakfast table with fine light effects in it.
One of this lady’s pupils, a Salter damsel, has an am-
bitious pic ure of a group of folk under a tree, with the
sunlight filtering through. It is a thing of considerable
promise. George Webb has relinquished portraits for the
moment and migrated to landscapes, which are to be had
bv the yard, or even the dress-length, in the present
exhibition.
The turner-on of the tap up above has been behaving
in a reckless manner, and all last week, when we were
striving to wear our best chiffons for various frivols, he
just poured himself upon the battered and sodden land-
scape. On Thursday he was specialty pugnacious, and
the new electric cars had swimming matches up and
down King William-street. There was a chrysanthemum
show in the Town Hall ; and their Exes., who energeti-
cally go to everything on earth regardless of weaiher,
plunged their motor through the raging elements to the
scene of vegetation. Mrs. Chief Justice Way and sundry
presidents and things were there to assist them in the
arduous proceeding, and they took one daughter and
two aides along. There were no decorated dinner tables
this year, and the Cave ar.d Lewis trophy was snatched
by a Victorian. Afier which, we’re not going to play.
Victorians will be disqualified next year.
Gawler has had its hair in curl papers for a month or
more, but last week it took it out and burst forth in all
its splendor for the raising of the top stone of St. George’s
Anglican Church tower. There was fierce excitement.
The keystone of the tower arch was put in position 48
long years ago, away in the dusty back-tracks of 1861,
and ever since the church has been saving up its pennies
to finish the job. The thing was done with Masonic
honors, and Grand-Master Chief-Justice Lieutenant-
Governor Way performed the feat. And this is what
happened (please attend very carefully, or you might miss
some): The Grand Master delivered an address. The Grand
Chaplain offered prayer. The Grand Superintendent of
Works adjusted the stone.. The Deputy Grand Master
reported that it was square. The Senior. Grand Warden
said it was level. The Junior Grand Warden alleged it
was plumb. And the Grand Master opined-that it was
well and truly laid—same as if, it had been an egg. After
that, the Grand Bishop gave a dedication prayer ; and
the grand flag was hoisted by the Grand Trustee, and the
grand congregation went into the church. The tower is
a goodly pile, anyway. It is battlemented, is 80ft. high,
and is 26ft. square at the base and 22ft. square at the top.
Furthermore, it is made of Barossa freestone and slate.
The Cherniavski youngsters were persuaded to give
two extra concerts here—five in as many days—and if
they weren’t a-weary at the end of that time it’s a marvel.
The Bosanquet family went en manse to hear them, and
once or twice sent Mr. BJillie Hamilton behind with com-
pliments and requests for more. Tuesday night was the
boys’ 150th performance in Australia, and there were
souvenir photos and other excitements in celebration.
Now the three with their buckled shoon and their velvet
suits have gone westward.
The Lyric Club opened its doors with a crash on Wednes-
day eve, and showed its 50 odd new members to the
multitude. The word odd, I should explain, refers to the
number, not the members. There was very nearly a
panic and a dash for the street when Secretary Alec.
Morison announced that the Club had lost some 50
members last year through deaths, etc. But just as the
audience was fleeing from the tainted spot, he hastened
to explain that there had really been very few deaths,
and that most of the losses were owing to the etc. The
programme was contributed by Nora Kvffin Thomas,
who fiddled on a heavenly fiddle : pianist George Pearce,
and several melodious bards ; and the audience was large.
The Mavoress, Mrs. Mayfield, gave tea to nine miles
seven furlongs of women and a man or two the same
afternoon. It was a pleasant informal sort of reception
in the council chamber of the Town Hall. There were
bowls of gorgeous vegetation lurking about the room at
unsuspected intervals, and there were sundry pretty girl-
chums of Miss Mayfield’s also lurking about to see that
nobody went unfed and tealess. The Mayfields have de-
serted their Glenelg place and have come to dwell in
North Adelaide till the season ceases from raging.
Lorna, the second pretty Deelev daughter, has become
the promised bride of Dr. Dean Dawson, a young man
who mends the shattered frames of Lnura and the neigh-
borhood. The maiden is a nurse at North Adelaide pri-
vate hospital, so should be of material assistance to her
Benedict, in holding the chloroform bottle, and such-like
good works.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANNA.
“ Yulcan ” : The heaviest thud of the fall-
ing' Dreadnought fund occurred at Sydney Agri-
cultural Show. A huge, flaring sign was run up
in a prominent position, and sundry persons
strenuousl y pushed rattling boxes under the packed
noses of visitors for days, but, though that crowd
was 250,000 strong, and representative of every
Class of the whole State, the munificent result was
—hold your breath !— ONE pound ! And people
like Joe Cook still go on saying that the country
is stirred to the marrow and raving mad to give a
Dreadnought to the Cold Land.
The value of that arbitration system
which Toni Mann, whose occupation it threatens,
wants to do away with, was shown in some pro-
ceedings taken on April 28 in the N.S.W. Indus-
trial Court against a builder named Douzans, who
is erecting the huge structure for Mark Foy in
Liverpool-street, Sydney. Douzans was con-
victed on three charges of sweating—by employ-
ing skilled workmen at less than the minimum
allowed. Judge Heydon spoke' with refreshing
zeal to Douzans. and after hanging a sentence of
gaol without the option over the defendant’s
astonished head, fined him £7l and £2l costs.
But for the arbitration award the only remedy the
trades unions would have had would have been
the strike. And probably they would have come
out losers, and Douzans would have gone on his
cheap-labor way rejoicing.
A dark suspicion is growing up that what
really happened to Nicholas Willis was that some-
body crept up behind him in the dark, and hit
him on the head with the “ Life of Crick.” Poli-
ticians are having a bad time just now. There is
an election on in Newfoundland, and some strenu-
ous constituents have seized ex-Premier Bond and
thrown him into the sea.

NEURALGIA FOR
TWO TEARS
Acute Suffering Before the
Right Cure was Tried.
This Woman Found that the Right
to Cure
cure the Cause of the Trouble.
There are very few cases of neuralgia that are
not caused by a weak run down condition. About
the first thing the doctor notices in neuralgic
patients is the anaemic appearance. When there
is too little blood the nerves lose their tone.
That is the commencement of nervous troubles m
bloodless people. Neuralgia—a sharp shooting
pain in the facial nerves—is one disorder. Drugs
to ease the pain are like fuel to the fire. The
next attack requires bigger doses, and so on until
a drug habit is formed. Cure the original cause
—the weak watery blood, and the nerves will be
all right. Nature’s way is always to remove the
cause. Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills cure in Nature’s
way because they make new blood. That’s why
they have cured so many casesof neuralgia. They
cured Mrs. Georgina McGuckin, of 4 Cowle Street,
Perth, who states :
“When I first got neuralgia it came on gradu-
ally, and I thought it was toothache, but when
the pains came in my head also, I knew it was
neuralgia. Fits of pain attacked me and I would
collapse, and nothing seemed to relieve me.
Some days it was so bad that I had to lie down.
The pain was often so bad that I had to get some-
one to hold my head. The pressure relieved the
pain, but as soon as they took their hands away,
I had to cry out again. Day and night for weeks
I was nearly driven mad. My appetite fell off
and I grew thin and weak and was very miserable
altogether. I dreaded a noise of any sort, for it
always set the neuralgia going. Life became
hardly worth living. For two years I suffered
like this, hardly knowing a day’s peace. Then
my sister, Mrs. McDonald, told me to try Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People. She had
given them to her little boy who was run down,
and she thought that they would do me good, and
she actually brought me the first box, she had so
much faith in them. When I had finished that
box I felt so much improved that I got another.
That and another put new life into me. The
pains eased up and went away, my appetite
returned and I felt better in every way. That
was over six years.ago, and I have not had the
slightest return of neuralgia since.”
While so promptly effective these pills contain
no harmful stimulants or opiates. They are per-
fectly safe and create no drug habit. Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills are sold by chemists and
storekeepers, or sent by mail, post paid on
receipt of price, 2s. 9d. per box, six boxes 15s. 3d.,
by the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co. of Australasia
Ltd., Sydney.

POLITICAL
POINTS
NO INDUCEMENT.
Mr. Watson regarded immigration as the first line of
defence, but he attached to immigration an unjust con-
dition of land confiscation, which would cause Australia
to be far from attractive to honest and industrious would-
be settlers.—S. A. Register.
Landlord (to honest and industrious would-be
settler): "If I gave you a steady job the
Socialist Government might take all mu land
away , and then you’d be out of work.’’
Intending Immigrant : “ Then, by goom, Oi’ll
stay ’ome, bquoire/”
Thus Archdeacon Peacock, to the Warr-
nambool (Vic.) branch of St. George’s Society :
When in England he had had a long conversation with
Bishop Thornton, who told him that a few years ago he
would have regarded tariff reform as impossible, but
now he saw it coming, and realised its necessity. The
editor of a newspaper at Inverness (Scotland) had told
him that although his paper was Freetrade, he was con-
vinced that the only things to save Britain were tariff
reform and preferential trade with other parts of the
Empire. The head of a great paper manufacturing firm,
established 130 years, had told him the same thing, and
had said that unless his firm could find an outside
market it would go under.
Sydney Telegraph had recently a wild
screech about the scarcity of water in Sydney.
After all, it is a question that is easily solved.
Shift the Australian capital site back to Dalgety,
where there will be unlimited water for it, no
matter how big the city may grow, and bring the
outflow of the Cotter to Sydney. Then that city
will indeed be blessed. Scarcity will disappear,
gardens will thrive, and the weary denizens of
dust-cursed Waterloo and Newtown will have
rest at last, and spend their evenings in fern
houses, which they cannot have at present because
water is too scarce.
IT DAZZLES THE WORLD.
No discovery in medicine has ever created one quarter
of the interest that has been caused by Dr. Sheldon’s
New Discovery for Coughs and Colds. It has brought
relief in the most hopeless cases, when all else has failed.
For sale everywhere, price Is. 6d. and 3s.*
The British House of Commons has re-
jected the “Right to Work” Bill by a large
majority. It seems oplv what might be expected.
Every man has the right to have steady work at fair
wages, but He can’t have it if it isn’t there, and so
long as the ports are open to the goods of every
cheap labor country on earth, and any in-
dustry can be paralysed at short notice by
the dumping process, the work isn’t there. Also
the Labor party is partly responsible for it not
being there. Britain now thinks of trying its
hand at Wages Boards on the Victorian model as
a remedy for sweating. But so long as the ports
are open and it is necessary to undersell every
competitor there is almost sure to be sweating.
Under Freetrade fair wage is only another name
for no wage at all.
Victorian Lands Minister McKenzie, who
was sent to Maoriland to gather information for
the guidance of the Murray Cabinet, has returned
with abundant evidence of his researches. He
brings indisputable proof of the existence in the
Dominion of ordinary, graduated, and absentee
land-taxes. After much prodding in out-of-way
places, he is certain that the Maoriland Arbitra-
tion Court and compulsory purchase system are
not extinct. He has even discovered that there
are settlers and that there is a rainfall. In fact,
he has discovered everything that everybody knew
before.
N.S.W. State Premier Wade’s latest bit of
mulishness against B. R. Wise is alarming his legal
supporters horribly ; and they are considering if
Chawles Gregory isn’t too dangerous a commodity
to have about the premises. Boiled down, his ex-
planation of his treatment of Wise amounts to a
declaration that the spoils are for the political
victor. Now, if only Government supporters are
to have Government work, when the Labor party
arrives the practice will spell ruin for a lot of
Wade’s most ardent admirers. Morris Hughes,
Holman and a few others will make immense
fortunes ; but the great bulk of the fraternity will
be as dead broke as the punters at the last A. J.C.
meeting.
S. M. Herald (27/4/’O9) was much shocked
at the idea of Prime Minister Fisher getting a
dissolution if his Government went under by
reason of the Dreadnought business, because :
• • • If the Governor-General were to grant a dissolu
tion, it would amount to a challenge of the popular man-
date as expressed through Parliament.
Well, under our clumsy Party Government
system, doesn’t it always “amount to a challenge
of the popular mandate,” etc., when a Ministry
appeals to the country from an adverse vote of
Parliament ? If the Herald really believes that
this is the wrong way of doing things why doesn’t
it advocate the Elective Ministry system for a
while, and see if that would prove more satisfac-
tory ?
Victoria isn’t the only State where the big
landlord beats the public for most of the
land-tax that he ought to pay. In S. Aus.
a trustee co. owns 18 acres of land in a
suburb. Last year the council assessed the
block at £OO an acre, and the indignation of
that trustee co. was something tremendous
—so tremendous that it appealed against
the assessment, and shed several pints of
crocodile’s tears over the grasping tactics of
the council. A few weeks afterwards, when
the assessment had been reduced, somebody
vaccinated the council with an idea to secure
the block for a recreation reserve, and the
council opened up negotiations. Then the
co. promptly wiped away its crocodile tears
and demanded £125 an acre, and swore
by all the red-tape and sealing-wax in the
office that it was cutting the price in order
to get rid of the property in a lump. Of
course the deal is off ; but next year when
the assessor wanders round that way he will
have an easy job in valuing that particular
scrap of the suburb.
Sydney City Council has voted a sum not
exceeding £2l for the preparation of an address
of welcome to new State Governor Chelmsford.
It will probably be badly written ; it will cer-
tainly be badly read ; it will bore Chelmsford to
listen to it—addresses assuring a Governor of the
unswerving fealty of Alderman Jobson pall after
the fiftieth one has come to hand—and the end of
it will be the waste-paper basket. For these and
a number of other reasons a Supreme Court in-
junction should be applied for to prevent the
people’s money being misappropriated to pay to
clumsy attempts to assure a mere State Governor
re the unswerving fealty of a mere muncipal
Jobson.
The Victorian Government Statist’s live-
stock figures for the year ending March,
iys9, are not cheerful reading. They
shdw a decrease, for the 12 months, iii
every animal, with the comparatively un-
important exception of the horse. Dairy
cows are down 100,113—a truly awful
drop —and other cattle have fallen by
108,532. while sheep show a drop of over
1,000,000, and pigs 31,644. Part of the
dairy cattle loss is due to a bad season ;
but for the bulk of these decreases there
is simply no explanation, except the
accumulation of big estates and the bad
husbandry which always goes with big
estates. The drop in pigs equals over
30 per cent., and is the biggest mystery
of the lot. The pig is such an emin-
ently payable animal that his steady
decrease amounts to a horrible reflection
on the big landlord. It has been going
on for three or four years now. The
falling off in sheep is also notable. In
Maoriland, the cutting up of the big
sheep stations led actually to an increase
in the number of sheep kept. There is
something rotten in Victorian farming, and Pre-
mier Murray is hereby urged to wake up and
make a note of the fact.
“Nulla”: “Jingoree”—derived from
the same root as “corroboree”—would be a
good word to express the present Dreadnought
row. You have a “jingoree” at the beginning of
the proceedings, and, if your side wins, you have
a maffick at the end. If your side loses you have
a Jimmy Woodser instead of a maffick.
A cup of “Bournville” Cocoa is quite as sus-
taining as many of the dishes that are set before
you when travelling. It is also, of course, more
readily digested and more easily consumed—
points of importance when time "is limited. If
you could only see the beautiful Garden Village
where “Bournville” Cocoa is made you would
appreciate the fact that it must be the best.*
All who are interested in Australian
national politics should read “The Commonwealth
Crisis, by C. 11. Kirmess, the great serial now
running through The Lone Hand. Forecasting
a r ‘‘ peaceful ’ Japanese colonisation of the
Northern Territory, Kirmess traces the political
and war consequences with a masterly hand.
See that you do not miss “The Commonwealth
Crisis.”*
APROPOS RECENT DOINGS IN CHICAGO.
Only one wheat corner ever succeeded, being that conducted by Joseph in Egypt.
(From, a trail painting in a tomb at Thebes.)
Pitiless shaft directed by Sydney Sunday
Times (25/4/ 09) at Admirals Poore, Bosanquet
and other expert seamen who approve the scheme
of an Australian navy : : —
Those who talk so glibly about “ defending the coasts ”
of Australia have not even troubled to ascertain the
mileage.
Plainly, what the British Admirals in these waters
need is that someone with a profound knowledge
of Australia’s coast line (the editor of the Sunday
Times, for example) should take them round and
make them wise to things generally. There is no
one more helpful to a distressed British Admiral,
in a state of crass ignorance regarding the char-
acter and extent of the coasts which his vessels
have to patrol, than the editor of a Sydney Sunday
paper.
Private Enterprise is the two ends and
the bight of an ass. It has all along been gassing
about the necessity of developing the primary
industries, yet Elwood Mead, Victoria’s irrigation
expert, lately discovered the amazing fact that
that State regularly imports all its lucerne seed —
about 100 tons, worth £l7,ooo—although there is
nothing on earth to prevent the article being
raised on the premises. Seeing that irrigated
lucerne will yield two crops of hay and then turn
out enough seed to realise £lB per acre every year,
Mead seems justified in saying that the farmers
are living on a gold mine without knowing it.
An almost forgotten person named Bent
was resurrected into public memory in Victoria
the other day. A sub-committee of the Cabinet
reported, after exhaustive inquiry, that £.35,000
worth of land purchased by this Memory for the
purpose of establishing “improved small hold-
ings” was quite useless for the purpose. The
improved small holding was not intended to main-
tain a man and his family in affluence, but to
provide a refuge when the house-father was out of
other employment; and to fulfil this condition it
was necessary that such land should be near some
centre of population. The land the Bent gang
had purchased was anything but near a centre of
population. In fact it was nearer nowhere than
anywhere else.
It was a rather pathetic and wholly mis-
placed circumstance that just while the S. M.
Herald was in the thick of its Dreadnought col-
lection its own London correspondent, Henry
Lucy, should write to say that the whole naval
scare was a party move and a political hoax.
Said Lucy in the Herald of last Saturday :
In spite of cunningly concocted, sedulously stirred
clamor of assumed fright, everyone knows that the naval
position of Great Britain at the present moment is rela-
tively stronger than it has been since the days of
Trafalgar...
In engineering the scare about the Navy, Mr. Balfour
has suffered a succession of inconveniences.. . The in-
terval, whilst chilling to the ardor of the assault, has been
adroitly used by the Premier to set forth the case of the
Government, buttressed by the citation of a mass of par-
ticulars demonstrating the impregnable position of the
Navy.. .
These are dreadful things to say at a time when the
Herald’s Dreadnought fund is tottering on a
rather shaky off hind leg, and wants all the sup-
port it can get.

A proud nation like Britain cannot stoop to ask favors
from colonial Governments. —Melbourne Age.
ULTRA JINGO PRESS (excitedly): “See that?
Two wags of his tail to the right and one
flick round! That means , ‘ Send us a
Dreadnought, quick!’”
Those quaint bodies, the Federated Pro-
gress Associations of Victoria, held a conference
at Daylesford the other day. There was the
same futile cackling about matters that didn’t
concern them ; the same sawn-off crowing about
things they didn’t understand; but at least they
reached one solid conclusion. They “ recognised
the splendid possibilities of advertising the Com-
monwealth through the medium of tissue paper
wrapped round fruit exported to London.”
After that feat the brain-fagged delegates ad-
journed for a year.
Thus the S. M. Herald (27/4/’O9) con-
cerning the Peel River (N.S. W.) resumptions :
Resumption, as provided for under the Closer Settle-
ment Act, conserves the rights of the owners sufficiently
well, as we have seen in the Peel River instance. But-by
itself it does not secure results which can be accounted
adequate from the standpoint of the State. If the State
is to spend £500,000 of money—for the cost will be quite
that, probably, when the legal, administrative, and other
expenses are' added to the actual purchase sum—for
every 100,000 acres it resumes there is room to build up an
enormous debt, and yet not be very much farther
forward as a consequence of it. While we are glad to
think that this fine area of land on the Peel River will soon
be available to settlers, we cannot help thinkimr that re-
sumption alone will not solve the settlement problem.
And later (29/4/’O9) the Herald pursued the sub-
ject some more: —
The problem is among the most difficult we have to
solve, for, as we know, even a graduated land-tax does
not. do all it promises, though admittedly it does a great
deal.
It really looks as if we may yet see the Herald
fighting in the ranks of the big Australian party
for a progressive land-tax.
GETTING READY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Whisker to whisker in days of old the peoples strove for
power ;
Billhook, and axe, and clashing blade, and the falling
arrow shower,
War’s preparations were simple then, and they never
could take them long,
For they used to fight with any old thing that was fairly
heavy and strong.
A mace with a crown of spikes would do for a holy
crusading knight,
And a meat-axe fixed on a haft of steel was the man-at-
arms’ delight,
Road metal served in the Trojan war, and the armies of
Israel saw
In the hands of a realty capable man the force of a
donkey’s jaw.
Marathon’s field was possible once, and a lot of similar
scraps,
And Australia a thousand years ago m ght have held her
own—perhaps—
For it mattered little how dull the ears to the sounds of
approaching fate
When the drunkest king could get ready to fight as the
foeman smote the gate.
But the game is altered, and played by rules of a totally
different sort,
And it takes some years to construct a ship, or build and
equip a fort,
And the bravest heart and the strongest arm will fail in
the hour of need,
When the black death smokes at the garden gate of the
people who took no heed.
Woe to the land that is still at play when the shells begin
to fly,
Woe to the nation unfenced and bare when the wrath-
cloud dims the sky ;
Or ever her hammers have forged a gun, or her ship-
wrights have laid a keel,
Her jiride is bowed in the £ory dust and under the
foeman’s heel.
MAORILAND.
M.W.
