SPORTING
NOTIONS
Now that he has parted company with him,
manager Fitzpatrick is bearing out what was said in these
columns about Massa Johnson’s hitting power. Writer
was firmly convinced that had Johnson been the batter-
ing ram he appeared to be, Burns would have been killed
outright by .at least half a dozen of the 17 right-hand
blows Johnson landed on his jaw during the first three
rounds. Said Fitzpatrick: “One thing is sure. I never
told Johnson he could beat Jeffries. And he can’t.
Johnson simply can’t punch. He hammered a hundred
times at Burns, and it had mighty little effect.”
Johnson has announced that he will not meet
black brother Langford in London. But it is hard to see
how he can avoid standing up to Langford sooner or
later. That his brother black will challenge him to
defend his championship goes without saying, and, if he
can find the money for the side wager, Johnson simply
must fight him—unless he draws the color line. John-
son’s excuse for breaking his agreement with
Langford is that the purse put up by the National
Sporting Club, London, is not big enough for him
as champion of the world. Possibly it isn’t in proper
keeping with Johnson’s present rank and odor; but an
agreement is at least morally binding on even a champion
of the world. As said before, Johnson cannot side-step
Langford very long, and as Langford is a much quieter-
living gentleman than Johnson, the longer the scrap is
postponed the worse for the coon champion. In the
meantime there is another comet in the sky. Yorkshire
has seen the rising of a new star, and lan Hague has just
started on the road that leads to the pinnacle from which
he can challenge Johnson. “Woolwich Infant” Ben
Taylor was obliterated in short order, and then Gunner
Moir was outed at the very beginning of the first round.
Moir may not be a Johnson, but the man who can out him
in this fashion (unless it was a fluke, and of that no men-
tion is made) is dangerous. Already people are wonder-
ing if Yorkshire has produced a world-beater.
If Johnson thought he was badly treated in
Australia he must have been absolutely broken-hearted
when he got to the States. Even in British Columbia he
was given the cold shoulder ; and as soon as he set foot
in Victoria every hotelkeeper seized his book and filled it
up with bogus boarders, so that when Johnson and his
wife arrived the last room had just been taken. In fact,
he had a bigger fight to get shelter in Victoria than he
had to beat Burns in Sydney'. The halm to his soul were
the flambo.yant accounts of the way they were going to
welcome him home in Galveston, away down in Texas.
The negroes were said to be organising a welcome that
would have done Julius Caesar proud—till they’ read of a
white wife. Then a cloud came o’er the collective nigger
brow. Ham liked not this idea of a white wife. But the
dislike of the blacks to a white wife was sweet welcome
compared to the fury of the whites at the idea of a black
husband. And then the whites started to prepare a re-
ception. The price of feathers suddenly soared up.
Cauldrons were bought in haste, and the tar was kept
hot night and day, just in case Johnson came surrep-
titiously and faded away as quickly. Johnson hastily
announced that he would not visit Texas; but tried
to create a diversion by alleging that his wife
was three-parts negress. But the great outstanding
fact that she was suspected of having a white skin made
any idea of visiting Texas, unless he went there with
an army corps, quite impossible.
« * ¥ «
The Mclntosh is cutting a fearful and wonder-
ful dash in the States, and monopolising quite a lar.e
space in the columns of the papers that deal with spurt
in that country. He saw Johnson, and discovered that
the great scientist who makes his living by bruising the
countenance of his brother man is quite prepared to
attempt the putting of a head on Jeffries if a decent price
is offered for the job. Then he set to work to charm the
bulky Jeffries—who, by the way, is showing bad symp-
toms of swelled head. Wearing, above his other gar-
ments, his best Australian smile and a fur-lined overcoat,
the Mclntosh butted into Jeffries’ dressing-room. The
giant eyed him coldly, and with a grunt pointed to a
case, on which the Mclntosh set him down. “Come to
see you about fighting Johnson, Mr. Jeffries. I’m Mcln-
tosh, Of Sydney.” Jeffries grunted acknowledgment or
recognition of the fact, and Mac chewed a straw for a
moment and then went on. “I’ve seen some tall figures
mentioned in regard to this meeting, but I think most or
them have been made for newspaper purposes rather than
with a view to bringing you together. However, I’ll tell you
what I’m prepared to do, and you can think it over and
let me know your decision when it is convenient. I’ll
put up $50,000, to be divided as you agree upon between
yourselves.” “.Fifty thousand devils,” snorted Jeffries in
disgust. “No, dollars, dollars, Mr. Jeffries.” “Fifty
thousand- is that all ?” said Jeffries in an injured tone.
“Why some people up Alaska way have offered me
$200,000. Did you know that?” 2 I’ve heard it,” said
the Mclntosh, “ and if you think they can make good the
money, close with them. I’m not here to bluff or to beat
down. I figure it out that I can offer $50,000, and have
something left over for myself when the accounts are
settled. I can’t say more, as it might be beyond me to
make up if things went wrong. I won’t say less, because
I think the scrap is worth it. But that’s my figure. You
chew it over and let me know.” Then Mac went out and
bought a £3OOO motor car and, sitting in it, had his
photograph taken with a young millionaire beside him.
* * * *
Artie Cripps, Sydneyside Saxon, butted into ;
Ed. Williams, the long, limber Victorian middleweight,
at Bert Jenkins’s Cyclorama (Melbourne) the other Mon:
day night, and the result was a hard night’s labor for |
both of them. These men met for the sixth, and pro-
bably last, time, and as a result Williams is again Com-
monwealth champion in his class, with no one looming-
over the ring’s horizon who is likely to snatch the glory'
from him. The fight was clever throughout, and although
punishment was frequent and free, neither man displayed
unpleasant effects. Williams has coopered his stomach
with wads of muscle since the time when Cripps used
to beat him by smiting heavily and continuously at his
grocery and provision compartment, and the smiting on
this occasion did not prevent Edward from coming with
a great gallop at the finish, and putting both hands all
over Artie.
* * ¥ ¥
Ex-amateur middle-weight champion of Aus-
tralia. Dave Smith, and Tommy Burns’ sparring partner,
Fat O’Keeffe, put up a very willing 20 rounds’ disturb-
ance, the other night, at the Brisbane Stadium—every
place where two people meet to hit one another hard anil
often is called a Stadium now. Although Smith was
fightingfor the first’time under * ’•.> old-with-one-hand-
and-hit-with-the-other rule, impo .eel into this country
by Mr. Noah Brusso Burns, he soon had O’Keeffe’s
measure, and at the end of the commotion, had got
Patrick’s face in such a state of excitement that the
referee hadn’t to think hard to tell him that he was top
dog, or something of the sort.
* * ¥ »
Jack Arnst has been distinguishing himself
again ; and has now put up a world record by' riding from
Christchurch to Timaru (Maoriland), a distance of 112
miles in 4hr. 50inin.—equal to an average speed of over 23
miles an hour. He rode Dunlops, and was paced by a motor
cycle; and beat Mehrtens’ previous best (done in the
opposite direction) by lhr. 27min. 16sec. Of course it
must be remembered that Mehrtens’ record was put up in
competition, and without the benefit of pace. Arnst’s is
the fastest road ride accomplished in Australasia, or in
the world; and is a wonderful achievement. All the
same, writer does not think the practice of paced road
racing is a desirable innovation. But what a wonderful
family the Arnsts are—one brother, Jack, champion road
rider of the world; and the other, Dick, champion
sculler of the world, and also a mighty wheeler before the
Lord. They say there is a third brother, the baby of the
family, who is likely to eclipse both Jack and Dick, if
their opinions of him are only half correct.
* * " * *
“ Hermes ” : J. Arnst, winner of the Warrnam-
bool-Melbourne bike race in 1903, has just broken all pre-
vious records for tile Ohristchurch-Dunedin ride by cover-
ing the 247 miles in 12hrs. 22min. Starting from Christ-
church the road runs through country almost as level as
a billiard table, but after entering Otago the hills begin
to appear with more and more frequency the further the
rider gets south. Forty miles from Dunedin the bikist
encounters the Horse Range outside Palmerston—an
awful hill, with a worse descent on the southern side.
But it is within 16 miles of the southern city that the
real joy of the expedition develops. The complete happi-
ness commences at Merton, with a hill known among
local cyclists as “Kilhnug.” It might be mentioned
incidentally that one or two riders who were anything
but mugs have come to an untimely end on that
stretch of piled-up mullock which is slowly slipping
down into the sea. Rushing down a grade like
the slope of a Gothic gable, the rider strikes the little
township of VVaitati, and then he enters a Prohibition
district and commences a long seven miles steady
climb over roads that are too awful to be described
in a family newspaper. I used to ride from
Palmerston to Dunedin (40 miles) every other Saturday
night, and back again on the Sunday night, all to see my
best girl. On the last occasion I negotiated Killmug in
a blinding hailstorm, and reached VVaitati about 3 a.m.
Efforts to rouse a publican were rewarded with the
contents of a water-jug fired through the window ; but
even that was a blessing in disguise, as the water that
fell from the jug was warm compared to what poured
from the streaming heavens. On the long climb towards
Dunedin I lost my way, turned to the right too soon, and
dashing down a newly'-tnade road, sailed gracefully over
a sudden precipice about 20ft. into a creek. This was
the last straw. I roosted in a flax bush till daylight came
and revealed the machine under water. Fishing it out, I
rode back to Waitati, had a hot bath and breakfast.
Then I started for home by easy stages, and reached there
at eight next morning after passing the night at Wai-
kouiati. Next day but one I received a note from my
best girl, complaining that she was sure I didn’t love her
when I let a trifle like that keep me away after coming
so far. That was the extra straw after the last one.
Since then I have been content with a second-best girl.
Really, best girls are too exacting.
* * * *
In all human probability the Dunlop Warrnam-
bool-Melbourne road race will eventuate on October 2
this year. Owing to the pressure on road space (452
nominated last year, and 304 actually started), it is pro-
posed to limit the starters to 300 in the coming event.
The race too large to be properly handled.
E. J. McGee, a cyclist who gathered in a big
wheel race at Sydney recently, has a record of hard
graft. In 14 years McGee has taken part in 473 races—
the result being 125 wins, 49 seconds, 16 thirds, and 283
times unplaced.
* * * *
Union Rugby in Sydney on Saturday last was
strenuous ; and, incidentally, the subject of almost as
much betting as if it had been a race meeting. Glebe, in its
wild-cat match with Newtown at Sydney Cricket Ground
carried almost as much money as the favorite in the
Sydney Cup, and gave its backers a better run for their
money. All the same, this betting on matches ought to
be put down with a heavy hoof, or there will be scandals
before long. When the Glebe barracker began to wave
his coin round his head he offered odds in a lordly way ;
but the avidity with which his offers were taken produced
a sudden slump in the price, and the slump continued
till the starters, that is the players, went out at even
money. Newtown, with its premiership still fresh upon it,
naturally pawed the earth and neighed “ Ha ! ha ! ” But
Glebe, though nob so noisy, was equally eager, and
the 10,000 who looked on roared like all the bulls of Bashau
in their excitement. For right at the start, with a sud-
den charge, McKivatt led his merry men to the
Newtown line, and a wild-cat struggle commenced. The
Glebe men juggled the leather magnificently, but fast as
it was passed, the Newtowners tore theui down. One
Glebe man was actually over the line, but was hauled
back and the hall wrenched from him. Then with a howl
and a curse Newtown hurled the red-jerseyed crowd back
into neutral territory, and following up with a series of
short charges arrived at the Glebe line, where a forward
pass stopped them. Back and forward they swayed,
going down in heaps of struggling blue and red, each side
fighting for an opportunity to let the backs loose, till bv
sheer bullocking Newtown reached the Glebe line, and
out of the struggling mass a Newtown man crawled
across and scored. With Newtown leading by five to
nil. Glebe’s barrackers lost the power of speech. So
far the premiers had been on top. Almost im-
mediately the leather was set going, Newtown let
loose a series of well-sustained charges, which after
every check got closer to the goal line, and
finally swept over it. But the extra points were not
added. Then the pendulum swung back. Glebe’s back
machine got working, and with two sudden rushes scored
twice in rapid succession, but only managed to convert
once. When the almost-exhausted teams reeled off at
half-time the points were equal. The second spell
settled down to a trial of endurance. They bullocked
and wrestled, and raced up and down the field, appar-
ently perfectly balanced. Both sides strained every
nerve, but every effort was responded to, and the
result for a time was deadlock. At last Glebe’s
fullback suddenly potted a field goal and gave
his side a lead of four points; and on that field a
lead of four points seemed an impossible one. Time was
running out, and there were signs of exhaustion on both
sides. Glebe was out to win, and, having got a start,
resorted to monkey tricks to keep it. But Newtown was
full of sand, and played on with dogged resolution. It
was a sudden dribbling charge by Glebe that gave New-
town its opportunity. Farnsworth picked up from the
very toes of the dribblers, passed, and the Newtown hack
machine went through Glebe like a needle through calico.
But the kick on which the verdict of the day rested flew
wide, and Glebe won by 12 points to 11. 'Yet, on the
play, Newtown had the better part.
For the rest. Manly downed Western Suburbs
by nine to nil, and South Sydney did the same to Sydney
by three to nil. Balmain crushed North Sydney to the
tune of 16 to 11. University just managed to "beat St.
George by 14 to 13 ; and Eastern Suburbs, having the bye,
scored its competition points easily. The result is that
Glebe, South Sydney and Balmain are the first line with
four points each ; Manly, the weak team of last year, is
second line with three; Sydney, University, Eastern
and Western Suburbs are all in front of their better,
Newtown, with two points ; while the team that played
so well on Saturday has only one. St. George and North
Sydney are in the poultry line.
* • ■» *
The Soccer competition for the Rawson Cup in
and around Sydney on Saturday last supplied six rattling
games, the result of which was to give Pyrmont and
Rozelle first place with six points each. Sydney is run-
ning next with five, and Garden Island and Ultimo have
four each. With a team drawn largely from the reserves,
Pyrinont downed Balmain, after a great struggle, by two
to nil. Rozelle’s victory over Granville was much more
decisive, the goals being five to nil. Sydney crushed
Northern Suburbs by four to one, and the craftsmen of
Garden Island walloped Glebe by five to four. The sailors
of the Powerful could only draw with Western Suburbs
with a couple of goals. Ultimo beat Newtown by two to
nil.
The struggle between Pyrmont and Balmain
proved that the ancient club has not lost the cunning of
its foot. Balmain played a particularly strong team ;
but the strategy of Pyrmont in the final score proved
that the older club lias the tactics. Granville was cheap
meat to Rozelle, whose defence was impregnable and fine
; to see. Storey, the Rozelle goalkeeper, is a ’bailer on
whom there are no flies, neither does grass grow upon him.
Glebe’s disaster was a bolt from the blue. The first half
saw it leading by four to nil, and then apparently it took
to drink in the interval, or something. Anyhow, it was
very groggy in the second half, and the Garden Island
artificers trampled it under foot. It was over-eagerness
that made Northern Suburbs lose to Sydney. Its men
ran wild at the critical moment ; and Soccer is a game at
which baresark business leads todefeat. Ultimo’s wallop-
ing of Newtown was simply a case of the veterans down-
ing raw recruits. Newtown is a baby club, but a very
promising one.
The chief excitement in the Soccer circle
now is the visit of the YVestralians, who arrive in Sydney
to-day, May 6. The struggles will take place at Sydney
Cricket Ground on May 15 and 22, commencing at 3.15
p.m. Although there are 12 first-grade Soccer clubs
playing in and around Sydney every Saturday, there are
thousands who have never seen an exposition of this
prettiest-to-watch form of football. This is because the
lack of decent grounds compels the Soccer men to con-
duct their struggles in out of the way places. Practically,
the Eastern suburbs’residents have never a chance to see
a game of the classic form of football. These benighted
citizens will have their opportunity at Sydney Cricket
Ground, when the inter-State matches take place.
* * * »
It is whispered that something like a stampede
has set in among the Rugby Leaguers in Sydney ; and
that the Union is being overwhelmed with applications
for reinstatement. The Union started out to be lenient;
but the rush is said to be getting so great that the Union
players whose positions are endangered by the inclusion
of the returned rebels are getting restive. So probably
the Union will grow sterner week by week. There is no
blinking the fact that, so far, the League matches have
failed to attract the public; and the waning of public
interest and the exodus of members are ominous signs.
* * • *
As herein before observed, football is going to
boom in Victoria this winter, and the opening of the
season on Saturday gave every promise of good times,
both as to the play and the “gate.” Of the League
teams, Collirigwood, which is always slow at starting,
succumbed to Fitzroy by 61 points to 72, a result due
chiefly to slovenly passing and general casualness.
Like many next-door neighbors, these clubs waste no love
on each other, and the discussion was adorned by a
couple of cheerful scraps, one of which threatened to
engage the whole of the combatants. Essendon collided
with St. Kilda, and, after a hefty struggle, pulled
the game out of the conflagration m the last
quarter, by 54 points to 44. South went down to
Geelong per Tait’s special, arriving as usual half an hour
late, and, after friendly discourse, dragged the honors
out of the mud at the same stage as Essendon, by 53
points to 44. This seems to be the proper time to urge
Geelong - to get a move on. Melbourne encountered
Richmond, and avenged last season’s early defeat by
piling up 74 points to the other’s 63, the last quarter
again seeing the tables turned. Carlton —the mighty
Carlton —which went under only once last season,
which has three consecutive premierships to its
credit, and hoped to waltz through this tourney
undefeated, met ’Varsity, and, to the horror and
amazement of its barrackers, fell with a great bump be-
fore the upstart students. The figures were 47 to 32.
Certainly Carlton’s kicking was vile, but that of ’Varsity
was almost as bad. The fact is that the students were a
shade better trained, played as one man, and played to
avenge a game of which they were unfairly reft last
season. It is said that Secretary Worrnll’s language was
something awful ; not only was Carlton walloped by a
team of League novices in its second year, but the hopes
of rivalling Essendon’s long-ago feat of running the
season unbeaten were gone for a twelvemonth. And the
Lord knows where we’ll all be by then.
The Vic. Association’s scuffles were almost as
well patronised as the League’s. P’ran, supported by a
couple of ex-League stars and some promising juniors,
collided with last year’s premiers—’Scray—and bumped
them into the outer darkness where lie the things that
might have been. The premiers had practically the
whole of the argument up to half-time, when their tally
was 28 points to P’ran’s four; but thereafter the shop-
keeping suburb attended to the business in hand, and
steadily overhauled the others, finishing with 49 to 42.
It was a considerable feat, and entitles the performers to
the respect of all opponents. Brunswick joyously cabbed
over to Essendon, but the spare billets on the tramway have
apparently been given to extra competent ’bailers this year,
and the club that went down before Brunswick in last
season’s semi-final left the field with a comfortable vic-
tory to soothe its feelings—l3-goals 10 behinds- to Bruns-
wick’s 7 goals 9 behinds. North Melbourne was at home
to “ Ports” on its-tdelectable “ glue-pot,” and entertained
its visitors with a courteous though sloppy game, finally
despatching them to their homes with a one-point victory
to gloat over —60 to 59. The two-year-old team of Brighton
visited Williamstown and capsized it by 37 points to 27.
The blastiferous impudence of these youngsters in thus
drubbing seasoned veterans like ’Town was considered
most reprehensible and liable to lead to a bursting up of
the home or something. Brighton is evidently recovering
from the burden of the responsibility for Bent’s premier-
ship, and begins to wear a cheerful countenance and hold
its head up. The controversy between Preston and
Northcote gave the former 77 points' to the latter’s 51 ;
but these two clubs resemble Premier* Wade’s vaporings
about Yassberra, inasmuch as, except for the unpleasant-
ness they sometimes engender, they don’t count. A
cheerful episode perspired at Wesley College Ground,
where the Collegians, of the Junior Association, put up
an alleged record of 32 goals 33 behinds against Oak-
leigh’s three goals three behinds. Oakleigh would appear
to have been a trifle over-matched. Perhaps it under-
stands some other game.
* * * *
The Rugby League team (the Kangaroos)
returned from Britain by the White Star liner Suevic. Mr.
J. .1. Giltinan tripped home on .his lonesome in the
India. By taking the short cut through the Canal he
arrived in Sydney just in time to be landed comfortably in
an equity suit brought against him and Messrs. Trumpet
and Hoyle in connection with the financial affairs of the
League! The League proposes to make hay while the
sun shines by pitting the returned Kangaroos—or what
is:left of them—against the home-stayers in a couple of
matches. The League will require some money shortly;
as it was recently advertising for a paid secretary at a
salary of £250 per annum. And this Equity action
against the guarantors will require some financing.
* * * *
Queensland bowlers had hard luck Jon their
N.S.W. tour, and sadly missed the services of that
eminent trundler, Bouchard of Brisbane. The team was
a good one, but it would have been much stronger had
not business prevented many of the Queensland cracks
leaving home. Of course, a visiting team is always at a
disadvantage ; there is a lot in knowing your grass, just
as in billiards an intimate acquaintance with the table
counts for much. Moreover, this team came to a place
where bowling is at its zenith, and where the trouble in
picking the team is to know what good men to leave out.
The Walers were much impressed bv the skill and excel-
lent, judgment of Mr. Gould, and unanimously vote him
as one of the first, players of the Commonwealth. In the
absence of President Abercrombie, the Queenslanders
were led by his trusty lieutenant, Vice-President Burns.
Without a doubt bowlers are the most social sports
on earth. Perhaps it is because they include among them
so many men with long purses. Anyhow they do their
visitors proud, and the Banarialanders had a rollicking
time.
* * * *
The long-deferred match between the oldest-of-
the'-old bowlers in Strathfield and Parramatta clubs took
place on the Parramatta green, the other day. Strath-
field sent along a mighty quartette in age—Pierce 84,
Thompson 80, Cowdery 80, and Bunting 74—aggregating
318 years ; while Parramatta’s team was young by com”
parison—Johnstone 87, Callan 75, O. J„ Byrnes and Fred
Cox 73 each-a total of only 308 years. As evidence that
bowling is a pastime for old men, the Strathfielders won
the match by 22 points to 16.
* » * ,
Last week Lord XI. met and defeated
a State Parliamentary team at Sydney Cricket Ground;
and the result was a huge bunch of omens.. The Governor-
General’s team did not play the whole bunch of tricks,
but declared with seven wickets down for 208. This was
the first parallel. The Federal Parliament has never
played its full hand against the State Frights crowd, but
has always beaten it all the same. The Sydney Telegraph
headed its account of the match : “ Wade’s Has-Beens
Routed. Which is also prophetic. Wade himself was
bowled by Arnold for one—but he will probably make a
few more at the general election. When eDht of his
wickets had fallen for 129. Wade found that the rest of
his team had quietly left him in the lurch, and he was
unable to carry on. This is w*hat will probably happen
when he faces the music in reality. In the day of his
real trouble he will almost certainly discover that his
team has deserted him. Belshazzar’s dinner wasn’t in it
compared with Wade’s cricket match.
* * * *
It is evident that the Australian XI., which
opens against Notts, in England this week, is endeavor-
ing to get into the worst odor possible. In the first place,
when the Board of Control chose the responsible officers,
some of the team kicked up a fuss, and Noble was
allegedly in a bad humor because the Board asked him to
be captain. Now Manager Laver joins in a growl with a
letter in which it is alleged that the team was pushed
off bankrupt; that the choice of berths on the
boat led to trouble and squabbling among the
team ; and, finally, Laver kicks at the Board’s action
in putting its full title and monogram on the stationery
which will mostly be used by the player in writing to his
own or somebody else’s girl. W. P.'McElhone, who was
secretary of the Board when the team was chosen, rs on
the trail of Laver, and states that the manager received
£l5O, while each member received £5O besides having his
passage-money paid—preliminary sums largely in excess
of those handed out to previous teams—and that the
members of the team were perfectly satisfied with the
berths on the boat. Secretary Mac. says the Board will
finally deal with the recalcitrants when they happen back
to Australia.
The only first favorite to get first past the post
at Caulfield (Vie.) last Saturday was Eclipse in the hurdle
race. In the four flat races neither a first nor second
favorite scored during the afternoon. The hurdle event
opened the proceedings, and gave punters a delusive
sweetener, but for the following five races (including a
steeplechase) the average starting price of the winner
was Bto 1. And when a melancholy punter sits down in
cold blood to consider the methods of Isaac, whose other
name is Moses, he realises the fact that 8 to 1 on the
“ books ” would mean something very considerable on the
tote. How gorgeous are Isaac's profits when the average
price of five winners in succession4s Bto 1'
* * * ■*
The recent motor-boat race from Sydney to
Newcastle and back was not a howling success from a
sporting point of view, but it was a triumph from a
national st mdpoint, It demonstrated the fact that Aus-
tralian benzine, made from N.S.W, shale, heats the im-
ported fuel out of sight. The owner of Roxana, the
scratch boat, declares that, by using the N.S.W, goods,
he got greater speed with 20 per cent, less oil ; and that
the oil is superbly clean. Some objection has been
made to the smell ; hut when the local manufacturers
get their scrubbing plant installed it will no doubt be
completely deodorised, and give ev>n better results than
it is doing at present. And already it is proving an ideal
fuel without being scrubbed.
» * * ♦
Sergeant C. Edwards, who goes to England for
the Bisley rifle meeting at the expense of the Australian
Rifles (Sydney), is brother to Sergeant Stan Edwards,
who created much jealousy by gathering in the big rifle-
shooting prizes in Australia last year. In the “selection
shoot” (the Bisley test—running man and disc targets),
Edwards points totalled 328 ; Corporal Lungren was next
in order with 297 ; the rest of the shootists a long way in
the rear.
* * * *
A systematic effort is at last being made to yard
in the unscrupulous retailer of liquors who, for the sake
of an extra margin of profit, trades off imitations on the
unwary. Of late years, the beverage “ 0.T,” has caught
the Australian trade with a cyclonic whirl as the leading
teetotal liqueur, principally owing to the remarkable
analysts’ reports in Its favor ; and the usual imitation
and deception by would-be rivals has followed. The
“ 0.T.” proprietary, having done the decent thing in
issuing a timely warning, have a staff of Inspectors out in
all the States ready to come down with a knock-out on
ail and sundry who pass off substitutes when customers
call for the genuine, and only, “O.T.”*
TO OVERCOME INDIGESTION.
In the first place remove the cause, which is
mainly the use of improper food, eating too fast,
excessive use of alcoholic liquors or tobacco, and
sometimes excessive work or study. Get the sys-
tem down to its normal condition, clear out the
bowels, assist digestion by Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills, and the rest is simply a matter of diet.
' “Eighteen months ago I was subject to violent
attacks of indigestion,” writes Mrs. Honor
CHykson, Uralla, N.S.W.. “I could not bear
the pressure of my clothes on the body, and I
would feel as if I was suffocating. I also suffered
from defective vision and severe headaches. My
sister advised me to try Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills and pressed me to take three which she gave
me. I took them and noticed a feeling of great
relief. I thereupon purchased a bottle, and with
continued treatment I was quite cured. I always
keep them by me, and my sons generally carry a
bottle in their pockets. Whenever I "find any
symptoms of indigestion returning I take one or
two, and they put me right again almost immedi-
ately.”
, Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are not simply a
purgative which forces the food out of the body,
depriving the stomach of its nourishment and thus
starving the system ; they contain the essential
properties that are temporarily lacking in the
stomach through weakness, and complete diges-
tion and assimilation, and so restore the functions
of the digestive organs by obtaining the maximum
of nutriment from the food, which sustains the
body and assures good health.

The First Entertainment on Saturday Night was

Notice of Change in Horn Equipment
and Prices of EDISON Phonographs.
All Edison Phonographs will now be equipped with a large,
solid metal, polygonal-shaped Horn and a Horn Crane—
similar to our illustration.
The horns are extremely handsome —finished in black
with gilded decoration, and the sound production will also
be improved by this new departure.
The innovation necessitates a slight increase in the prices
of the complete machines and outfits, which now will be priced
as follows :
The Edison “Gem” ... £2 12s . 6d.
The Edison “Standard,” £5 ss. Od.
The Edison “Home” ... £7 7s. Od.
The Edison “Triumph,” £ll 11s. Od.
These prices are for the phonographs complete with the
large horn and crane.
In a few days we wi.l make the most remarkable announcement that
has ever been made regarding 1 Edison Records. WATCH THE PAPERS.

THE Kodak has simplified the
process of photography so that
now anybody can indulge in
this pleasant and profitable hobby.
The taking, developing and printing
of the pictures is so simple that
from start to finish a child may
master it.
And the Kodak secures better re-
sults, too. The Kodak Cartridge does
away with the dark room for loading
and unloading, and the Kodak Film
Tank enables you to develop in day-
light. Kodak photography is photo-
graphy with all the trouble left out.
Full details of all
Kodaks will be
supplied on request.
All Kodak
supplies are
sold at English
list prices.
The No. la Folding
Pocket Kodak.
This is an ideal instrument for the
tourist, as it folds into such a small
space. It takes pictures measuring
2jin. x 3£in. without reloading. Only
weighs 160 z., and is fitted with a
Meniscus Achromatic Lens, a Pocket
Automatic Shutter, and a brilliant re-
versible finder.

Pocket Kodak.

out.

Pure Herbal Skin Soap.
F. H. MOLESWORTH, F.C.S., F.G.S., Mem. Soc. Chem. Ind. London; Public Analyst and Assayer;
Late Lecturer In Chemistry S.A. Government and Technical College, Sydney.
Central Analytical and Assay Office, Vickery’s Chambers, 82 Pitt Street, Sydney, 21st September 1908
Messrs. H. E. KUGELMANN & CO., 199 Clarence Street, Sydney.—Gentlemen,—l have made a careful analysis of a sample of “ Kugelmann’s Pure Herbal Skin Soap,” which 1 find to be composed of
vegetable oils and fats, saponified with pure alkali. It contains no free alkali, nor any substance deleterious to the most delicate skin. The perfume and color are both of natural and harmless plant origin
neither having any trace of animal or poisonous materials. Having powerful but non=poisonous antiseptics thoroughly incorporated in the process of manufacture, this soap possesses healing properties
which will remove diseases of the skin and restore it to a healthy condition. It is a beautifully finished article, comparing favorably with similar imported soaps, and if used regularly as an adjunct of the
toilet, it will assist in keeping the body in health, and will have a pleasing, soothing effect on the skin. Yours faithfully, F. H. MOLESWORTH, F.C.S., F.G.S. M.S Cl. London Analyst

SOAP is fast achieving a position before

his treatment. In short, two great principles
form the solid base of this Plerbal Soap :
1. That ABSOLUTE PURITY is essential in
any article of constant application to that delicate,
vital organ, the Skin.
2. That soap affords one of the readiest mediums
for the medical treatment of the human body.
The HERBAL SKIN SOAP in its present
form represents the fruit of nearly forty years’
experience in the use and prescription of beneficent
herbs for the cure of Australian maladies, and,
therefore, is better adapted to the climatic and
other conditions of Australian life than any
imported soap.
For the Prevention of Redness and Skin Irrita-
tion of all kinds, the cure of Eczema, Tetter,
Ringworm, Shingles, Blotches, Pimples and
Freckles; the prevention and cure of Dandruff,

and making it soft and glossy. As a dentifrice, it
arrests decay and produces a dazzling brilliance.
For Shaving, is of Unique Benefit, owing to its
production of a rich, lasting and creamy lather, and

upon the skin.
In the nursery its beneficent effects upon the
tender skin of infancy obviates the use of powder,
etc., the child’s skin being always kept cool and
smooth by its use.
“ Beauty’s Aid ” is the well-earned title of this
Herbal Skin Soap, because, in a healthy and per-
manent manner, it enhances natural beauties as
no mere nostrum can ever hope to do. In every
department of the toilet, and for every medicinal
or hygienic purpose for which soap can be used,
this soap has proved superior to any other soap.
The Greatness of the Herbal Skin Soap is the

14-1 G Queen-street, Melbourne.
Dear Sirs, —About four years ago, when I first
had your Herbal Skin Soap brought under my
notice, I was almost totally bald ; in fact, I hail
no hair at all on the top of my head. I had tried
all kinds of so-called “ Hair Restorers,” but they
did me absolutely no good whatever ; in fact, I
was losing even the little bit of hair I still had.
About June, 1902, when I was employed at the
“ Opera House ” hy the great Australian Entre-
preneur, Mr. Harry Rickards, a friend, as a great
favor, gave me a box of your Herbal Skin Soap.
I used to wash my head with it, and after a
while I noticed that my hair had stopped falling
out. Up to this time 1 did not attribute the stop-
page to using your Soap, but at last I could not
think of anything else that was stopping my hair
from falling, so I started to rub the lather well
into my head every day. I used to rub it well
in every day for about half an hour, and at last I
noticed a thick down appearing all over my head,
which had been as “ bald as a badger ” before, and
in a little while my hair was as thick as ever, and
has remained so ever since. I may also state that
my wife finds your Herbal Skin Soap unequalled
for the Complexion, while I can say the same for
it for Shaving. I will always be pleased to give
any further information re your marvellous Herbal
Skin Soap to any enquirer, as I cannot speak too
highly of it. (Signed) HARRY AIREY.

Victoria, this sixteenth day of July, 1907.)

Heat. She informed me that the first application
soothed the unpleasant irritation, and that, after
using it two or three times, the inflammation
entirely disappeared.
I have also used it for something from which we
suffer out here-in the “Never Never ” country,
where vegetables are scarce and practically un-
obtainable, and which bushmen know as the
“ Barcoo Rot”—an angry irritant sore, that
spreads over the surface of the skin when it
receives a scratch or a slight wound, and declines
to heal for some time, even under the influence of
Carbolic Ointment and a course of Blood-Purify-
ing Mixture. In this connection I have found the
Herbal Skin Soap marvellous in its healing and
soothing qualities. I have also used it as a wash
for dressing festered wounds, for which purpose it
has shown marked curative medical healing proper-
ties, and is undoubtedly a boon to the public. I con-
sider that for Eczema or other diseases of the Skin
it is without an equal, and that when its virtues
become known and appreciated by the public it is
likely to oust the imported Skin Soaps from the
Australian market.

its pores from impurities, and guards it from the
dangers of disease. It frees the Skin, also, from
Blotches, Pimples, Sunburns and Blemishes, etc.
I can honestly recommend it to mothers when
bathing their little ones, as it allays all irritation
and strengthens the Skin. (Signed) MATTHEW

SEVENTY-FIFTH ANNUAL REPORT OF THE DIRECTORS OF THE BANK OF AUSTRALASIA,
with the Accounts for the HALF-YEAR to 12th OCTOBER, 1908, presented to the Proprietors at the
Annual General Meeting, held on Thursday, Ist April, 1909.
REPORT.
The Directors submit to the Proprietors the Balance Sheet as at 12th October last, with the Profit Account for
the half-year to that date. After providing for rebate on bills current, for British and colonial rates and taxes, and
for all bad and doubtful debts, the net profit for the half-year amounted to £187,691 3s. lOd.
To this sum must be added £16,104 Is. lOd. brought forward from the previous half-year, making a total of
£203,795 ss. Bd., out of which the Directors have declared a Dividend for the half-year at the rate of 12 per cent, per
annum, or £2 Bs. per share, and a Bonus of Bs. per share, free of income tax.
The Dividend and Bonus will absorb £112,000, and the Directors have further appropriated £60,000 to the
Reserve Fund, and £15,000 in reduction of the cost of bank premises. There will then remain £16,795 ss. Bd. to be
carried forward to the next account.
The Dividend and Bonus will be payable in London and in the colonies on 2nd April.
The Directors retiring by rotation are :—The Rt. Hon. the Earl of Lichfield, Sir E. Montague Nelson, K.0.M.G.,
and the Rt. Hon. Sir Andrew R. Scoble, K.C.5.1., K.C., who, being eligible, offer themselves for re-election.
The Auditors, Messrs. Welton, Jones and Co., also offer themselves for re-election.
O. E. BARNETT, Chairman.
4 Threadneedle Street, London, 22nd March, 1909.

QUEENSLAND
The Queen State of
the Commonwealth.
WONDERFUL WONDAI LAND,
WHEN you buy a farm, what do you look for? We take it that you
want well-watered land, rich and fertile, producing abundant crops
of maize, potatoss, lucerne aid fattening grasses ; that
you wish to be near a railway, that you require long terms and land at low
prices without survey or residential conditions, with a good rainfall and well
watered. You will, therefore, be exactly suited by acquiring a farm on
“Moiuhtre” Estate, Wondai —Kingaroy District, Queensland.
We are now subdividing this fine property of 21,000 acres into areas to suit
purchasers. The average rainfall is 31 inches. A permanent creek runs
right through the property, and water may be had at shallow depths in any part.
This Estate has for many years enjoyed a great reputation as
first-class fattening, agricultural and dairying country. Lucerne,
maize, paspalum, potatoes, and rich fattening grasses are produced there in
abundance.
“ MONDURE ” is only 4 miles from Murgon Railway Station,

During the year from 31st March last a Branch has been opened at Loxton, on the River Murray,
and Agencies at Lochiel and Warooka, and the Branch at Tarcoola closed.

to apply £30,000 of the Bank’s funds towards the formation of a Provident Fund for the benefit of the

of Bank Premises Account.

oth May, 1909, at 11 o clock a.m., at which the Shareholders’will be called upon to elect Two Directors
in place of Alexander George Downer, Esq., and Arthur Waterhouse, Esq., who retire by rotation,
but are eligible for re-election, and offer themselves accordingly. The Shareholders will also have to
elect Two Auditors for the ensuing year, and fix the remuneration of the retiring Auditors.

THE BIG BLOCK,
BRISBANE
IN BANANA LAND
The week ending April 24 was a record one
for Bananaland in the matter of land selection.
The total area taken up was 525,4G4 acres, and
comprised 23 grazing selections, 145 agricultural
selections, and 22 prickly pear selections. Most
of the grazing selections were in the Barcaldine
and Longreaclr districts; Nanango topped the
list of agricultural selections; and Dalby con-
tributed nine of the prickly pear lots.
Senator McColl, of Victoria, who went to
America as the Australian delegate to the Dry.
Farming Congress or Dry-Rot Congress or some-
thing, has written to the Queensland Minister for
Agriculture concerning an interview he had with
Luther Burbank on the subject of prickly pear.
Burbank says that the pear is a blessing, not
a curse. He has managed to grow a number
of spineless varieties of it, and reckons that
even with the spines ifc is a valuable plant.
From it he has made alcohol, both for drinking
and industrial purposes, and the fibre, he alleges,
is one of the best things he knows for making
paper. He says that the paper is too good for
ordinary work, but is just the. thing for bank
notes and similar purposes. He declares he has
brought the fruit to such a state of perfection that
no ordinary human being need turn up his nose at
it if it wereputon the table before him. Burbank
claims to have grown 100 tons of fruit on an acre.
Ostrich-farming threatens to look up. If
the experiences of ex-Surveyor Behan, of Jericho,
in Central Queensland, are a guide, there is
money in the game, even in those parts which
some people call “the desert.” Although Behan
lacked practical knowledge at the beginning of
his ostrich career, the fluffy female handed over
to him 120 eggs during the.year, and 75 of them
were fertile. A good many of the eggs perished
while still in infancy (the egg that the gods love
dies young), but 29 chicks survived and started to
lay on their own account) and from the original
parents and their children and grandchildren there
iiave already come the material for many feather
boas and innumerable Merry Widow hats.
APPENDICITIS.
When Natnrei evoluted man from the beast
there remained a useless bit of duct (some rem-
nant of a one-tiftie necessary organ) attached to
the intestine. A tiny grain of undigested matter
may find its way into this “sac,” and appendi-
citis hurries another visit to the operating table or
the grave. But Nature atones for her oversight
bv oroducing a positive preventive in the shape of
FRANZ JOSEF Natural Aperient Water. A
mild, pleasant dose before breakfast cleanses the
system, tones the stomach, stimulates the liver,
and by draining off all foreign matter from the
system, removes all danger of the dread disease.
“ Beware of manufacturing a drink out of
your prickly pear,” said the hard drinker, who
had sampled the liquors of all nations. “It’s of
the cactus breed, and there is no doubt its juice
will ferment, but it’s the most hellish fluid known
to man. I’ve seen the Malays of Gaupil Island
drink it—they call it gagus. A gagus drinker loses
the power to walk Or stand ; his bones simply
shrivel and dry up, and he withers away. Opium
and whisky are mere coffee and tea compared to
gagus. Of course, you may be able to chemically
counteract the noxious principle, but that will
raise the price of the stuff, and the cheap drunk
will still consume it in its raw state. It’s cheap
enough. It might cost a whisky drinker 10s. to
see one small snake. A gagus-fiend can see a ter-
race of boa-constrictors for 6d.”
Another inventor, Mr. A. Rigby of
Mackay, has arisen in the land. His patent
machine supplies whisky and the like on the
penny-in-the-slot plan. So the publican can first
measure the spirit contents in the morning, and
can see at a glance during the day how much has
disappeared, and then he can count up the cash
when the shades of evening fall and strike a
balance. Barmaids will not be wanted with this
automaton. It will not be welcomed by the man
with the purple blossoms on his nose, because he
cannot coax the implement to give more than the
regulation nobbier, and it will be a cold sort of an
affair .to’Arry who loves to mash Flossie Beer-
pump, and who can’t make love to a mere
machine. But it will pay the publican. Still, he
will need a mastiff on the premises to prevent the
dead-beat bolting with the whole caboodle.
AN EARLY BEGINNING.
LITTLE PAT PLUNKET : “Hav’ yez got a job ov wurrk yez c’d give to a mahn?"
COCKIE : “ Well, I dunno. Gan yer milk?"
PAT: “Milk, is it? Sure ’Us the firrst thing I iver did in me loife.”
The Courts have showered a new blessing
upon the hotel lodger in Bananaland, though he
is yet without his vote. According to a Full
Court decision, against which leave to appeal to
the High Court was refused, he can on a Sunday
take away from the hotel liquid refreshments
wherewith to quench the thirst of friends outside.
For 25 years he didn’t know this blessed fact and
his friends went dry, and he saved the expense of
shouting for them on the Day of Rust; but a
Boonah resident at last perceived the real inward-
ness of the situation and backed his opinion. The
hotel lodger is now a very popular man in hip
district.
If Premier Kidston gets his way, Banana-
land’s Dawson Valley, a province as big ns Scot-
land, will shortly be roused to life by a railway.
There are soil resources in the Dawson Valley
sufficient to keep busy all the primary producers
of Bananaland ; coal deposits enough to whirl all
the industrial wheels of the Continent; and
enough trees, unless the community is an in-
curable tree-eater, to supply' a nation. Mount
Morgan can utilise coal to the extent of 50,000
tons a year as soon as the Government is ready to
supply the article from the State mines. For
that reason the line will go through Mount Mor-
gan and down the Dee Valley, and then rush
into the centre of the new country and there raise
the industrial devil.
Walkers!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Walkers Limited, of Maryborough, completed one
desirable century, the other day, when it turned out its
hundredth locomotive made for the Bananaland Govern-
ment.—The Bulletin, S/4/’O9.
This is a ballad of Walkers, fashioned with much
applause:—
While the land was burdened with Talkers, work-
ing their facile jaws ;
Weeping at loss of their nigger ; crying to south
and north,
Walkers, with skill and vigor, were sending their
engines forth !
While Kidston and Philp were sounding tocsins of
wrath and war, \
Walkers were busy pounding the steel by the
furnace door.
Smiting with strong steam-hammers, toiling with
lathe and drill—
Walkers, amidst the clamors, stuck to their busi-
ness still.
Boring the cylinders truly, forging the throws and
shafts—
Hail, says the Creed raised newly—hail to the
hand that g afts !
Shaping toe drawbars squarely, turning the
pistons true;
Seating the slide-valves fairly—labor of brain and
thew !
Engine and linked tender, taking the tracks with
ease ;
Hail to the hands that render services such as
these !
Hail to the smith and draftsman, raising the
engines tall ;
Cheers for the faithful craftsman—cheers for the
workers all 1
Rivet and chuck and spanner—iron and brass and
steel;
These are the White Man’s banner, flag of the
Commonweal !
Planer and vice and hammer, anvil and flaming
forge—
These with their strength a-clamor have levelled
the mountain-gorge !
Binding the lands together, fastening North to
South ;
Dragging with steel-strong tether food for the
workers’ mouth !
Helping Australia forward—yea, in the blood-rod
day,
Aiding the Nation war-ward, paving the Nation’s
way !
This, then, a ballad of Walkers, I for Australia
sing ; ■
Not of the Countless Talkers flapping a ceaseless
wing !
A psalm for the craftsmen loyal, launching the
engines true —
A chant for the breed right-royal, for the name-
less foundry-crew !
Simply an anthem votive, merely a Muteship’s
call;
Hail to the Locomotive—Hail to the Builders
All !
Riveters, hammerers, caulkers—workers of each
degree—
I sing you this ballad of Walkers —a song in a
major key.
VIC.
GRANT HERVEY.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, May 1, 1909.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
The hitching of Geo. Love Warry, who in his
spare moments dabbles in the gentle vegetable,
and Miss Elizabeth Annie Heal, who is blessed
with many of this world’s goods, was accomplished
at All Saints’ Church on Wednesday. The event
fairly had genteel suburbia “off its ’andle.”
Wickham Terrace was blocked by a struggling
mass of curious femininity, and the bride, decked
in a cash value of about £4OO worth of clothes and
jewellery, had to be assisted through the crowd by
the stalwart arm of the law—several of it. The
blushing bridegroom had made a contract with, the
best man to the effect that the latter should nudge
him when it was time to say “I will,” and
thereby he got into a bog. For the best man,
seeing him put his foot down firmly on the bride’s
train, nudged him as a hint to move it, and he
promptly signified his willingness to take on a
wife several minutes too soon. Another nudge,
meant as a hint to stop saying “I will,” merely
prompted him to repeat his ill-timed remark, and
after that it got on his nerves, and he said it
“ good and hard ” whenever the minister paused to
take a breath. I am told that the breakfast
at £1 a head was knee-deep in joy, and the
scene at the railway station beggared descrip-
tion. (Description has so often been beggared that
once more doesn’t matter.) The enthusiasm rose
to fever heat, and the air was full of waving hand-
kerchiefs and sundries. Next day 15 clubites
lunched with wet bandages round their heads,
they having applauded till the roof of the
dome of thought felt as if it would fly off.
“‘The last thing I remember,’’ said one cheery
citizen, “was getting out of the carriage window.”
The sceneat All Saints’ was summed up by auntie’s
Washer-lady on Thursday thus : “Ah ! Miss
Amelia, it wor’ a gran’ sight ! And the crowd—
whv it took the virgin all he knew ter get the
bride inter the porch at all, at all ! ”
The tuneful Ada Crosifey has come again and
gone like a meteor in the night. Shearrived at seven
p.m. Thursday, dived intoan emerald green confec-
tion, and wended her way to the Exhibition Build-
ing at eight. There a big crowd gave* her welcome.
The performance was notable for its brightness,
and this in' spite of the fact that the co. was so
dead tired with much travelling that Percy
Grainger, of the golden locks, had to be wakened
before each of his appearances. Ada received a
rose-decked boomerang from # Mrs. Bland Holt
(who, being out of “The Bondman” cast,
was enjoying herself in a front balcony seat),
and a quantity of other vegetation. I left
Sametini pouring water on Percy to induce him to
wake and go home, the pianist having dropped
asleep again on two chairs. Lady Chelms-
ford, the John Forrest couple, Verney and his
smile, the chief Federal Justice and daughter,
Mr. .Justice O’Connor, and many other notables
were in the audience.
On Wednesday evening there was a record
Liedertafel audience to welcome back to Brisbane
Mrs. Gilbert Wilson, and that graceful lady was
nigh overcome by the public enthusiasm. Her
beautiful voice is only more lovely for its tour,
and she now lets loose a dainty trill. It’s pleasing
to hear the full, round notes again. I’m game to
bet all my current liabilities that the Common-
wealth can show nothing better in the vocal line.
Our returned soprano clothed herself in a flesh-
tinted satin model from Caulet Soeurs. with won-
derful embroideries defining its d irectoire propor-
tions, and panels, sleeves and decolletage of lovely
blonde. The contributions of the society already
show the capable training of conductor Kaye.
Its work, with Dr. Beorne, M. 8., at the piano, in
a Beethoven piece, was the best thing the
orchestra has done for years.
There died on Thursday Brisbane’s first
W.C.T.U. president, Mrs. F. T. Brentnall, who,
in spite of 10 years’ invalidism, was a prominent
worker in the cold water cause. The old lady
was descended from a notable Scotch Highland
landowner with a kilt, who wielded a claymore
under the Young Pretender, and had the ancestral
shack and building lots annexed by the Crown
(which was Geo. II.) in consequence. Mrs.
Brentnall leaves two daughters. One of them —
Mrs. C. B. Harris—-is about the finest platform
orator we possess.
Hooray ! The taxi-cabs are out at last, a.nd
their smart grey and crimson fittings are the joy
and the comfort of all who climb into them. They
are the smartest things imaginable, and far nicer
than those in either London or Melbourne, which
are obscure backward places and of no account.
The Governor has been shedding his vice-regal
light upon the Kingaroy show, and narrowly
escaped a railway smash on the way down. We
really mfist not smash up your prospective pro-
perty if we can help it. Her Ex. was at the
annual display of the Needlework Guild, at the
Frank Corrie Villa, on Thursday, where 15,000
garments for the lame, halt and blind were on
view amid the tea and cake.
There’s no doubt that rinking is a levelling in-
fluence. One night recently our very smartest
staff officer went a-rolling, and when the collision
was over he found himself in the arms of the
cook who grills his flesh at the hash-shop.
Yours affectionately,
MARIG.
At Wan-ill Creek (Q.), the other day, 46
bullocks —two teams knocked into one —hauled a
queer sort of load. It consisted of a house, with
its furniture, pictures, hangings, etc., in their
usual places ; and the shift was about a mile.
During the trip, which took nearly three days, the
owner and his family lived in the house, had their
meals as usual, and slept in the usual beds the
two nights they were on the voyage.
“ Quan ” : Be the stone fish (B. 15/4/’O9).
It is common all along the Queensland coast, but is
not as plentiful in the South as in the North. One
was caught only the other day in Cabbage-tree
Creek, near Sandgate (Brisbane). It is a sluggish
sea-brute, and either hides among rocks and weeds
of the same color as itself, or buries itself in the
mud. The strong dorsal spines are each provided
with a poison sac, which is situated about the
middle of the spine. When an unfortunate bare-
footed fisherman treads upon one of these
iniquities the spines stick into his foot, and his
weight forces the poison into the wound. Fre-
quently the tips of the spines break off and remain
in the foot. The agony sometimes lasts for weeks.
The beast’s scientific name is Synanceja horrida.
He is evidently a relation to Sultan Abdul Hamid,
of Turkey. Abdul and the fish show the same
characteristics.
“ B.B.” : Once a month Townsville shakes
the dust from its eyes to watch the arrival of
immigrants from the Cold Land by the Torres
Straits steamship service. Some 400 a month are
coming to hand, and the last batch included 50
girls for domestic service. Three ships have now
arrived with immigrants, and each one has
brought an extra passenger placed aboard by the
Stork when in mid ocean. The latest arrival from
Storkland rejoices in the title of Hannah Ripping-
hum Grange Deitz.
If -usually reliable men are not pulling this
country’s leg, the mountain plum of Papua is well
worth acclimatising in Queensland, and probably
further south. The fruit is described as an inch
long, greenish-yellow in color, and wholesome
eating. The Jardine plum-apple of Albany Island
and the Cape York Peninsula is another edible
that is worth looking up. It is a stone fruit,
about the size of a small orange, with a quarter
inch of crisp pulp surrounding the kernel. The
pulp is good eating even now, and in skilful hands
might very likely be made better.

THERE ARE MARVELLOUS POSSIBILI-
TIES IN MAIZE.
Queensland To-day: the Queen State
of the Commonwealth.
No. XVIII.
FORTUNES IN MAIZE.
By J. D. Fitz Gerald.
The climate and soil of Queensland are peculiarly
suited for the cultivation of maize, and the state-
ment of that fact opens up a view of the possi-
bilities of the wealth which will yet be obtained
from the corn crop in the northern State. The
value of the Commonwealth maize crop in 1906-7
was £1,326,071, and of this large sum Queensland’s
quota was £462,922. In that year there were
139,860 acres under maize in Queensland, which
was an increase over the previous year of 26,086
A REGIE NEB’S PROSPECTS IN MAIZE AT GATTON,
SOUTH QUEENSLAND.
acres, and was the largest acreage sown with this
cereal since the settlement of the State. This
comparatively small acreage is but the germ of
the great industry that is to be. The markets of
the world invite Queenslanders to pour the
yellow grain into the holds of ships, for
in Mark-lane (again using an urban name
to express the central pivot of the world’s mar-
kets) there are minted yellow sovereigns waiting
to be exchanged for the yellow grain. And behind
the middlemen of Mark-lane there are the hungry
consumers waiting for the world’s elevators to
suck up the ripe grain from the ships’ holds
and convey it with all speed into use. As the
bloated populations of the Old World increase,
their lands do not increase proportionately. As
transport is hastened, as distance is annihilated,
as handling is cheapened and expedited, so the
position of the Queensland grower is being im-
proved and his enterprise is being encouraged.
So urgent is the need of the great populations of
the Northern Hemisphere, and so rapidly does
improvement in handling and transportation pro-
ceed, that a maize-grower in the Southern, Cen-
tral, or Northern District of Queensland is almost
as favorably situated as regards his final market
as the farmer of an English county, a German
principality, or a French department.
For maize is in demand throughout the world.
Every farmer likes maize-growing. It is an
“easy crop.” In a climate like that of North or
Central Queensland it should, when its value is
realised and its culture scientifically treated, be-
come one of the greatest, 'if not actually the
greatest, source of the State’s wealth. That this
is no exaggeration will be seen from the Ameriean
figures which will be quoted presently. In 1906-7
the maize production of the Commonwealth was
as follovys
State. Bushels.
New South Wales 5,763,000
Victoria 704,961
Queensland .. 3,703,274
It will thus be seen that, in proportion to popula-
tion, Queensland is the largest grower of maize in
the Commonwealth. With over a million and a
half of people, N.S.W. produced less than six
million bushels ; while Queensland, with a million
less people, produced nearly four million bushels.
It can confidently be predicted that within a
measurable distance of time, Queensland will be
the largest producer for home, Commonwealth
and foreign use of all the States of Australia.
We may continue on this assumption, viz., that
Queensland is destined to be the greatest pro-
THRESHING MAIZE AT LOGAN’S, FOREST HILL, SOUTH QUEENSLAND.
j ;
dimer of maize in the Commonwealth. Her
destiny is fixed by her geographical position, her
climate, the qualities of her soil, and the nature of
her other primary industries. For maize-growing
is an adjunct to these industries. A few words
from the 1906-7 report of Mr. E. G. E. Seri veil,
Under-Secretary of the Queensland Department
of Agriculture and Stock, will give an idea of the
possibilities of maize farm-
ing. Writing in Septem-
ber, 1907, he says :
The area under maize fluc-
tuates in a degree that should
not be, when, in addition to
its principal use as feed for
horses, there is the great
demand in connection with
dairying. In 1896, the first
year of the decade ending in
1905, the area stood at 115,715
acres. During the two fol-
lowing years it fell to 102,835
acres, and in 1900 rose to
127,974 acres, and to 150,95 S
acres in 1904. This rise was
succeeded by a drop of 31,000
acres, to be followed by an
increase in area to 139,806
acres—the largest area in one
year, excepting 1904, since
1896. The average returns
have seemed to follow the
area under cultivation, for,
during the decade to 1905
the average for 1896 —26'49
bushels to the acre—was not
surpassed. In 1906, how-
ever, 32 45 bushels to the acre
were obtained, and it is to
be hoped that we shall not
again see a diminishing harvest
of this plant, which is valu-
able as a feed to man and
beast, and to the latter in
stalk as well as grain.
The “ easy crop ” nature
of maize, and its value
in mixed farming—which
is the bonanza of the
“small man”—are well
known in Queensland ;
but they are not realised
so keenly as they are in
Canada and the United
States. That may be
noted by the immigrant
in calculating his chances
on arrival in Queensland.
In no country in the world
is the value of maize
farming more clearly ac-
knowledged than in the
United States. If America
can be said to have a
truly national dish —or
rather two national dishes
—they are “young green corn and pumpkin pie.”
The dishes are the by-products of the one in-
dustry. For pumpkins are the fitting accompani-
ments of the corn crop. The pumpkin vines and
the corn stalks grow together on the same land.
Than young green corn, as the Americans treat it
—boiled and served with butter, pepper and salt
—there is no choicer dish. Lucullus himself
FISHER'S IMPHEE, LAKE CLARENDON, SOUTH QUEENSLAND.
might have enjoyed it, and Vattel have been
proud to cook it. And the proper accompaniment
of such a gourmet’s delicacy is the pumpkin pie,
with ground ginger and delicate paste. No
wonder that there is a “great pie belt” in the
United States. But the appeal which the corn
crop makes to the farmer—and especially the
small farmer—is that it is the easiest sown and
the easiest harvested of all crops. It may be
planted in Queensland any time from July
to January. It is ready for harvesting in
about 120 days. When ripe it is plucked
like a fruit. It is easily husked, and shell-
ing is done by small machines. Two crops
a year may be grown in certain districts, in
sub-tropical scrub and coastal country. A few
more sentences from Mr. Scriven’s report will
bear transcription :
The use to which maize is
put, apart from food in its
natural state, in other coun-
tries, indicates that greater
use could be made of it here
with the consequent greater
demand. From some varieties
cornflour is manufactured.
Starch is also produced, which,
with the addition of acid,
is turned into glucose. The
stalks, besides being used
for food for stock, are used
for thatch and fuel, and pro-
duce valuable fibre, which is
spun into durable yarn.
Stalks of maize are also used
for paper-making. They also
yield a syrup from which
an excellent spirit can be dis-
tilled. The grains yield an
oil which is much used in in-
dustries.
In this State, however, not-
withstanding the many uses
to which this plant can be
put, it is practically con-
demned to but one of them
—viz., feed for stock. In the
United States of America it
is viewed with a very dif-
ferent interest, for of the
world’s production of about
3,200,000,000 bushels, that
country produces nearly three-
fourths ; in 1905 the harvest
amounted to more than
2,625,000,000 bushels, and yet
it is probable that, compara-
tively, Queensland is more
adapted to this cereal.
We may, perhaps, realise
the possibilities of maize
by a few comparisons of
the production generally.
Take these figures :

in the latter than the former in 1906; while one pro-
vince of Canada produced more than double the
• quantity that the Commonwealth produced, not-
withstanding the suitable temperate, and tropical
climate of the latter. To show the almost in-
credible development which has gone on in this
industry in America, I take eleven of the States
of the union, which are among the biggest pro-
ducers of maize. The combined area of these 11
States is the same as Queensland, viz., 670,000
square miles. Their combined production of
maize is 1,808,251,000 bushels. Here are the
figures :

Thus a State like Missouri, the territory of which
could be put into Queensland nine times comfort-
ably, and ten times with a squeeze, produces 62
times as much rnaize as Queensland ; while lowa,
which goes 12 times into Queensland, produces
370 million bushels more maize per annum, on the
1906 basis, than does our giant northern State.
That we may gather some idea of the value of
the maize industry, we turn to the annual report
of the United States Department of Agriculture,
and we find that the total production of maize in
the United States in 1906 was 2.927,416,091 bushels,
and the value £291,631.619. This immense pro-
duction —which, expressed in either dollars or
pounds sterling, appears to be a fabulous sum—
amounts in one year to a sum equal to one-third of
the National Debt of the United Kingdom. This
huge sum was earned by the cultivation of an area
of 96,737,581 acres, or approximately one-fourth the
area of Queensland. The American boasts, as I
have already quoted, that “the State of Illinois
alone produces half as much corn as is grown in
all the world outside the United States.” It is a
proud boast, and we Australians, in a young and
undeveloped country, with our rich soil and
benignant climate, may well envy our American
cousins their success in wresting from their soil
this rich plunder. Our envy, however, is not of
the gangrened sort, but rather that of emulous
ambition; for, with the advantages which we
possess, there is nothing to prevent us also win-
ning great wealth in our maize crop.
W. H. Traill, in that interesting work, “A
Queenly Colony,” expressed a strong opinion of
the value of maize as a crop suitable for the class
of small settlement which is going on in Queens-
land, and to which the immigrating classes of
Europe are invited. He quotes William
Cobbett, as showing that, in his time,
in America, “all the -hogs and pigs, all
the poultry of every sort, the greater
FIRST CROP, MAIZE AND PUMPKINS, LAKE
CLARENDON DISTRICT, SOUTH QUEENSLAND.
v 'part of the oxen, and a considerable part of the
sheep, are fattened upon corn ; it is the best feed
for horses, and when ground and dressed in
various way, it is used in bread, in puddings, and
in several other ways in families; and that, in
short, it is the real staff of life in all countries
where it is the common culture, and where the
climate is hot.”
A leading case is to hand, showing how the
pioneer of the ’sixties began his success with
maize. Mr. James Deerain, pf Bromelton,
Beaudesert, already quoted, took up land at
Bromelton, and turned his attention to maize and
potato growing, and, “about the fourth year,”
he says, “ maize was bringing a good price, and I
planted about 50 acres in a belt of scrub along
the river bank. This was all planted with the
hoe, and produced a very heavy crop—over 80
bushels to the acre.” With maize at 3s. per bushel
(its price in 190 G, and it would probably have been
much more in 1872), the settler would have
realised £6OO from his 50 acres of river frontage.
But the days of the 80-bushel yields are not over
yet. On Laidley Creek, Mr. A. Hunter has
grown 80 bushels to the acre, and Mr. P. H.
McGrath 100 bushels to the acre.
To give the intending emigrant a slight idea of
the variety of mixed farming, in connection with
which maize is an adjunct, the 1906 returns from
the railway station at Forest Hill, a comparatively
newly settled district opened to small settlers in

Enough has been written to show the possi-
bilities of wealth in the golden corn harvest.
When the results of wealth development through
maize crops in the United States are taken into
consideration, we must come to the conclusion
that individual fortunes and a great advancement
of the corporate wealth lie in our taking up this
cereal in deadly earnest. Had the 1906 crop in
the United States brought the price which maize
brought in the Commonwealth, the value would
have reached the enormous total of £439,112,413,
which would have been equal to the sum required
to build 219 Dreadnoughts, allowing two millions
for each of the battleships.

“ Melb.” : St. George’s Hall reopens, with Hoyt’s newly-
imported biograph. New York has 300 piograph shows,
so there should be room for one more in Melbourne.
Here we have West’s, Tait’s, and the Bijou every night.
Though St. George’s Hall has been closed so long, it has
been fashioned entirely according to the Board of Health
requirements, and is a fine, substantial building. The
ramshackle shops in front were demolished, with astonish-
ing revelations of yore-time building by Jerry Runemup.
St. George’s Hall has a history. Rickards made his first
Melbourne appearance there. His company was led by
Lizzie Watson, afterwards partner with Harry Braham.
Lottie, the gymnaste, will be remembered as a great furore
at St. George’s. Her pupil, Katie, afterwards became Mrs.
Rickards. When the Theatre Royal was burnt down,
early in ’72, Coppin and his company performed at St.
George’s Hall, introducing such plays as Robertson’s
“School” and “Society,” with Lena Carey, Hattie
Shepparde, Appleton, Ireland, and Greville. Coppin
gave a sort of imitation of Governor Canterbury, in
“Society,” as Lord Ptarmigant. E. C. Moore was
manager for Lottie, Frank, and Victor. Said the ram-
pant Moore one morning, “ Eighty deadheads of the
Press came into my show last night, and all the outcome
of their united efforts was three lines ! ”
“ Melb ” : Miss Daisy Belmore, the spacious and genial,
reappears in “The Girl Behind the Counter.” She will
be remembered in the last Wilson Barrett company, with
which she acted her original part of Dacia, in “ The Sign
of the Cross.” Indeed, Barrett named that lady after her.
Miss Belmore’s brother was stage manager with Edward
Terry in Australia, and will be remembered in “Sweet La-
vender.” Her father, G. Belmore, was one of the best Lon-
don comedians. He made a great hit as Steve Hargreaves,
the Softy, in “ Aurora Floyd.” When Irving first ap-
peared in “ The Bells,” the programme opened with the
farce of “My Turn Next,” in which Belmore was Taraxi-
cum Twitters. He was Sam Weller to Irving’s Jingle, in
“Pickwick.” Then he became the original Cromwell to
Irving’s Charles I. Against his own intelligent views,
Belmore had to accentuate-Cromwell into the villain.
George Rignold made Cromwell a virtuous character in
Richards’ play.
F. R. Benson, the English actor-manager who plays
hardly anything else but Shakspeare, lately revived the
seldom-seen and dubiously Shakspearian Part 11. of
“ Henry the Sixth.” He took for himself the character
of Cardinal Beaufort, who dies early. So the audience
thought it had seen the last of him ; but when he took a
call at the end, after the fine, large, realistic riot scene,
he appeared in a ragged shirt—and only then was it
realised that the enthusiastic star had been grafting hard,
and all unknown, as one of Jack Cade’s mob ! Are you
listening, J.K., andT.K., and H. 8.?
“Harrison O.” : Some recently-collected figures regard-
ing the salaries earned by (or at any rate paid to) mummers,
warblers and music-hall persons show the enormous
advance which has taken place with the advancing years
in the lucre appropriated by these fortunate individuals.
It is reported that Nell Gwynne was paid £1 per night
for breaking the collective heart of the ancient English
beaux of her day. Mrs. Siddons’ beauty and wit, com-•
bined with her histrionic ability, were valued at £5O per
night; but the explosive Sarah Bernhardt has been paid
as much as £2OO per night—this, perhaps it is unneces-
sary to mention, in the land where millions are made out
of the doubtful sausage and various potted horrors.
The nightly wage earned by he-mummers has also become
more swollen of late, in spite of keener competition and
the absence of a theatrical Wages Board. Burbage re-
ceived £3 nightly, D. Garrick £lOO, Macready £5O. and
Kemble £6O. Coming nearer to our own day, it
appears that Henry Irving generally annexed about
£l2O a night, while Coquelin’s screw' was more like £l4O.
In the last two instances the wages paid may be taken as
indicative of the histrionic worth of the respective
earners. Manipulators of musical instruments are
treated much better than of yore. Mozart, we are told,
considered himself a bloated capitalist when he succeeded
in snaring £5 for a night’s work ; nowadays the swarthy
Kubelik often gets £lBO, and the hairy Paderewski £525.
It is rumored (probably: unyeraciously) that a famous
trombone player once received £6OO for one selec-
tion —he was originally offered £3OO for four, but
one of his victims considered that this was rank
sweating, so he raised the pay and reduced the
time of work. Undoubtedly the most profitable place
at present for the person of small mentality is the
music-hall stage. Dan Leno (who was a comparatively
clever clown) received £3OO per week; at present the
facially-amusing Scawtch drunk-humorist, Harry Lauder,
is receiving just on £lOOO weekly in Uncle Ham’s country.
Little Tichhas, up to now, been compelled to exist as best
he may upon a miserable weekly dole of £5OO. Marie
Lloyd, the embonpoint intoner of risky ditties, is paid
£240 per week, and Kitty Loftus a bit more. For pos-
turing and writhing in a condition of semi-nudity for the
edification of the Cockney, Maud Allan is handed out
£3OO per week.
“Scene-shifter”: The hero of Trafalgar has frequently
been used for stage purposes. Nelson figures prominently
in the current revival of “The Mariners of England,” a
bellowdrama which W. Anderson has revived at Mel-
bourne King’s on account of the Dreadnought upheaval.
When the news of Nelson’s passing out reached England,
both Drury Lane and Covent Garden waded in to
solemnise the occasion. Old Drury had a scene showing
a bust of the admiral in the centre of the stage, and in
the background a cloth representing a sea-fight. The
principals and chorus were grouped around, and yelled a
more or less patriotic ditty, more or less out of tune.
During the years that-the locust has eaten since then,
the British admiral has frequently been resurrected for
stage purposes. There are about two score plays on the
subject. One of the best—“ Nelson, or the Life of a
Sailor—a national drama,” by Fitzball, librettist of
“ MWritana,” created a big boom at the cockney Adelphi
in 1827. Terry, an ancestor of the illustrious Ellen, was
the admiral, and all the big historical events associated
with Nelson, including the Victory scene, were worked
in. But the real hero of the piece was the famous
T. P. Cooke, who was a sailor, and fought in the
marine scuffle off Cape St. Vincent, before he took to the
boards. He played a fictitious character, the orthodox
gallant tar of the period, one Jack Stykes. Cooke was
the original William in “Black-Eyed Susan,” a piece
associated in Australia with George Rignold. Buckstone,
in after years a big comedian, was the low coined v man
in “ Nelson,” which had a big run. The most notable of
modern pieces on the subject is “ Nelson’s Enchantress,”
produced at the London Avenue in 1897. Forbes-ltobert-
son impersonated the marine hero of the Nile, and Mrs.
Pat Campbell played Lady Hamilton, whose relationship
with Nelson has furnished so much scandal. The piece
was in four acts, and had a few good situations. The
dramatic interest was weak. A tableau of “ The Death
of Nelson at Trafalgar ” at the end fetched the patriotic
public, but the piece did not run long. It was different
with “ The Mariners of England,” built on solid melo-
dramatic lines. It was produced in the same year and is
still going.
“Sr”: “ Theorbo ” (B. l/4/’O9) makes reference to the
institution of Gregorian music in Australian Catholic
churches. The suggested innovation has failed to take
on, not wholly because of the lack of a supply of Gregorian
music, but largely because the proposed chants are not
an attractive substitute for the present classical and
perhaps theatrical masses. Only one church has obeyed
Pius X.’s desire—the large Jesuit place of worship in
Richmond (Vic.). There the lady sopranos, altos, and
the rest got the sack from the choir, and small boys and
heavy men took their places. The Gregorian music pre-
sented by the new choir is successful, and the singers have
made something like a name for themselves. Other R.O.
churches stick to their old devices.
Carrie Moore’s career in her panto, engagement at
London Adelphi was very chequered. When she had put
in three or four weeks as the “principal boy,” she sud-
denly retired for the supposed purpose of going abroad
with her husband. Just as suddenly she came back —
after two or three days’ absence—and resumed work. A
while later, she suddenly dropped herself out of the cast
again on the plea of ill-health, and the panto, saw her no
more.
“The Merry Widow” iscausihg alot of trouble around
the Balkans, it being alniqst a level chance that.aggrieved .
persons from Montenegro or thereabouts will try to
break up the show. At Vienna and Constantinople,' also
in Bosnia and Croatia, it caused actual rioting.
There was a little disagreement between writer and
actor in a Melbourne city bar the otherevening. “ Well,”
said the distinguished mummer, bitterly, “if the worst
comes to the worst, I can always earn a living writing
trashy journalism.” “No,” said the pressman, “the
worst can only come to the worst while your audiences
come to see you act. ”
