ADVANCE. DEPARTMENT.
THIS Department offers most advantageous terms to settlers or intending' settlers on the land, a few of
the important features being:—
(a) Advances made from £5O to £2OOO. ( d ) Loan instalments (interest and principal)
(b) Low AND FIXED rate of interest during are payable half-yearly.
currency of loan. (e) Borrower has the right to repay the loan
(c) Money may be lent for any term up to on any instalment date; interest to that
31 years. date only being charged.
The tenures upon which the Advance Department may lend are(l) Freeholds, (2) Certificated Con-
ditional Purchases (with or without associated Conditional Leases), (3) Uncertificated Conditional
Purchases (with or without associated Conditional Leases), (4) Homestead Selections (before or after
issue of the Homestead Grant), (5) Settlement Leases, (6) Settlement Purchases, (7) Conditional
Purchase Leases.
SAVINGS BANK DEPARTMENT.
THIS Department makes advances up to £25,000 upon city and suburban freeholds and on country lands well
* situated and of good quality at moderate rates of interest.

TRIPLEX PLUNGER PUMPS
These Pumps are strong and substantial. They are
designed to be driven by belt from line shaft or from gas,
gasoline, oil 'or steam engine, or from an electric motor.
The working head is placed on the surface of the ground
and the cylinders in the well below. The frame is made
of two strong castings which carry the bearings of the
working parts and are secured together by four iron tie
rods and cross head guide. The crank shaft is of steel and
the gears machine cut. We make these pumps either with
two or three cylinders and for manual power or belt drive.

A Melbourne “business man” is at pre-
sent doing a certain amount of hard thinking
while his wife wears an old bonnet and a contrite
frame of mind. Recently he found that letters
from his country customers, allegedly containing
postal notes and other moneys, were coming to
hand empty, whereupon he fulminated a tremen-
dous charge of larceny combined with laX super-
vision, and heaved it at the Postal Department,
along with a demand that the swindle cease
forthwith. Before arresting the whole staff, the
Department’s *tec inquired round, and learned
that the man’s wife was in the habit of paying for
household goods with country postal notes. So
he straightly charged her with opening her hus-
band’s letters unbeknownst. When the tears had
ceased, a disillusioned business Uian sadly owhed
up that he had been too hasty, artd that there
were more Unreliable persons than postal officials
who had the handling of letters.
“0.K,” Writes :
Melbourne is not deeply impressed by the Sydney
“ Society of Artists” show at the Guild Hall in SwanstoH-
street. The paintings by Syd Long, Douglas Pry, Julian
and Howard Ashton, Misses Alice Musket, Florence Rod-
way and others make no special appeal to a community
which takes little interest in the work of its best local
artists. In the figure paintings just how on view there
is the same lack of charm for the common eye that is
commonly remarked at a Vie. artists’ exhibition. The
faces are not pretty or strikingly natural, the coloring is
unbeautiful, faults of drawing obtrude themselves
—they don’t have to be looked for. There
are no fascinating merits in the pictures to
distract attention from their demerits. They are
works of art, but the effect seems hardly worth
the art and lahor of producing them. Among die land-
scapes there are some pleasing, clever compositions, and
Lionel Lindsay’s presentment of Sydney Harbor from
North Shore heights takes one’s fancy, the more particu-
larly because there isn’t much harbor in it. When there
is a great deal of it in a modern Australian painting the
influence of Arthur Streeton is apt to control the water-
scape, and one is asked to suppose that the Beautiful
Harbor is full of bright, blue paint. The black-and-white
exhibits of Norman Lindsay form the only remarkable
section of the show. Here there is force in general
and power in detail. Lindsay's repeated medleys of human
forms—well drawn or otherwise—keep the eye and the
mind so interested that the surface effect of monotony is
easily overlooked or underlooked. B sides, the allegorical
pictures are not always overloaded witli figures. “The
Conquering God,” for instance, is attract ivein all respects.
Of the smaller drawings, illustrating “ Idylls of the
King” or “Rabelais,” the best, perhaps, is that of
Lancelot making his first appearance at Arthur’s Court,
where Mrs. Arthur is evidently marking the Julius Knight
for prey.


MICK SIMMONS
Haymarket, Sydney, N.^S.W.

FIND ZAM-BUK INVALUABLE.
I
OUTDOOR workers generally have skin-trouble of one kind or another.
Perhaps the skin gets hard and cracks most painfully; or it may be
that the cold or dirt gets into some trivial injury, inflammation and
disease almost certainly following. Cold-
sores round the mouth and nostrils are
also common, and so are rough and
chapped hands. Zam-Buk Balm is
equally soothing, healing and curative
for all such conditions of the skin, and
is consequently a real boon. This pure
and powerful balm takes the “smart”
out, kills pain, “cools” inflammation,
and allays itching. Continued use of
Zam-Buk Balm ensures perfect healing with new
healthy skin. Every outdoor worker should carry
a pot in his pocket or tool bag.
“No home, and especially those of working-men,
should be without a pot of Zam-Buk,” says Mr. G.
j Ritchie, of O’Sullivan Street, Wood End Pocket,
Ipswich, Q. “My occupation of pipe-fixing necessitates
climbing ladders, and recently I had a nasty fall through
the ladder slipping from beneath, resulting in a wound
two inches long on my leg. It became swollen and was
very painful, but knowing already the value of Zam-
Buk, as soon as I arrived home I bathed my leg and applied the balm,
and the very next morning the pain had all gone, and in a little
while the swelling went down and the wound disappeared. I was soon
at work again, which, for a man of my age, sixty-four, is very gratifying,
and shows how quickly and well Zam-Buk did its work. There is no doubt
Zam-Buk is the working-man’s friend.”
Zam-Buk Balm is the natural and ideal application for
pimples, rashes, eruptions, blotches, spots, sores, wounds,
ulcers, eczema, psoriasis, ringworm, scalp diseases, piles, etc.
Keep a pot handy for cuts, bruises, burns, scalds, sprains,
pains, swellings, chafings, sore feet or hands, etc. Of
all chemists and stores at Is. 6d. and 3s. 6d. per pot
(3s. 6d. size contains nearly four times the Is. 6d.).

Wedding Bells.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
GINGER MICK was spliced to-day,
Spliced to Lady Sue ;
Murphy gave the bride away
Down in Waterloo ;
Beauties came by ’bus and car,
Shouting, with delight:
“ What’s the matter with Ginger na-ar ?
“ He’s—all—right! ”
Scarlet-stockinged prettiness
In a yellow skirt;
Even in her wedding-dress
Lady Sue could flirt !
She’d a sweet engagement ring
(Brummagem and glass),
And around her neck a string
Of bead-pearls and brass.
Murphy stood the wedding spread
At the “ Bull and Bush ” ;
Half-a-dozen pots a-head
Settled half the push ;
Some have suffered a relapse,
Sprawling on the floor,
Shouting lurid songs—perhaps
There’s a fight in store.
Murphy swilling whisky neat,
Revelled in the fun ;
Lady Sue, swung off her feet,
Danced with everyone—
Romped the lancers, turn about,
Spun tile wildest whirls.
Till her flimsy skirt stuck out
Like a ballet-girl’s.
Pickle Town is full of beer,
Trebling every beat,
Scores of Johns are swarming here,
Lest the pushes meet;
•lesson’s Village push is out,
Prowling for a fight. . . .
“Ph wat’s the dashed old row about ?”
“It’s—all—right !”
Beauties romping through the streets,
Careless of their sins.
Pelt the Johns along the beats
With banana skins ;
Some nave gone to “ nark ” the Chows,
Yelling like the rest,
Some have had a cotton blouse
Ripped across the breast.
Ginger Mick was spliced to-day,
Spliced to Lady Sue ;
There’s the deuce and all to pay
Down in Waterloo ;
Other people, other views
Cupid hits as soon ;
What's the matter with Perouse
For the honeymoon?
N.s.w.
W. M. WHITNEY.
In the City Beyond the Range.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
AS the mail “ coach ” vanished in dust
and haze down the long vista of the pine
scrub the ni ither sat down under the big
box tree to read Julia's letter. She was feeling a
little worried about her girl, who, a year before,
had gone to take “ a place ” in “ the City beyond
the Range,” as they around Old Junee styled
Sydney. She had done it for the purpose of help-
ing to pay off the arrears on the stripper harvester
which the old man had bought on time-payment.
And by that simple act Julia had risen to be a
monument in the district, a bright and glorious
example which the other daughters of the district
were told to gaze upon and admire.
Julia had secured a place in what she termed
“ the home of some real toffs ” at Darling Point,
wages 12s. 61. per week, paid fortnightly. The
first month she only managed to send along a
solitary 10s. ; but after that she sent £l,per fort-
night regularly. Being asked how she dressed,
she replied that the tips and extras gave her
plenty of pocket money and dress funds. So the
family put the £1 per fortnight into the harvester
fund and was satisfied.
The first haze of doubt arose when George
P. Wardell, assistant forester, returned from
Sydney and raved over a life-size portrait of Julia
that he had seen in some great exhibition of pic-
tures. The mother was conversant with the
gilded vice of “ Ouida,” Marie Corelli and Miss
Braddon, and in the depths of her knowledge of
the world she kept from the old man the awful
news that Julia had taken to having her picture
“took.”
Then her letters suddenly stopped, though two
envelopes had come to hand within a fortnight,
each containing a money-order for £5. And when
the aged parent sat down under the box tree to
read Julia’s latest communication, she hoped that
it would not only contain money but some par-
ticulars as to how the girl was getting such
affluence.
But the letter contained no money, and was
brief and exceedingly to the point.
Dere mother. —M.v heart is broke, coming home on
thursday. send Corrigan with big wagonete, tonss of
boxes. youre poor girl
JULIA.
Fear, consternation and general dilapidation
fell upon Mrs. Warringdon. It was bad enough
for Julia to be getting big sums of money in the
city, but to come home broken-hearted and drag
them all in the dust was terrible. And the old
woman rocked herself in her agony as she thought
of how they had boasted of “ Juliar’s” wonderful
qualities and her thrift and industry. It was
awful. She felt that she would have given the
world to have told her husband now about the life-
sized portrait in the picture show. But after a
while shb concluded she had done everything for
the best. It wasn’t for her to speak evil of Julia
to her father —he would only have snorted and
said he didn’t believe a word of it.
* * * *
If the news of her impending approach had
broken up the family, it was nothing to the effect
that her appearance in the flesh produced on the
mother and two sons when she drove up along-
side Corrigan.
Corrigan was no prude, but his scandalised
countenance showed plainly what he thought.
He kept his eyes straight in front of him. And
when the gorgeous sunshade suddenly closed up,
and Julia stood revealed, out-dazzling Solomon
wnen he had them all on, the aged mother gave
vie yelp of dismay and horror and fled inside.
Also the two boys turned suddenly a ferocious red
and disappeared round the corner of the house
with something that sounded like oaths.
Corrigan affected not to have seen anything.
But he wondered if, after such a reception. Julia
would stay, and he waited to see if she would give
any instructions.
She let herself down from the box, and, shaking
out her manifold and luxurious draperies, stepped
on to the verandah. There she thought hard for
a moment, and then dived into the recesses of her
handbag and drew out a chain-purse.
“How much, Mr. Corrigan?” she demanded
huskily.
“Mr. Warringdon engiged me, so I s’pose he’ll
settle,” said Corrigan coldly.
“No,” said Julia, “I’ll pay me own bills.
Whoever engiged yo’did it as me agent, so just
say what’s the damage. An’ as you seem to have
bin uncomfortable all the time, p’raps yo’d better
say somethin’ extra for personal what-you-may-
call-it.”
Corrigan blushed. He was sorry he had been
cold. He felt that Julia was being treated too
roughly.
“No harm, Miss Julia; no harm meant. I
ain't much of a talker, an’ as I gets older the
worse I gets. If you wants to settle now a quid’s
the Usual thing. Thank yo’. Shall I put ’em on
the verandah, or where? ”
“Yes, put’em down ’ere. Me father’ll fix ’em
up. And be very careful, Mr. Corrigan ; they’ve
all got fancy things easilj' broke in ’em.”
She sat on the verandah till the last trunk was
unloaded and Corrigan had vanished through the
sliprails into the pine scrub. Then mopping her
eyes with a square of lace she strode aggressively
inside.
Her mother sat in the far corner of the shaded
room. Julia stood in the light at the door, and
the long, interested look that the “ good ” woman
always bestows on the doubtful one, confirmed her
first hurried impression.
It was a handsome girl that stood in the door-
way—a girl with a sad, hunted expression on her
features. Her dark hair was most fashionably
coiled over her forehead, and surmounted by a
wilderness of feathers and the other things that
go to make up a picture hat. Her dust cloak
of chameleon silk protected and half concealed
a frock which seemed to be composed of rectified
and polished spider-web spangled over with drops
of frozen water. Round her neck a chain of golden
links and amethysts gleamed brazenly. Her
shoes were bronze-tinted, and on the right arm,
encased in a long, expensive glove, a heavy
bangle of gold and amethyst scoffed loudly at
virtue and a “place” at 12s. 6d. per week.
“ Well, mother, have you all gone dotty, or
what?” she demanded in a voice that thrilled
with indignation , and was altogether out of har-
mony with her classic appearance and beautiful
raiment.
The mother still stared at her, fascinated, but
made no reply.
.“ Watcher mean by makin’ a holy show of me
like that, an’.before Corrigan, too? What is the
matter with you—tell me that? What ther Devil
do you mean by it? Answer me!” and she
rapped her gorgeous sunshade on the floor imperi-
ously.
“ Juliar, you went away with one carry-all an’ a
macintosh. You come back in clothes better nor
Lady Dudley's an’ enough luggage to fill the
stable. What <fo you mean by cornin’ home to
your mother’s house like that? Why don’t you
come humble like? ”
“ I ain’t of the humble sort, I s’pose. Anyhow,
no matter what yous thought, you’d a right to re-
ceive me decent.”
“Oh, Juliar, Juliar, me girl, I”
“Stow that!” yelled Julia, with a sudden
burst of wrath. “ Stow that, an’ tell me straight
what yous think I am. What are you driving at ?
Of course,” she suddenly continued, anticipating
atly reply, “if I ain’t wanted ’cause I might be a
drag on yous, I kin go Ise where —-I’ve a full purse
an’ won’t want for friends ! ” And she laughed
bitterly.
“Don’t be so brazen, Juliar! What yer want
to bring all them things for. Couldn’t you comp
home like a decent girl?”
“Who says I ain’t decent, anyhow?” she
growled ominously.
“ Well, if you can send home all yer wages but
half a crown a week and get all these things outer
the half-crown, you’re a wonderful girl, Juliar.
Things like them don’t grow on bushes even in
Sydney. ' So if they wasn't come by badly, then
how was they come by? Answer me that, my
girl.”
“ I’m no girl of yours after this day’s treatment.
But since you ask me, though not in the way I
expected, I’ll trouble you to look at me marriage
lines.”
The mother took the document and examined it
carefully, and then began to sniff.
“So you took me for a bad girl, eh ?” snorted
Julia, “just because I happened to have some
pretty clothes. Well, if you hadn’t lived all yer
life in the bush you’d have known that them sort
never gets things like these. All they has, poor
wretches, is Brum. Look at these—the real thing,
everyone of ’em. This bracelet cost as much as
yer separator. This star in me hat is worth more
than yer sewing machine.”
The mother, still sniffing, arose and tried to
fawn on her. Julia side-stepped with agility.
“No, yer don’t, mother ! You didn’t jump to
that idea all in a minute when you saw me in the
waggonette. That’s bin in yer mind a long while,
growin’ and growin’ every day. An’ yous didn’t
seem to care while I sent the money home—the
goodness in you only broke out when I come
meself. Booh ! ”
“ Juliar, me dear, what else could I think ? ”
“ Don’t arst me what you could think.
If I’d a girl I’d stick to her even if she
was real bad as long as she showed she
was fond of me. An’ never a sixpence I
got but I sent home a share of it; an’ you make a
holy show of me before strangers, who’ll spread it
over the district. I’m goin’ to see me father, an’
if he’s like the rest of you, off I go this very night.”
And she swept out of the darkened room and
away to where she saw her father hurrying up
from the stable to see if she had arrived..
* * * *
The air was full of the shrill orchestral music of
the cicadie, and the Riverine evening was melting
rapidly into purple darkness when Julia sat down
on the log seat at the bottom of the little garden
to tell her father the story of her adventures in
the City beyond the Range, the Babylon of many
waters that had broken her heart and given her
comparative wealth. Her father interrupted her
to express his thanks for the assistance she had
given him, an 1 to tell her how handy her remit-
tance had proved.
“Was you ever in love, dad?” inquired the
girl, looking at him wistfully in the fading light,
and ignoring his remarks about the money. The
old man nodded.
“ Well, I’m glad of it, as you’ll know how I
feel. I’ll start at the beginning of it all, an’ you’ll
understand. It’s all because there was no school
round here when we was little uns, dad.”

Outside he called a cab, and I bounced in—l

“‘Your new home, Venus Andrewmenee!
How do you like it?’
“ This scared me a bit, an’ I asked him what he
meant.
“ ‘ I mean I’m going to marry you right off
this very night if you’ll have me, and all that you

“An’ he put his arms round me and kissed me,
an’ me soul went out to him. He was so different

married.
“Out through great windows I caught glimpses

you love me, say nothing !’
“ An’ I didn’t want to talk. All I wanted was
to be with him, to watch his wonderful face, to
hear him' make the piano laugh with joy and
shriek with pain and rage; to watch him making
pictures of me, and feel his kisses. I was dumb.
‘‘And, such days! He gave me money each
day saying:
“‘Go out and buy so and so, Andrewmenee.
Come back at quarter to six an’ we’ll dine ! An’
there were cabs, and theatres, an’ music, and
boats down the harbor. Strange people with long
hair who said but little arid moved about very
softly came to our place at nights and made
wonderful music. And in the day other strange
people with long hair came an’ made pictures—
but never a one would he allow to paint me.
When he found they were trying to sketch me he
would send me to another room. But no matter
who came I was not allowed to speak: ‘Don’t
talk, Andrewmenee; whatever you do, don’t
talk,’ he said.
“One day there came a thin-lipped, plainly-
dressed woman. Shesaidshe had been sent to teach
me grammar. I said I didn’t want ter do any
fancy work as practising my darncing kep’ me
busy all the whiles. She sniffed a bit but went
away. An’ when I tried to thank him he laid his
hands on me shoulders and whispered :
“‘Sh ! Don’t say a word, beautiful one !’.
“ Every morning a girl brought tea to the room,
but one morning she never came. He grew rest-
less an’ touched the bell. Nobody came. He got
outer bed and went to see what had happened. I
waited, wonderin’ if the girls had gone orff through
the night as they sometimes do. An’ I waited
an’ waited, an’ then I got out, put on a wrapper,
an’ went to see where he was. His clothes was
hanging over the chair, his hat was on the peg in
the hall, and nothing was disturbed. He was
wearin’ pale blue silk pyjamas an’ bath slippers.
I went downstairs an- found everything very
quiet. In the kitchen there Was no fire, no sign
of anybody. I went from floor to floor, and found
nobody. I was alone in an empty house.
“ I went back an’ dressed. His clothes was
where lie had left’em. I waited and waited, but
he never came back.
“I neve: 1 saw him again. I went to the police,
an’ they seemed drunk. L waited an’ waited, day
an’ night, with the lights burnin’ on the stair, so
as he shouldn’t hurt himself when he did come.
But he never came.
“ Then I went an’ saw the young ladies where
I used to work, and’ they said papa would go into
it and see what it all meant. But days passed,
an’ they never come near; so I went to a lawyer
bloke an’asked his opinion. After asking me a
lot of questions, he opened his head and said if I’d
give him £2O he’d have me husband by the heels
in harf an hour.
“Then I performed on him, an’ he seemed
kinder dazed when I’d finished. But before I
went he asked me for me address an’ said I’d hear
from him.
“ Next day he came to see me. He told me me
husband was mad, and had married me while
under the influence of some drug. His family was
performing somethin’ awful, and all sorts of things
was goin’ to happen.
“ I said I’d go an’see his family, but another
fellow came before I got dressed an’ said he was
the family solicitor. They wanted me to take
£SOO down and clear out. I wasn't taking any
thing. I wanted me husband, an’ if he was sick
or mad I’d nurse him. That was all I wanted,
an’ he went off with a flea in his ear.
“ Later on, he comes back with me own lawyer
bloke, an’ there was a terrible lot of arguin’ an’
wrangling till my bloke sez, in a tired sorter way :
“ ‘ Look here, I’m goin’ ter see this woman
righted. Either yous comes down handsome an’
proper, or else we goes to court, an’ then there’ll
be a pretty how-dee-doo. We ain’t perticular
which wav we gets it.’
“ 1 was just goin’ to say somethin’, an’ tell ’em
all I wanted was me husband to nurse, but me
lawyer he jumps up an’ sez, just like me husband,
1 Don’t talk. Say nothing ! ’
“ ‘ Why ?’ I sez.
“ ‘ Because you don’t understan’ how to deal
with snobs. Fer two pins I’d go straight
and lay an information, and send some of the
Naicest people where their forefathers kem from.’
“ My word ! You oughter seen the other
lawyer. He jumps up and grabs his hat, and
when he gets to ther door he turns round.
“ ‘Say nothing,’ sez he, ‘an’l have no doubt
that an honorable settlement will be arrived at
before noon.’
“ ‘ We’ll give you till two o’clock,’ sez theother;
‘an’ then if our terms isn’t agreed to, up the
spout goes ther fire.’
“ It was all Dutch to me ; but at the end of it
there was much signin’ of papers, an’ I’ve agreed
to leave ’em alone, never to see him agen, say
nothing, and draw £2O per month. And ther
rooms where I was so happy is mine fer ever,
never to be disturbed.
“And there’s his clothes hangin’on the chair,
his hat in the hall, just as he left ’em. I took his
boots away from the door ’cause people used to
trip over’em, and they was getting knocked about.
“Now, I tell you what I’m goin’ to do. I’m
going to save up me gonce, an’ then when I gets
a lot in hand I’m goin’ to steal me husband from
where they sav he is, an’ get him home an’nurse him
back to what he useter to be. I won’t say a word,
keep him nice an’ quiet, an’ if I kin only have
him there to look at it ’ll be just lovely.”
“I’ve heerd of such things; but I’m sorry as
you should have struck one on ’em, me girl.
Someday, p’raps, he’ll get better an’ come back to
yo’, fer nobody in his proper senses would leave a
woman like you, Juliar, me girl,” said the old man
as they wandered back to the house where a
penitent mother awaited them with tea.
C. A. JEFFRIES.
Ships.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Times repeats Lord Milner’s dictum that naval
co-operation is not a question of shifting- burdens.
but of developing fi esh centres of strength.
—News item.
L to the ships —
V These that shall ride at gaze
To myriad bursting dawns ablaze,
And the sullen West’s eclipse,
Nosing the taint of the Northern breeze
In the stillness of wide nights redly-starred,
Rampantly fronting the racing seas
Their ranging guns shall guard !
Hail to the hammer and beaten plate,
And the hands that thrust from the dockyard gate
This keen-prowed cordon to fend the goal !—-
Skoal!
Wacs hael—the ships !
Square to the straits, athrone
On the rocks where the Channel combers drone
And the mad south-wester whips.
Through the scattering drift that flecks her
mouth,
And the foam that runs on her hauberk-rings,
Our Mother hearkens the strident South
Grow gusty with threatenings.
Children, your weight on her white breast warm
Doth irk, and ye cumber her falchion-arm,
And a Homeland waits for your strength. (Stand
clear !)
Here !
Brine hael—the ships !
Flung from the ocean’s wrist
Like a falcon with jesses of amethyst,
The dawn stares down on the slips
Where our travail shall trample the scorn which
saith
That we, in a wide land sparsely set,
Should chaffer for life with the huckster Death,
Though a little people yet—
A little people of largesse small,
Yet shall each man give as he hath withal—
And dearest of world-lands one land is—
This!
Skoal to the ships —
Built of our blood and breath,
For the love that a clean heart cherisheth,
And the life of our children’s lips !
Bend to the word of a people grown
Strong of wide spaces and salt sweet air,
Plenty, our Mother, have you your own,
Plenty to spend or spare ;
This is our leal word loyalest,
Serving ourselves, that we serve von best !
* * *
To the ships and the seas that the.shfps patrol
(God give you, children !)
Skoal!
VICTORIA.
J. ALEX. ALLAN.
